Classification of Views on Body's Quiddity
Author classifies views on body and gives two syllogisms refuting atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the three views on the nature of body from Islamic philosophy: the theologians' atomism, the Illuminationists' continuous simplicity, and the Peripatetics' matter-form composition.

Core Idea
Three schools, one body: atoms, continuous simplicity, or matter plus form.

Before proving that the indivisible part is impossible, the author maps out the landscape of opinions on what a body is. Understanding these positions is crucial for recognizing the stakes of the argument. Let's see how each school defines the quiddity of body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three schools
Find the names of the three groups mentioned: the theologians (mutakallimūn), the Illuminationists (ishrāqiyyūn), and the Peripatetics (mashshā'ūn).
Each group has a fundamentally different view of body's ultimate composition.
2
Extract each school's position
Read the definition given for each: The theologians say body is composed of indivisible atoms (jawāhir fardah). The Illuminationists say it is a simple (basīṭ) continuous substance. The Peripatetics say it is composed of prime matter (hayūlā) and corporeal form (ṣūra jismiyya).
Prime matter is receptive but not extended; corporeal form gives extension.
3
Compare key features
Note how each view handles divisibility: theologians say atoms are indivisible, Illuminationists say body is continuous (infinitely divisible), Peripatetics also allow divisibility but through form. Also note the author's allegiance.
The author chooses the Peripatetic view and will refute the theologians.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that the author's refutation of atomism is part of establishing the Peripatetic matter-form theory. This classification shows the reader the alternatives before the proofs.
This is typical in Islamic philosophical works: first map the views, then argue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إبطال الجزء الذي لا يتجزى. هذه المسألة وما بعدها من مباحث الهيولى والصورة والتلازم بينهما من الإلهيات؛ لأن هذا البحث عن تحقيق ماهية الجسم ووجوده، والباحث عن ماهية الشيء ووجوده العلم الإلهي. وليست من الطبعيات؛ لأن موضوعها الجسم الطبعي، والبحث في كل علم عن أحوال الموضوع وعوارضه الذاتية لا عن ماهيته ووجوده كما يدل عليه حد الموضوع. فإن قيل: لم أوردها ههنا، والمقصود ههنا البحث عن عوارض الجسم الطبعي؟ قلت ذكرها ههنا على سبيل المبادئ؛ ليكون للشارع في العلم الطبعي بصيرة في تحقيق المآرب؛ لكون كثير من المسائل الطبعية منوطا على ثبوت الهيولى وبطلان الجزء الذي لا يتجزى، فإن لم يذكر في أوائل الطبعيات كان للمتعلم حيرة في درك تلك المسائل. اعلم أن في تحقيق ماهية الجسم اختلافات فالمتكلمون على أنه مؤلف من جواهر فردة لا تقبل القسمة أصلا، والإشراقيون على أنه جوهر بسيط متصل بذاته، والمشاؤون على أنه مؤلف من جوهرين: أحدهما: جوهر قابل للصورة الجسمية ليس في حد ذاته متصلا ولا منفصلا، ويقال له الهيولى، والثاني: جوهر ممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة بذاته ويقال له: الصورة الجسمية والمصنف محليه اختار مذهب الحكماء المشائين، فأبطل رأي المتكلمين أولا، ثم بعد ذلك أثبت تركيبه. ولهم في إبطال الجزء الذي لا يتجزى براهين كثيرة، وأورد المصنف له ههنا برهانين سهلين، تقرير الأول: أن الجسم لو كان مؤلفا من أجزاء لا تتجزى لكان جزء منها وسطا وآخرين طرفين، فنقول: الجزء الوسطاني إما أن يكون مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين أو لا، فإن لم يكن مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين كان الوسط داخلا في أحد الطرفين فلا يكون الوسط وسطا ولا الطرف طرفا، وقد فرضنا الوسط والطرف، هذا خلف، وأيضا التداخل محال؛ لأن اتحاد الجوهرين المتحيزين بذاتهما - بحيث يصيران في الوضع والحجم شيئا واحدا – محال عند العقل بالضرورة، ولو جاز دخول الأجسام بعضها في بعض لكان الماء الذي على الإناء المملوء ماء دخل مع ذلك الماء في الإناء، وما فاض الماء من الإناء على وجه الأرض، والتالي باطل بالشهادة، وإن كان مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين فما به لاقى هذا الطرف غير ما به لاقى الطرف الآخر، فانقسم الجزء الوسط إلى قسمين: أحدهما ملاق لهذا الطرف، والثاني لذلك الطرف، وكذلك الطرفان أيضا بما لاقى وبما لم يلاق. ولأنا لو فرضنا جزءا على ملتقى جزئين، فإما أن لا يلاقي شيئا منهما، أو يلاقي بعض كل واحد منهما، أو يلاقي بعض واحد وكل الآخر، والأول باطل بالبداهة وإلا لم يكن على الملتقى، والثاني والثالث يستلزمان الانقسام، هذا خلف. لو فرضنا جزءا تفصيله هكذا فإما أن يلاقي ذلك الجزء الواقع على الملتقى بتمامه أو ببعضه واحدا من المتلاقين بتمامه أو ببعضه، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو ببعضه، كلا من الجزئين بالتمام أو شيئا، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو بعضه واحدا منهما بالتمام، وبعضا من الآخر، فالاحتمالات عشرة، والأربعة الأول كلها محال بالبداهة؛ لاستلزام كل منهما أن لا يكون المفروض على الملتقى عليه، وأما الستة الآخرة كل واحد منها محال أيضا؛ إذ الأول منها يستلزم انقسام ما على ملتقى، والثاني بانقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء، والثالث انقسام ما على الملتقى وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والرابع انقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والخامس يقتضي انقسام ما على الملتقى وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والسادس انقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين.
Chapter on disproving the indivisible part. This issue and what follows of the investigations of prime matter, form, and the connection between them belong to metaphysics; because this investigation is about realizing the quiddity of body and its existence, and the investigator of the quiddity of a thing and its existence is the divine science. And it is not from the natural sciences; because their subject is the natural body, and investigation in every science concerns the states of the subject and its essential accidents, not its quiddity and existence, as indicated by the definition of the subject. If it is said: Why has he mentioned it here, while the intention here is to investigate the accidents of the natural body? I say: He mentioned it here by way of principles, so that the commentator on the natural science may have insight in achieving the goals; because many natural problems depend on the establishment of prime matter and the falsehood of the indivisible part, and if it were not mentioned at the beginning of the natural sciences, the student would be perplexed in understanding those problems. Know that there are differences in realizing the quiddity of body: the theologians hold that it is composed of indivisible atoms that cannot be divided at all; the Illuminationists hold that it is a simple substance continuous in itself; the Peripatetics hold that it is composed of two substances: one a substance receptive of corporeal form, neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, called prime matter, and the second a substance extended in the three dimensions in itself, called corporeal form. The author chose the school of the Peripatetic philosophers, so he first refuted the opinion of the theologians, then afterwards established its composition. They have many proofs for disproving the indivisible part, and the author gave here two easy proofs. Exposition of the first: That if the body were composed of indivisible parts, then one part would be middle and two others ends. We say: the middle part either prevents the meeting of the two ends or not. If it does not prevent the meeting of the two ends, then the middle would enter into one of the ends, so it would not be middle nor the end an end, but we assumed middle and end — this is a contradiction. Also, interpenetration is impossible because the union of two substances that are extended in themselves, such that they become one in position and volume, is necessarily impossible in reason. And if interpenetration of bodies were possible, then the water on the brim of a vessel full of water would enter into the water in the vessel, and what overflowed from the vessel onto the ground would [also enter] — but the consequent is false by observation. And if it does prevent the meeting of the two ends, then that by which it meets this end is not that by which it meets the other end, so the middle part divides into two divisions: one meeting this end, the second that end, and likewise the two ends also divide by what they meet and what they do not meet. And because if we assume a part on the meeting point of two parts, then either it does not meet either of them, or it meets a part of each, or it meets a part of one and all of the other. The first is obviously false, otherwise it would not be at the meeting point. The second and third entail division — this is a contradiction. If we assume a part in detail: that part placed at the meeting point either meets one of the two meeting parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially both parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially one of them entirely and a part of the other. There are ten possibilities. The first four are all obviously impossible, because each entails that the assumed part is not at the meeting point. As for the remaining six, each is also impossible: the first entails division of what is at the meeting point; the second entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts; the third entails division of what is at the meeting point and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fourth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fifth entails division of what is at the meeting point and one of the two meeting parts into two parts; the sixth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts and one of the two meeting parts into two parts.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of two proofs against the existence of indivisible parts (atoms), framed within a discussion on the nature of body. The author begins by explaining why this topic, though metaphysical, is introduced in natural science: it provides necessary foundations for many physical issues. Then he classifies three views on body: the theologians (mutakallimun) who hold it is composed of indivisible atoms; the Illuminationists who say it is a simple continuous substance; and the Peripatetics who hold it is composed of prime matter (matter) and corporeal form (extension). The author adopts the Peripatetic view. The first proof is a reductio: if body were composed of indivisible parts, imagine a middle part between two ends. If it does not prevent their meeting, that entails interpenetration (which is impossible and empirically false as shown by water overflow). If it does prevent their meeting, then the middle part must have two distinct faces (one meeting each end), thus it is divisible — contradiction. The second proof is more detailed: consider a part placed where two parts meet. It must either meet none, part of each, or part of one and all of the other. All possibilities lead to division of that part or the other parts, contradicting indivisibility. The author then enumerates ten possibilities exhaustively, each leading to contradiction. These proofs demonstrate that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to logical absurdities, thus body must be infinitely divisible. The examples used (water in a vessel) are empirical illustrations from the natural world.

الهيولى

Key Takeaway

First Proof: Reductio against Atomism
Author classifies views on body and gives two syllogisms refuting atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first proof against the indivisible part as a reductio ad absurdum and explain why each branch leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
If atoms exist, then either they interpenetrate or they divide—both impossible.

The first proof against atomism uses a clever thought experiment: imagine a middle atom between two end atoms. Does it prevent them from touching? Both answers lead to absurdities. Let's walk through the logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption to be refuted
Assume body is composed of indivisible parts (atoms). Then consider a line of three atoms: one in the middle, two as ends.
This is the starting point of the reductio.
2
Pose the dilemma
The middle atom either prevents the two ends from meeting (touching) or it does not. We must consider both cases.
This is a classic logical disjunction: either P or not-P.
3
Case 1: Middle does not prevent meeting
If it does not prevent, the ends would meet at the middle. But then the middle would be inside one of the ends (interpenetration), which contradicts the fact that it is in between. Also, interpenetration of two extended substances is impossible in itself and empirically false (as shown by water overflow).
This leads to a contradiction: the middle is no longer middle.
4
Case 2: Middle prevents meeting
If it prevents, then it must contact each end at different points (faces). But if it has two distinct faces, it is divided into two parts, contradicting its indivisibility.
Again a contradiction: the supposedly indivisible atom becomes divisible.
5
Conclude the reductio
Since both cases lead to contradictions, the original assumption (that body is composed of indivisible parts) must be false. Therefore, body is not composed of such atoms.
This is a valid reductio ad absurdum proof.

Source Text

Chapter on disproving the indivisible part. This issue and what follows of the investigations of prime matter, form, and the connection between them belong to metaphysics; because this investigation is about realizing the quiddity of body and its existence, and the investigator of the quiddity of a thing and its existence is the divine science. And it is not from the natural sciences; because their subject is the natural body, and investigation in every science concerns the states of the subject and its essential accidents, not its quiddity and existence, as indicated by the definition of the subject. If it is said: Why has he mentioned it here, while the intention here is to investigate the accidents of the natural body? I say: He mentioned it here by way of principles, so that the commentator on the natural science may have insight in achieving the goals; because many natural problems depend on the establishment of prime matter and the falsehood of the indivisible part, and if it were not mentioned at the beginning of the natural sciences, the student would be perplexed in understanding those problems. Know that there are differences in realizing the quiddity of body: the theologians hold that it is composed of indivisible atoms that cannot be divided at all; the Illuminationists hold that it is a simple substance continuous in itself; the Peripatetics hold that it is composed of two substances: one a substance receptive of corporeal form, neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, called prime matter, and the second a substance extended in the three dimensions in itself, called corporeal form. The author chose the school of the Peripatetic philosophers, so he first refuted the opinion of the theologians, then afterwards established its composition. They have many proofs for disproving the indivisible part, and the author gave here two easy proofs. Exposition of the first: That if the body were composed of indivisible parts, then one part would be middle and two others ends. We say: the middle part either prevents the meeting of the two ends or not. If it does not prevent the meeting of the two ends, then the middle would enter into one of the ends, so it would not be middle nor the end an end, but we assumed middle and end — this is a contradiction. Also, interpenetration is impossible because the union of two substances that are extended in themselves, such that they become one in position and volume, is necessarily impossible in reason. And if interpenetration of bodies were possible, then the water on the brim of a vessel full of water would enter into the water in the vessel, and what overflowed from the vessel onto the ground would [also enter] — but the consequent is false by observation. And if it does prevent the meeting of the two ends, then that by which it meets this end is not that by which it meets the other end, so the middle part divides into two divisions: one meeting this end, the second that end, and likewise the two ends also divide by what they meet and what they do not meet. And because if we assume a part on the meeting point of two parts, then either it does not meet either of them, or it meets a part of each, or it meets a part of one and all of the other. The first is obviously false, otherwise it would not be at the meeting point. The second and third entail division — this is a contradiction. If we assume a part in detail: that part placed at the meeting point either meets one of the two meeting parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially both parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially one of them entirely and a part of the other. There are ten possibilities. The first four are all obviously impossible, because each entails that the assumed part is not at the meeting point. As for the remaining six, each is also impossible: the first entails division of what is at the meeting point; the second entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts; the third entails division of what is at the meeting point and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fourth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fifth entails division of what is at the meeting point and one of the two meeting parts into two parts; the sixth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts and one of the two meeting parts into two parts.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of two proofs against the existence of indivisible parts (atoms), framed within a discussion on the nature of body. The author begins by explaining why this topic, though metaphysical, is introduced in natural science: it provides necessary foundations for many physical issues. Then he classifies three views on body: the theologians (mutakallimun) who hold it is composed of indivisible atoms; the Illuminationists who say it is a simple continuous substance; and the Peripatetics who hold it is composed of prime matter (matter) and corporeal form (extension). The author adopts the Peripatetic view. The first proof is a reductio: if body were composed of indivisible parts, imagine a middle part between two ends. If it does not prevent their meeting, that entails interpenetration (which is impossible and empirically false as shown by water overflow). If it does prevent their meeting, then the middle part must have two distinct faces (one meeting each end), thus it is divisible — contradiction. The second proof is more detailed: consider a part placed where two parts meet. It must either meet none, part of each, or part of one and all of the other. All possibilities lead to division of that part or the other parts, contradicting indivisibility. The author then enumerates ten possibilities exhaustively, each leading to contradiction. These proofs demonstrate that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to logical absurdities, thus body must be infinitely divisible. The examples used (water in a vessel) are empirical illustrations from the natural world.

Second Proof: Exhaustive Case Analysis
Author classifies views on body and gives two syllogisms refuting atomism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the second proof exhaustively enumerates all possible ways a part at a meeting point can contact others, and show that each possibility leads to division.

Core Idea
Ten possibilities for contact at a meeting point, all lead to division of the indivisible part.

The second proof is a more detailed analysis. Imagine a part placed exactly where two other parts meet. How can it relate to those parts? The author lists ten logical possibilities and shows each one forces the part to be divisible. Let's work through this exhaustive case analysis.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Assume a part (al-juz') lies at the meeting point of two other parts. The part is supposed to be indivisible. Now consider how it can meet (yulāqī) the two parts.
This is a refinement of the first proof's dilemma, now with more options.
2
List the first three informal possibilities
The author first gives a simpler version: the part either meets neither, meets a part of each, or meets part of one and all of the other. The first is impossible because then it wouldn't be at the meeting point. The other two entail division of the part (since meeting part of each implies distinct contact areas).
This already refutes the assumption, but the author goes further.
3
Enumerate the ten detailed possibilities
Then he examines all ways the part can meet the two other parts: whether it meets one entirely or partially, and the other entirely or partially, etc. This yields ten combinations. The first four are ruled out because they would mean the part is not at the meeting point. The remaining six all entail division of the part or the meeting parts.
The enumeration is exhaustive: all possible contact relations are covered.
4
Verify each leads to division
For each of the six remaining possibilities, the author briefly states what division results (e.g., division of the part into two, three, or more parts). This contradicts the assumption of indivisibility.
You may not need to memorize all six; just understand the method.
5
Conclude the proof
Since every possible way a part can be at a meeting point leads to division, the notion of an indivisible part is impossible. This is a second, more rigorous refutation.
This proof is stronger because it covers all cases without reliance on empirical examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إبطال الجزء الذي لا يتجزى. هذه المسألة وما بعدها من مباحث الهيولى والصورة والتلازم بينهما من الإلهيات؛ لأن هذا البحث عن تحقيق ماهية الجسم ووجوده، والباحث عن ماهية الشيء ووجوده العلم الإلهي. وليست من الطبعيات؛ لأن موضوعها الجسم الطبعي، والبحث في كل علم عن أحوال الموضوع وعوارضه الذاتية لا عن ماهيته ووجوده كما يدل عليه حد الموضوع. فإن قيل: لم أوردها ههنا، والمقصود ههنا البحث عن عوارض الجسم الطبعي؟ قلت ذكرها ههنا على سبيل المبادئ؛ ليكون للشارع في العلم الطبعي بصيرة في تحقيق المآرب؛ لكون كثير من المسائل الطبعية منوطا على ثبوت الهيولى وبطلان الجزء الذي لا يتجزى، فإن لم يذكر في أوائل الطبعيات كان للمتعلم حيرة في درك تلك المسائل. اعلم أن في تحقيق ماهية الجسم اختلافات فالمتكلمون على أنه مؤلف من جواهر فردة لا تقبل القسمة أصلا، والإشراقيون على أنه جوهر بسيط متصل بذاته، والمشاؤون على أنه مؤلف من جوهرين: أحدهما: جوهر قابل للصورة الجسمية ليس في حد ذاته متصلا ولا منفصلا، ويقال له الهيولى، والثاني: جوهر ممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة بذاته ويقال له: الصورة الجسمية والمصنف محليه اختار مذهب الحكماء المشائين، فأبطل رأي المتكلمين أولا، ثم بعد ذلك أثبت تركيبه. ولهم في إبطال الجزء الذي لا يتجزى براهين كثيرة، وأورد المصنف له ههنا برهانين سهلين، تقرير الأول: أن الجسم لو كان مؤلفا من أجزاء لا تتجزى لكان جزء منها وسطا وآخرين طرفين، فنقول: الجزء الوسطاني إما أن يكون مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين أو لا، فإن لم يكن مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين كان الوسط داخلا في أحد الطرفين فلا يكون الوسط وسطا ولا الطرف طرفا، وقد فرضنا الوسط والطرف، هذا خلف، وأيضا التداخل محال؛ لأن اتحاد الجوهرين المتحيزين بذاتهما - بحيث يصيران في الوضع والحجم شيئا واحدا – محال عند العقل بالضرورة، ولو جاز دخول الأجسام بعضها في بعض لكان الماء الذي على الإناء المملوء ماء دخل مع ذلك الماء في الإناء، وما فاض الماء من الإناء على وجه الأرض، والتالي باطل بالشهادة، وإن كان مانعا من تلاقي الطرفين فما به لاقى هذا الطرف غير ما به لاقى الطرف الآخر، فانقسم الجزء الوسط إلى قسمين: أحدهما ملاق لهذا الطرف، والثاني لذلك الطرف، وكذلك الطرفان أيضا بما لاقى وبما لم يلاق. ولأنا لو فرضنا جزءا على ملتقى جزئين، فإما أن لا يلاقي شيئا منهما، أو يلاقي بعض كل واحد منهما، أو يلاقي بعض واحد وكل الآخر، والأول باطل بالبداهة وإلا لم يكن على الملتقى، والثاني والثالث يستلزمان الانقسام، هذا خلف. لو فرضنا جزءا تفصيله هكذا فإما أن يلاقي ذلك الجزء الواقع على الملتقى بتمامه أو ببعضه واحدا من المتلاقين بتمامه أو ببعضه، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو ببعضه، كلا من الجزئين بالتمام أو شيئا، أو يلاقي بتمامه أو بعضه واحدا منهما بالتمام، وبعضا من الآخر، فالاحتمالات عشرة، والأربعة الأول كلها محال بالبداهة؛ لاستلزام كل منهما أن لا يكون المفروض على الملتقى عليه، وأما الستة الآخرة كل واحد منها محال أيضا؛ إذ الأول منها يستلزم انقسام ما على ملتقى، والثاني بانقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء، والثالث انقسام ما على الملتقى وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والرابع انقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة وانقسام كل من الجزئين المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والخامس يقتضي انقسام ما على الملتقى وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين، والسادس انقسام ما على الملتقى إلى ثلاثة أجزاء وأحد المتلاقيين إلى جزئين.
Chapter on disproving the indivisible part. This issue and what follows of the investigations of prime matter, form, and the connection between them belong to metaphysics; because this investigation is about realizing the quiddity of body and its existence, and the investigator of the quiddity of a thing and its existence is the divine science. And it is not from the natural sciences; because their subject is the natural body, and investigation in every science concerns the states of the subject and its essential accidents, not its quiddity and existence, as indicated by the definition of the subject. If it is said: Why has he mentioned it here, while the intention here is to investigate the accidents of the natural body? I say: He mentioned it here by way of principles, so that the commentator on the natural science may have insight in achieving the goals; because many natural problems depend on the establishment of prime matter and the falsehood of the indivisible part, and if it were not mentioned at the beginning of the natural sciences, the student would be perplexed in understanding those problems. Know that there are differences in realizing the quiddity of body: the theologians hold that it is composed of indivisible atoms that cannot be divided at all; the Illuminationists hold that it is a simple substance continuous in itself; the Peripatetics hold that it is composed of two substances: one a substance receptive of corporeal form, neither continuous nor discontinuous in itself, called prime matter, and the second a substance extended in the three dimensions in itself, called corporeal form. The author chose the school of the Peripatetic philosophers, so he first refuted the opinion of the theologians, then afterwards established its composition. They have many proofs for disproving the indivisible part, and the author gave here two easy proofs. Exposition of the first: That if the body were composed of indivisible parts, then one part would be middle and two others ends. We say: the middle part either prevents the meeting of the two ends or not. If it does not prevent the meeting of the two ends, then the middle would enter into one of the ends, so it would not be middle nor the end an end, but we assumed middle and end — this is a contradiction. Also, interpenetration is impossible because the union of two substances that are extended in themselves, such that they become one in position and volume, is necessarily impossible in reason. And if interpenetration of bodies were possible, then the water on the brim of a vessel full of water would enter into the water in the vessel, and what overflowed from the vessel onto the ground would [also enter] — but the consequent is false by observation. And if it does prevent the meeting of the two ends, then that by which it meets this end is not that by which it meets the other end, so the middle part divides into two divisions: one meeting this end, the second that end, and likewise the two ends also divide by what they meet and what they do not meet. And because if we assume a part on the meeting point of two parts, then either it does not meet either of them, or it meets a part of each, or it meets a part of one and all of the other. The first is obviously false, otherwise it would not be at the meeting point. The second and third entail division — this is a contradiction. If we assume a part in detail: that part placed at the meeting point either meets one of the two meeting parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially both parts entirely or partially, or it meets entirely or partially one of them entirely and a part of the other. There are ten possibilities. The first four are all obviously impossible, because each entails that the assumed part is not at the meeting point. As for the remaining six, each is also impossible: the first entails division of what is at the meeting point; the second entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts; the third entails division of what is at the meeting point and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fourth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three and division of each of the two meeting parts into two parts; the fifth entails division of what is at the meeting point and one of the two meeting parts into two parts; the sixth entails division of what is at the meeting point into three parts and one of the two meeting parts into two parts.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of two proofs against the existence of indivisible parts (atoms), framed within a discussion on the nature of body. The author begins by explaining why this topic, though metaphysical, is introduced in natural science: it provides necessary foundations for many physical issues. Then he classifies three views on body: the theologians (mutakallimun) who hold it is composed of indivisible atoms; the Illuminationists who say it is a simple continuous substance; and the Peripatetics who hold it is composed of prime matter (matter) and corporeal form (extension). The author adopts the Peripatetic view. The first proof is a reductio: if body were composed of indivisible parts, imagine a middle part between two ends. If it does not prevent their meeting, that entails interpenetration (which is impossible and empirically false as shown by water overflow). If it does prevent their meeting, then the middle part must have two distinct faces (one meeting each end), thus it is divisible — contradiction. The second proof is more detailed: consider a part placed where two parts meet. It must either meet none, part of each, or part of one and all of the other. All possibilities lead to division of that part or the other parts, contradicting indivisibility. The author then enumerates ten possibilities exhaustively, each leading to contradiction. These proofs demonstrate that the assumption of indivisible parts leads to logical absurdities, thus body must be infinitely divisible. The examples used (water in a vessel) are empirical illustrations from the natural world.

Body as Composite: Peripatetic vs Ishraqi Views
Author defines body as composite and contrasts schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Peripatetic and Ishraqi definitions of body and identify the two components (hyle and form).

Core Idea
Body is not simple; it is a composite of matter and form.

In this passage, the author establishes that every body is composed of a receptive substrate (hyle) and an inhering principle (corporeal form). He then contrasts this Peripatetic position with the Ishraqi view that the continuous substance is the body itself. Understanding this distinction is crucial for metaphysical discussions in kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Locate the main thesis
The passage begins with 'Every body is composed of two parts...' Identify the two parts: the subject (hyle) and the inhering (form).
2
Understand why the form is not proven separately
The author says the refutation of atoms already proves continuity, which is the corporeal form. So no separate proof is needed.
This shows efficiency in the author's argumentation.
3
Contrast Peripatetic and Ishraqi views
For Peripatetics, body = hyle + form. For Ishraqis, the continuous substance is self-subsistent and identical with body itself.
Note the shift: Ishraqis reject the composition.
4
Analyze the two extensions
The author distinguishes essential extension (remains during rarefaction) from accidental extension (changes). He critiques this distinction as difficult.
This leads to further debate in natural philosophy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الهيولى
كل جسم فهو مركب من جزئين يحل أحدهما في الآخر، ويسمى المحل الهيولى والحال الصورة، وبرهانه
إثبات الهيولى عقد المصنف له لإثبات الهيولى، ولم يعقد لإثبات الصورة الجسمية التي هي الجزء الآخر للجسم؛ لأنه لما أبطل تركب الجسم من الجزء الذي لا يتجزى- ومن البين أنه جوهر ذو وضع قابل لأبعاد الثلاثة - ثبت أنه جوهر متصل في حد ذاته، وما هو إلا الصورة الجسمية، ولهذا لم يتعرض لإثبات هذه على حدة؛ لأن بطلان الجزء في قوة الاتصال فما قيل إن وجودها معلوم بالضرورة، ينادي على غفلة، كيف، ولو كان وجودها معلوما بالضرورة لم يحتج إلى بطلان الجزء. نعم، وجود جوهر ذي أبعاد معلوم بالضرورة، أما أنه متصل أو منفصل فمحتاج إلى البيان،
وبالجملة ما هو مزعوم المشائين من وجود جوهر متصل جزء للجسم فليس بينا. كل جسم إلخ هذا عند المشائين، وأما عند الإشراقيين: فالجوهر المتصل قائم بذاته غير حال في شيء آخر، وهو عين حقيقة الجسم. يحل الحلول في اللغة: فرود آمدن چیزی در چیزی، وفي الاصطلاح الاختصاص الناعت أي يكون لوجود الشيء تعلق بشيء آخر بحيث لا يتصور وجوده بدون ذلك الشيء، ويكون نعتا له وذلك منعوتا به، فإن كان ذلك الشيء ذا وضع يكونان متحدين فيه.
الحال: هو الصورة الجسمية: معناها عندهم جوهر ممتد في الأقطار الثلاثة، وقالوا: في الجسم امتدادان جوهري وعرضي، الأول هو جزء الجسم مقوم للهيولى، والثاني: كم سار فيه وأثبتوا التغاير بينهما أن الأول يبقى عند التخلخل والتكاثف، والثاني يتبدل ويتغير والمتبدل غير الباقي أقول: إن القول بالامتدادين في غاية العسر لا يشهد عليه بداهة العقل بل تأبى الفطرة السليمة حتى الشيخ شهاب الدين ذكر
Chapter on Establishing the Hyle. Every body is composed of two parts: one of them inheres in the other. The subject is called hyle, and the inhering form is called form. Its proof: The author structured this chapter to establish the hyle, and he did not structure one to establish the corporeal form which is the other part of the body, because when he refuted the composition of the body from the indivisible part—and it is evident that it is a substance having position capable of three dimensions—it was established that it is a continuous substance in itself, and that is nothing but the corporeal form. For this reason he did not undertake to establish this separately, because the refutation of the part implies the continuity. So what is said that its existence is known by necessity shows negligence. How? If its existence were known by necessity, there would be no need to refute the part. Indeed, the existence of a substance possessing dimensions is known by necessity, but whether it is continuous or discrete requires demonstration. In summary, what the Peripatetics claim about the existence of a continuous substance as part of the body is not evident. 'Every body is composed...' etc. This is according to the Peripatetics. However, according to the Ishraqis, the continuous substance is self-subsistent, not inhering in another, and it is the essence of the body itself. 'Hall' (inherence) in language means 'the entering of something into something else' (in Persian), and in terminology it is a characterizing attachment, i.e., the existence of a thing has a relation to another thing such that its existence cannot be conceived without that thing, and it is a description of it and that thing is described by it. If that thing has a position, they are united in it. 'Al-Hal' (the inhering) is the corporeal form: its meaning according to them is a substance extended in the three dimensions. They said: in the body there are two extensions: essential and accidental. The first is the part of the body constitutive of the hyle; the second is the quantity running through it. They established their difference in that the first remains during rarefaction and condensation, while the second changes and alters. And what changes is other than what remains. I say: the claim of two extensions is extremely difficult; it is not attested by rational intuition, rather sound innate disposition rejects it, until Shaykh Shihab al-Din mentioned.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory discussion on the composition of body. The author states that every body is composed of two parts: a locus (hyle) and an inhering form (sura). He then explains why he did not need to prove the form separately: the refutation of the atom already established that body is continuous, which is precisely the corporeal form. He critiques those who claim the form's existence is self-evident, pointing out that if it were, the refutation of atoms would be unnecessary. He distinguishes the Peripatetic view from the Ishraqi view: for Peripatetics, body is a composite; for Ishraqis, the continuous substance is self-subsistent and identical with body. He also gives a linguistic and technical definition of 'hall' (inherence). Finally, he discusses the two extensions (essential and accidental) in body, and criticizes this doctrine as difficult and not supported by intuition, referencing Shaykh Shihab al-Din.

جوهر متصل

Understanding Hylemorphism: Matter and Form in the Body
Author establishes that every body is composed of matter and form, and provides proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and define the two constituents of a body (hyle and form) and explain why the refutation of atomism implies the existence of corporeal form.

Core Idea
Hyle is the locus, form is the resident; refuting atomism already proves the form.

In this passage, the author defines the fundamental composition of a body as matter (hyle) and form (corporeal form). He then explains why he does not need a separate proof for the form: because by rejecting the atomistic view that a body is made of indivisible parts, it logically follows that the body is a continuous substance, and that continuous substance is exactly the corporeal form. We will walk through the logical structure of this argument and see how it connects to Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a body
Find the Arabic terms for the locus and the resident. The passage states: 'كل جسم فهو مركب من جزئين يحل أحدهما في الآخر، ويسمى المحل الهيولى والحال الصورة'. So the two parts are hyle (matter) and form (corporeal form). Write them down.
The form here is the corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية), not the logical form (differentia).
2
Understand the proof strategy
The author says he did not compose a separate proof for corporeal form because 'لما أبطل تركب الجسم من الجزء الذي لا يتجزى... ثبت أنه جوهر متصل في حد ذاته، وما هو إلا الصورة الجسمية'. So by invalidating atomism, the continuous nature of body is established, which is precisely the corporeal form.
3
Evaluate the author's critique of self-evidence
The author argues that what is known necessarily is the existence of a substance with dimensions (جوهر ذو أبعاد), but whether it is continuous or discrete is not self-evident; it requires demonstration. He refutes the idea that corporeal form is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الهيولى
كل جسم فهو مركب من جزئين يحل أحدهما في الآخر، ويسمى المحل الهيولى والحال الصورة، وبرهانه
إثبات الهيولى عقد المصنف له لإثبات الهيولى، ولم يعقد لإثبات الصورة الجسمية التي هي الجزء الآخر للجسم؛ لأنه لما أبطل تركب الجسم من الجزء الذي لا يتجزى- ومن البين أنه جوهر ذو وضع قابل لأبعاد الثلاثة - ثبت أنه جوهر متصل في حد ذاته، وما هو إلا الصورة الجسمية، ولهذا لم يتعرض لإثبات هذه على حدة؛ لأن بطلان الجزء في قوة الاتصال فما قيل إن وجودها معلوم بالضرورة، ينادي على غفلة، كيف، ولو كان وجودها معلوما بالضرورة لم يحتج إلى بطلان الجزء. نعم، وجود جوهر ذي أبعاد معلوم بالضرورة، أما أنه متصل أو منفصل فمحتاج إلى البيان،
وبالجملة ما هو مزعوم المشائين من وجود جوهر متصل جزء للجسم فليس بينا.
Section on the proof of hyle. Every body is composed of two parts, one of which resides in the other. The locus is called hyle and the resident is called form. Its proof: The author composed this section for the proof of hyle, and did not compose one for the proof of corporeal form which is the other part of the body, because when he invalidated the composition of body from the indivisible part—and it is clear that it is a substance having position, receptive of the three dimensions—it was established that it is a continuous substance in itself, and that is nothing but the corporeal form. For this reason he did not undertake to prove this separately, because the invalidity of the part is in the force of continuity. So what is said that its existence is known necessarily indicates neglect. How? If its existence were known necessarily, there would be no need to invalidate the part. Yes, the existence of a substance with dimensions is known necessarily, but whether it is continuous or discrete needs demonstration. In sum, what is claimed by the Peripatetics of the existence of a continuous substance as a part of the body is not evident.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental composition of a body according to the Peripatetic philosophers: every body consists of two parts—one called hyle (matter) which serves as the locus, and the other called form which resides in it. The author then explains why he only provides a proof for hyle and not separately for corporeal form. The reasoning is that by refuting the atomistic view (the indivisible part), it already follows that the body is a continuous substance, and that continuous substance is precisely the corporeal form. Thus, the proof of the corporeal form is implicit. The author also refutes the claim that the existence of corporeal form is self-evident, arguing that what is self-evident is the existence of a substance with dimensions, not whether it is continuous or discrete. The passage concludes that the Peripatetic claim of a continuous substance as part of the body is not immediately evident and requires demonstration.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayula)
الصورة (al-surah)
الجزء الذي لا يتجزى (al-juz' alladhi la yatajaza)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was it unnecessary to provide a separate proof for the corporeal form?
A) Because it is self-evident.
B) Because it follows from the refutation of the indivisible part.
C) Because matter and form are identical.
D) Because the Ishraghiyun reject it.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of atomism establishes the body as a continuous substance, which is the corporeal form, so the form does not require a separate proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof of Hyle by Disjunctive Elimination
Author proves hyle through elimination of possibilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism that eliminates magnitude and form as the recipient of separation, thereby establishing hyle.

Core Idea
Hyle is the only possible recipient for separation; it cannot be magnitude or form.

The author argues that since bodies capable of separation must have a persistent recipient, and that recipient cannot be the body's magnitude or form, it must be something else — which he calls hyle. This argument uses a disjunctive syllogism: the recipient is either A, B, or C; it is not A; it is not B; therefore it is C. Let's walk through this logical operation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The body is continuous and capable of separation. Therefore it must have a recipient that persists through separation.
This is the minor premise of the overall argument.
2
List the possible recipients
The author lists three possibilities: the magnitude (miqdar), the form (sura) that entails magnitude, or something else.
This forms the major premise of the disjunctive syllogism (either A or B or other).
3
Eliminate magnitude
When separation occurs, magnitude ceases to exist. But the recipient must persist. Therefore magnitude cannot be the recipient.
This is a modus tollens: if it were the recipient, it would persist; it does not persist; so it is not.
4
Eliminate form
Because magnitude is a necessary consequent of form, when magnitude disappears, form also disappears. Thus form cannot persist through separation either.
Again, the recipient must persist; form does not persist.
5
Derive the conclusion
Since both magnitude and form are eliminated, the only remaining possibility is a third thing, which is hyle.
The conclusion follows by disjunctive syllogism.

Source Text

فصل في إثبات الهيولى
القابلة للانفكاك مثل الماء والنار، يجب أن يكون في نفسه متصلا واحدا، وإلا لزم الجزء الذي لا يتجزى ويلزم من هذا إثبات الهيولى في الأجسام كلها؛ لأن ذلك المتصل قابل للانفصال، فالقابل للانفصال في الحقيقة إما أن يكون هو المقدار أو الصورة المستلزمة للمقدار أو معنى آخر، لا سبيل إلى الأول والثاني وإلا لزم اجتماع الاتصال والانفصال،
أجزاء،
القابلة ههنا بحث وهو أنه لما وصف الأجسام بكونها قابلة للانفكاك فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال بكون الجسم متصلا؛ إذ بداهة العقل حاكمة أن الشيء ما لم يكن متصلا لم يكن قابلا للانفكاك والجواب: أن مراده أن بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك بحسب الحس يجب أن يكون في نفس الأمر متصلا على ما يفهم من قوله: "في نفسه، ومن كلام الشارح أيضا حيث قيد قوله : "كما هو عند الحس"، ولا يخفى أنه يحتاج حينئذ إلى الاستدلال. متصلا الاتصال كون شيء بحيث يوجد لأجزائه بعد فرض وقوعها حدود مشتركة، والمتصل هذا المعنى يطلق على فصل الكم ومن خواصه قبول الانقسام بغير نهاية. وإلا لزم إلخ أي وإن لم يكن متصلا واحدا فلا بد أن يكون مؤلفا من وتلك الأجزاء إن لم تكن قابلة للقسمة أصلا لزم الجزء الذي لا يتجزى، وقد تبين بطلانه سابقا، وإن كانت قابلة للقسمة، فإما في جهة واحدة فلزم الخط الجوهري، وإما في جهتين فلزم السطح الجوهري وبطلانهما بمثل ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا يتصور أن تكون قابلة للقسمة في الجهات الثلاث؛ لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد، وعلى تقدير قبول تلك الأجزاء قسمة في جهات ثلاث تكون أجساما، فلم يكن ذلك الجسم مفردا، بل صار مركبا، وقد كان الكلام في المفرد هذا خلف. لأن إلخ خلاصة الدليل أن الجسم المتصل في الواقع يطرأ عليه الانفصال بمعنى حدوث هو يتبين، أو عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه الاتصال، ولا بد له من قابل وقابله في الواقع إما الجسم التعليمي أو الصورة الجسمية المستلزمة له، وهما باطلان فتعين أن يكون معنى آخر، وهو الهيولى.
والقابل يجب وجوده مع المقبول، فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر، وهو المعني من الهيولى......
وهو المعني وزبدة الكلام في هذا المقام: أن الجسم المفرد متصل وقابل للانفكاك، فالقابل إما أن يكون مقداره القائم به، وإما أن يكون صورته المستلزمة للمقدار، لا جائز أن يكون مقداره قابلا للانفكاك؛ لأنه حين طريان الانفصال ينعدم، والقابل يجب أن يكون باقيا إذا كان المقبول وجوديا أو عدم ملكة والانفصال كذلك؛ لأنه عبارة إما عن حدوث هو يتبين أو زوال الاتصال عما من شأنه أن يكون متصلا، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني أي الصورة المستلزمة للمقدار ؛ ضرورة استلزام عدم اللازم لعدم الملزوم، ويجب أن يكون القابل مع لوازمه باقيا وههنا انتفى اللازم أعني المقدار كما علمت من قبل، فكذلك ينتفي الملزوم أي الصورة فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر سواهما، وهو الهيولى. فإن قيل: لم يثبت من هذا البيان كون الهيولى محلا للصورة، بل بقاء أمر قابل مطلقا قلت بعد ما ثبت أن الاتصال ذاتي للجسم لا بد أن يكون مشتملا على أمر قابل للفصل، وإلا كان الانفصال انعداما للجسم بالمرة، وهو باطل بالضرورة، فذلك الأمر الباقي في الحالين إما أن يكون محلا للصورة المتصلة أو حالا فيها أو لا محلا ولا حالا والأخيران باطلان فتعين الأول، أما الثاني فلأنه لو كان حالا فيها للزم من انعدام الصورة المتصلة حين الانفصال انعدامه؛ ضرورة أن انعدام المحل يستلزم انعدام الحال، وإنما دعانا إلى القول بالأمر القابل انعدام الصورة المتصلة ووجوب بقاء أمر قابل.
وأما الثالث فلأنه حينئذ يكونان متباينين ليس لأحدهما علاقة مع الآخر، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجسم حقيقة أحدية محصلة؛ لأنه لا بد للتوحد الطبعي من الافتقار بين الأجزاء والارتباط، وإذا كانت الأجزاء مستغنية بعضها عن بعض كانت الحقيقة اعتبارية لا محصلة وأنت تعلم أن الجسم ليس كذلك، بل هو حقيقة محصلة لها توحد طبعي لا يتوقف على الاعتبار هكذا قيل وفيه تأمل؛ الجواز أن يكون بين الهيولى والصورة ارتباط وعلاقة سوى علاقة الحلول، وإنما يجب للحقيقة المحصلة
Chapter on proving the hyle. That which is capable of dissociation, like water and fire, must in itself be one continuous thing, otherwise it would entail the indivisible part, and from this follows the proof of hyle in all bodies; because that which is continuous is capable of separation, and the recipient of separation in reality is either the magnitude or the form entailing the magnitude or another meaning. There is no way to the first and second, otherwise there would be a conjunction of continuity and separation. The parts. Here there is an investigation: when bodies are described as being capable of dissociation, there is no need to infer that the body is continuous, since the primacy of the intellect judges that a thing, as long as it is not continuous, cannot be capable of dissociation. The answer: his intention is that some bodies capable of dissociation according to sense must in reality be continuous, as understood from his statement 'in itself' and from the commentator's statement also where he qualified his statement 'as it is according to sense', and it is not hidden that then it needs inference. Continuous: continuity is a thing such that its parts after being assumed to occur have common boundaries, and the continuous in this sense is applied to the category of quantity, and among its properties is the reception of division without end. 'Otherwise it would entail' etc., meaning if it is not one continuous thing, then it must be composed of parts, and those parts if they are not divisible at all, then the indivisible part is necessary, and its falsity has been shown previously; and if they are divisible, then either in one dimension, so the substantial line is necessary, or in two dimensions, so the substantial surface is necessary, and their falsity is like what passed in the negation of the part; and it is not conceivable that they are divisible in three dimensions because the discussion is about the individual body, and on the assumption that those parts accept division in three dimensions, they would be bodies, so that body would not be individual but composed, and the discussion was about the individual, this is a vicious circle. 'Because' etc. The gist of the proof is that the body which is continuous in reality experiences separation in the sense of the occurrence of an interval or the removal of continuity from what is characteristically continuous, and it must have a recipient, and its recipient in reality is either the mathematical body or the corporeal form entailing it, and both are false, so it is determined that it is another meaning, which is hyle. And the recipient must exist with the received, so it is determined that the recipient is another meaning, which is what is meant by hyle. And it is the meaning. The essence of the discourse in this place is that the individual body is continuous and capable of dissociation, so the recipient is either its magnitude subsisting in it, or its form entailing the magnitude. It is not possible that its magnitude be capable of dissociation because when dissociation occurs it ceases to exist, and the recipient must persist if the received is existential or privative, and dissociation is likewise because it is either the occurrence of an interval or the removal of continuity from what is characteristically continuous. And there is no way to the second, i.e. the form entailing the magnitude, due to the necessity of the non-existence of the necessary consequent entailing the non-existence of the antecedent. And the recipient with its concomitants must persist, and here the necessary consequent, i.e. magnitude, has ceased as you know from before, so likewise the antecedent, i.e. the form, ceases. So it is determined that the recipient is another meaning besides them, which is hyle. If it is said: this statement does not prove that hyle is a locus for the form, but only the persistence of a recipient absolutely. I say: after it is established that continuity is essential to the body, it must include something capable of separation, otherwise separation would be the complete annihilation of the body, and that is false necessarily. So that persisting thing in both states is either a locus for the continuous form, or an accident in it, or neither locus nor accident. The last two are false, so the first is determined. As for the second, because if it were an accident in it, then from the annihilation of the continuous form at the time of separation would follow its annihilation, since the annihilation of the locus necessitates the annihilation of the accident. And what led us to affirm the receptive thing is the annihilation of the continuous form and the necessity of the persistence of a receptive thing. As for the third, because then the two would be mutually exclusive, having no relation to each other, so the body would not be a unified realized essence; because for natural unity, there must be mutual need and connection among the parts, and if the parts are independent of each other, the essence would be conceptual, not realized. And you know that the body is not like that, but it is a realized essence having natural unity that does not depend on conceptualization. Thus it was said, and there is reflection in it: it is possible that there is a connection and relation between hyle and form other than the relation of inherence. And only for the realized essence
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration (burhan) for the existence of hyle (matter) as a substrate for bodies undergoing division and separation. The author begins by stating that perceptibly divisible bodies (like water and fire) must in themselves be actually continuous; otherwise, they would be composed of indivisible parts, which has been refuted. From this continuity and capacity for separation, a recipient of separation is needed. This recipient cannot be the body's magnitude or its form (which entails magnitude), because when separation occurs, magnitude ceases to exist, but the recipient must persist. Therefore, the recipient must be something else, i.e., hyle. Then the author addresses an objection: This only proves a persisting recipient, not that hyle is a 'locus' (mahall) for form. The response: Since continuity is essential to body, the persisting recipient must be either a locus for form, an accident in form, or independent. The latter two are impossible: if an accident, it would vanish when form vanishes; if independent, the body would not be a unified reality. Hence hyle must be a locus for form. The passage closes with a note that this argument can be questioned, suggesting alternative relations between hyle and form.

Key Terms
الهيولى
المتصل
المقدار
الصورة
القابل
الانفصال
الانفكاك

Checkpoint
According to the proof in this passage, why is the magnitude (miqdar) eliminated as a candidate for the recipient of separation?
A) Because magnitude is indivisible.
B) Because magnitude ceases to exist when separation occurs, but the recipient must persist.
C) Because magnitude is the same as the form.
D) Because magnitude is an accident of the soul.

Key Takeaway
Hyle is the persistent substrate that underlies bodies and receives form, proved by eliminating magnitude and form as possible recipients of separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using the same disjunctive method, try to prove the existence of a necessary substratum for change (e.g., in the transformation of water to air). List the possible substrates (e.g., the body's moisture, its form, etc.) and eliminate them to show that something like hyle is required.
?
Task 2: Identify another passage in Hidayat al-Hikma or a similar text that uses a disjunctive syllogism. Write out the disjuncts, the eliminated ones, and the conclusion. Verify that the elimination is sound.

Hyle as Locus for Form: A Categorical Argument
Author argues that hyle must be a locus for form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical argument that hyle must be a locus (mahall) for form based on the nature of natural unity.

Core Idea
Hyle is not just a bare substrate; it must be the locus of form to give the body natural unity.

After proving that hyle exists, the author addresses an objection: the previous proof only shows that some recipient persists, not that hyle is a locus for form. He responds by arguing that the persisting recipient must be either a locus for form, an accident in form, or independent. The latter two are impossible, so hyle must be a locus. This is another example of a categorical argument using elimination.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector says the proof only shows a persistent recipient, not that this recipient is a locus (mahall) for the form.
This clarifies what needs to be proven.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The author says that the persisting thing must be one of three: a locus for form, an accident in form, or independent (neither).
This is an exhaustive and exclusive division.
3
Eliminate the second possibility
If it were an accident in form, then when form disappears at separation, the accident would also disappear. But we need something persistent.
So it cannot be an accident.
4
Eliminate the third possibility
If it were independent, then hyle and form would have no relation, and the body would not be a unified, realized essence. But we know bodies have natural unity.
So it cannot be independent.
5
Conclude the first possibility
Therefore, the only remaining option is that the persisting recipient is a locus for form, i.e., hyle is the substrate that receives and sustains the form.
This establishes hyle as the mahall for form.

Source Text

أجزاء،
القابلة ههنا بحث وهو أنه لما وصف الأجسام بكونها قابلة للانفكاك فلا حاجة إلى الاستدلال بكون الجسم متصلا؛ إذ بداهة العقل حاكمة أن الشيء ما لم يكن متصلا لم يكن قابلا للانفكاك والجواب: أن مراده أن بعض الأجسام القابلة للانفكاك بحسب الحس يجب أن يكون في نفس الأمر متصلا على ما يفهم من قوله: "في نفسه، ومن كلام الشارح أيضا حيث قيد قوله : "كما هو عند الحس"، ولا يخفى أنه يحتاج حينئذ إلى الاستدلال. متصلا الاتصال كون شيء بحيث يوجد لأجزائه بعد فرض وقوعها حدود مشتركة، والمتصل هذا المعنى يطلق على فصل الكم ومن خواصه قبول الانقسام بغير نهاية. وإلا لزم إلخ أي وإن لم يكن متصلا واحدا فلا بد أن يكون مؤلفا من وتلك الأجزاء إن لم تكن قابلة للقسمة أصلا لزم الجزء الذي لا يتجزى، وقد تبين بطلانه سابقا، وإن كانت قابلة للقسمة، فإما في جهة واحدة فلزم الخط الجوهري، وإما في جهتين فلزم السطح الجوهري وبطلانهما بمثل ما مر في نفي الجزء، ولا يتصور أن تكون قابلة للقسمة في الجهات الثلاث؛ لأن الكلام في الجسم المفرد، وعلى تقدير قبول تلك الأجزاء قسمة في جهات ثلاث تكون أجساما، فلم يكن ذلك الجسم مفردا، بل صار مركبا، وقد كان الكلام في المفرد هذا خلف. لأن إلخ خلاصة الدليل أن الجسم المتصل في الواقع يطرأ عليه الانفصال بمعنى حدوث هو يتبين، أو عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه الاتصال، ولا بد له من قابل وقابله في الواقع إما الجسم التعليمي أو الصورة الجسمية المستلزمة له، وهما باطلان فتعين أن يكون معنى آخر، وهو الهيولى.
والقابل يجب وجوده مع المقبول، فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر، وهو المعني من الهيولى......
وهو المعني وزبدة الكلام في هذا المقام: أن الجسم المفرد متصل وقابل للانفكاك، فالقابل إما أن يكون مقداره القائم به، وإما أن يكون صورته المستلزمة للمقدار، لا جائز أن يكون مقداره قابلا للانفكاك؛ لأنه حين طريان الانفصال ينعدم، والقابل يجب أن يكون باقيا إذا كان المقبول وجوديا أو عدم ملكة والانفصال كذلك؛ لأنه عبارة إما عن حدوث هو يتبين أو زوال الاتصال عما من شأنه أن يكون متصلا، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني أي الصورة المستلزمة للمقدار ؛ ضرورة استلزام عدم اللازم لعدم الملزوم، ويجب أن يكون القابل مع لوازمه باقيا وههنا انتفى اللازم أعني المقدار كما علمت من قبل، فكذلك ينتفي الملزوم أي الصورة فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر سواهما، وهو الهيولى. فإن قيل: لم يثبت من هذا البيان كون الهيولى محلا للصورة، بل بقاء أمر قابل مطلقا قلت بعد ما ثبت أن الاتصال ذاتي للجسم لا بد أن يكون مشتملا على أمر قابل للفصل، وإلا كان الانفصال انعداما للجسم بالمرة، وهو باطل بالضرورة، فذلك الأمر الباقي في الحالين إما أن يكون محلا للصورة المتصلة أو حالا فيها أو لا محلا ولا حالا والأخيران باطلان فتعين الأول، أما الثاني فلأنه لو كان حالا فيها للزم من انعدام الصورة المتصلة حين الانفصال انعدامه؛ ضرورة أن انعدام المحل يستلزم انعدام الحال، وإنما دعانا إلى القول بالأمر القابل انعدام الصورة المتصلة ووجوب بقاء أمر قابل.
وأما الثالث فلأنه حينئذ يكونان متباينين ليس لأحدهما علاقة مع الآخر، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجسم حقيقة أحدية محصلة؛ لأنه لا بد للتوحد الطبعي من الافتقار بين الأجزاء والارتباط، وإذا كانت الأجزاء مستغنية بعضها عن بعض كانت الحقيقة اعتبارية لا محصلة وأنت تعلم أن الجسم ليس كذلك، بل هو حقيقة محصلة لها توحد طبعي لا يتوقف على الاعتبار هكذا قيل وفيه تأمل؛ الجواز أن يكون بين الهيولى والصورة ارتباط وعلاقة سوى علاقة الحلول، وإنما يجب للحقيقة المحصلة
Chapter on proving the hyle. That which is capable of dissociation, like water and fire, must in itself be one continuous thing, otherwise it would entail the indivisible part, and from this follows the proof of hyle in all bodies; because that which is continuous is capable of separation, and the recipient of separation in reality is either the magnitude or the form entailing the magnitude or another meaning. There is no way to the first and second, otherwise there would be a conjunction of continuity and separation. The parts. Here there is an investigation: when bodies are described as being capable of dissociation, there is no need to infer that the body is continuous, since the primacy of the intellect judges that a thing, as long as it is not continuous, cannot be capable of dissociation. The answer: his intention is that some bodies capable of dissociation according to sense must in reality be continuous, as understood from his statement 'in itself' and from the commentator's statement also where he qualified his statement 'as it is according to sense', and it is not hidden that then it needs inference. Continuous: continuity is a thing such that its parts after being assumed to occur have common boundaries, and the continuous in this sense is applied to the category of quantity, and among its properties is the reception of division without end. 'Otherwise it would entail' etc., meaning if it is not one continuous thing, then it must be composed of parts, and those parts if they are not divisible at all, then the indivisible part is necessary, and its falsity has been shown previously; and if they are divisible, then either in one dimension, so the substantial line is necessary, or in two dimensions, so the substantial surface is necessary, and their falsity is like what passed in the negation of the part; and it is not conceivable that they are divisible in three dimensions because the discussion is about the individual body, and on the assumption that those parts accept division in three dimensions, they would be bodies, so that body would not be individual but composed, and the discussion was about the individual, this is a vicious circle. 'Because' etc. The gist of the proof is that the body which is continuous in reality experiences separation in the sense of the occurrence of an interval or the removal of continuity from what is characteristically continuous, and it must have a recipient, and its recipient in reality is either the mathematical body or the corporeal form entailing it, and both are false, so it is determined that it is another meaning, which is hyle. And the recipient must exist with the received, so it is determined that the recipient is another meaning, which is what is meant by hyle. And it is the meaning. The essence of the discourse in this place is that the individual body is continuous and capable of dissociation, so the recipient is either its magnitude subsisting in it, or its form entailing the magnitude. It is not possible that its magnitude be capable of dissociation because when dissociation occurs it ceases to exist, and the recipient must persist if the received is existential or privative, and dissociation is likewise because it is either the occurrence of an interval or the removal of continuity from what is characteristically continuous. And there is no way to the second, i.e. the form entailing the magnitude, due to the necessity of the non-existence of the necessary consequent entailing the non-existence of the antecedent. And the recipient with its concomitants must persist, and here the necessary consequent, i.e. magnitude, has ceased as you know from before, so likewise the antecedent, i.e. the form, ceases. So it is determined that the recipient is another meaning besides them, which is hyle. If it is said: this statement does not prove that hyle is a locus for the form, but only the persistence of a recipient absolutely. I say: after it is established that continuity is essential to the body, it must include something capable of separation, otherwise separation would be the complete annihilation of the body, and that is false necessarily. So that persisting thing in both states is either a locus for the continuous form, or an accident in it, or neither locus nor accident. The last two are false, so the first is determined. As for the second, because if it were an accident in it, then from the annihilation of the continuous form at the time of separation would follow its annihilation, since the annihilation of the locus necessitates the annihilation of the accident. And what led us to affirm the receptive thing is the annihilation of the continuous form and the necessity of the persistence of a receptive thing. As for the third, because then the two would be mutually exclusive, having no relation to each other, so the body would not be a unified realized essence; because for natural unity, there must be mutual need and connection among the parts, and if the parts are independent of each other, the essence would be conceptual, not realized. And you know that the body is not like that, but it is a realized essence having natural unity that does not depend on conceptualization. Thus it was said, and there is reflection in it: it is possible that there is a connection and relation between hyle and form other than the relation of inherence. And only for the realized essence
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration (burhan) for the existence of hyle (matter) as a substrate for bodies undergoing division and separation. The author begins by stating that perceptibly divisible bodies (like water and fire) must in themselves be actually continuous; otherwise, they would be composed of indivisible parts, which has been refuted. From this continuity and capacity for separation, a recipient of separation is needed. This recipient cannot be the body's magnitude or its form (which entails magnitude), because when separation occurs, magnitude ceases to exist, but the recipient must persist. Therefore, the recipient must be something else, i.e., hyle. Then the author addresses an objection: This only proves a persisting recipient, not that hyle is a 'locus' (mahall) for form. The response: Since continuity is essential to body, the persisting recipient must be either a locus for form, an accident in form, or independent. The latter two are impossible: if an accident, it would vanish when form vanishes; if independent, the body would not be a unified reality. Hence hyle must be a locus for form. The passage closes with a note that this argument can be questioned, suggesting alternative relations between hyle and form.

الصورة
المحل
الحال
التوحد الطبيعي
الافتقار
الارتباط

Why does the author eliminate the possibility that the persisting thing is an accident (hal) in the form?
A) Because an accident cannot exist without a substance.
B) Because if it were an accident, it would cease to exist when the form ceases, but we need it to persist.
C) Because an accident is not a physical entity.
D) Because the form is an accident itself.

Key Takeaway
Hyle must be the locus (mahall) of form because the alternatives (accident or independent) would lead to unacceptable consequences for the unity and persistence of the body.

Analysing the Proof for Prime Matter
Argument eliminating quantity and form as substrates, concluding matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical elimination argument for the existence of prime matter (hayūlā) and identify the logical moves that rule out alternative candidates.

Core Idea
Prime matter is the persistent substrate that cannot be quantity or form.

The author of Hidayat al-Hikma presents a rigorous proof for the existence of prime matter (hayūlā) as the underlying principle of change in bodies. Using a series of categorical eliminations, he shows that neither quantity nor form can serve as the persistent receiver of division, leaving a third entity: matter. In this lesson, we will dissect the logical structure of this proof and see how philosophical reasoning in the Islamic tradition employs syllogistic form to establish metaphysical principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the proof
The author aims to prove the existence of hayūlā as a receptive principle (qābīl) that persists when a body undergoes division. The proof assumes division is possible and that something must persist.
2
Extract the two eliminated candidates
The author considers quantity (miqdār) and form (ṣūra) as possible receivers. Quantity is eliminated because it is annihilated at the moment of division. Form is eliminated because its necessary concomitant (quantity) is absent, implying form's absence.
This is a modus tollens argument: if form existed, quantity would exist; quantity does not exist, so form does not exist.
3
Follow the trilemma for the persistent thing
After eliminating quantity and form, the author presents a trilemma: the persistent receiver is either a locus (maḥall) for the form, an accident in the form, or neither. The latter two are impossible: if it were an accident in form, it would vanish with form; if neither, the body would lack natural unity and be merely conceptual.
This trilemma is a disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens) that forces the conclusion that the persistent receiver is a locus.
4
Confirm the conclusion and note the reservation
The conclusion is that the persistent receiver is a locus for the form, i.e., hayūlā. The author adds a reflective note that a relation other than inherence might be possible, but he maintains that natural unity requires a genuine relation, and inherence is the evident case.
The final phrase 'وفيه تأمل' indicates the author is aware of a potential subtlety, but the main proof is considered sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إثبات الهيولى والقابل يجب وجوده مع المقبول، فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر، وهو المعني من الهيولى...... وهو المعني وزبدة الكلام في هذا المقام: أن الجسم المفرد متصل وقابل للانفكاك، فالقابل إما أن يكون مقداره القائم به، وإما أن يكون صورته المستلزمة للمقدار، لا جائز أن يكون مقداره قابلا للانفكاك؛ لأنه حين طريان الانفصال ينعدم، والقابل يجب أن يكون باقيا إذا كان المقبول وجوديا أو عدم ملكة والانفصال كذلك؛ لأنه عبارة إما عن حدوث هو يتبين أو زوال الاتصال عما من شأنه أن يكون متصلا، ولا سبيل إلى الثاني أي الصورة المستلزمة للمقدار ؛ ضرورة استلزام عدم اللازم لعدم الملزوم، ويجب أن يكون القابل مع لوازمه باقيا وههنا انتفى اللازم أعني المقدار كما علمت من قبل، فكذلك ينتفي الملزوم أي الصورة فتعين أن يكون القابل معنى آخر سواهما، وهو الهيولى. فإن قيل: لم يثبت من هذا البيان كون الهيولى محلا للصورة، بل بقاء أمر قابل مطلقا قلت بعد ما ثبت أن الاتصال ذاتي للجسم لا بد أن يكون مشتملا على أمر قابل للفصل، وإلا كان الانفصال انعداما للجسم بالمرة، وهو باطل بالضرورة، فذلك الأمر الباقي في الحالين إما أن يكون محلا للصورة المتصلة أو حالا فيها أو لا محلا ولا حالا والأخيران باطلان فتعين الأول، أما الثاني فلأنه لو كان حالا فيها للزم من انعدام الصورة المتصلة حين الانفصال انعدامه؛ ضرورة أن انعدام المحل يستلزم انعدام الحال، وإنما دعانا إلى القول بالأمر القابل انعدام الصورة المتصلة ووجوب بقاء أمر قابل. وأما الثالث فلأنه حينئذ يكونان متباينين ليس لأحدهما علاقة مع الآخر، فيلزم أن لا يكون الجسم حقيقة أحدية محصلة؛ لأنه لا بد للتوحد الطبعي من الافتقار بين الأجزاء والارتباط، وإذا كانت الأجزاء مستغنية بعضها عن بعض كانت الحقيقة اعتبارية لا محصلة وأنت تعلم أن الجسم ليس كذلك، بل هو حقيقة محصلة لها توحد طبعي لا يتوقف على الاعتبار هكذا قيل وفيه تأمل؛ الجواز أن يكون بين الهيولى والصورة ارتباط وعلاقة سوى علاقة الحلول، وإنما يجب للحقيقة المحصلة
Chapter on Proving the Hylē. The receptive principle must exist with what it receives; so it is determined that the receptive principle is another meaning, which is meant by hylē. And the reliable summary of the discussion in this context is: The single body is continuous and divisible. The receptive principle is either its inherent quantity or its form that necessitates quantity. It is not possible that its quantity is receptive of division, because at the occurrence of division it is annihilated, while the receptive principle must persist when what it receives is existential or a privation. Division is likewise; because it is either the occurrence of a separation or the removal of continuity from what is supposed to be continuous. And the second alternative—the form that necessitates quantity—is also impossible; necessarily because the non-existence of the necessary (quantity) entails the non-existence of that which necessitates it (the form). The receptive principle must persist with its necessary concomitants, but here the necessary (quantity) is absent as you knew before, so the necessitating entity (the form) is also absent. So it is determined that the receptive principle is another meaning besides them, which is hylē. If it is said: This proof does not establish that hylē is a locus for the form, but merely the persistence of a receptive principle absolutely. I say: After it is established that continuity is essential to the body, it must include something receptive of separation; otherwise separation would be the complete annihilation of the body, which is necessarily false. That persistent thing in both states is either a locus for the continuous form, or an accident in it, or neither a locus nor an accident. The last two are false, so the first is determined. As for the second, if it were an accident in the form, then the annihilation of the continuous form at separation would necessitate its annihilation, because the annihilation of the locus necessitates the annihilation of the accident. What led us to posit a receptive principle is the annihilation of the continuous form and the necessity of persistence of a receptive principle. As for the third, then they would be two separate entities with no relation between them, so the body would not be a realized unitary reality; because natural unity requires need and connection between parts. If the parts are independent of each other, the reality would be conceptual, not realized. You know the body is not like that; rather it is a realized reality with natural unity not depending on consideration. Thus it is said, and there is contemplation: It is possible that between hylē and form there is a connection and relation other than the relation of inherence. What is necessary for realized reality is a relation, and the inherence relation is not evident. Perhaps the sound intellect judges that when each of the two is separate and distinct, not one inhering in the other, each is independent of the other, so there is no natural unity preventing separation, and the reality would be conceptual not realized. Understand.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof for the existence of prime matter (hayūlā) as the underlying substrate in a body. The author begins by stating the principle that the receptive principle (qābīl) must exist simultaneously with what it receives (maqbūl). Hence, if a body undergoes division, something must persist to receive the new state. The author then eliminates two candidates—the body's inherent quantity (miqdār) and its form (ṣūra)—as the receiver. Quantity is eliminated because it disappears upon division; form is eliminated because its necessary concomitant (quantity) is absent, implying the form's absence. Therefore, the receiver must be a third thing: matter. An objection is raised: the proof only shows the persistence of some receptive principle, not that it is a locus (maḥall) for form. The author responds with a trilemma: the persistent thing is either a locus for form, an accident in form, or neither. The latter two are false: if it were an accident in form, it would vanish with form; if neither, the body would lack natural unity and be merely conceptual. Hence it must be a locus. The author adds a reflection noting that a relation other than inherence might suffice, but concludes that natural unity requires a genuine relationship.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayūlā): The prime matter underlying a body, the receptive principle that persists through change. [Note: In later Peripatetic tradition, hayūlā is identified with the first matter of all corporeal substances.]
القابل (al-qābīl): The receptive principle that receives the form or accident. [Note: In logic, qābīl can mean subject of predication; here it is metaphysical.]
الانفكاك (al-infikāk): Division or separation of a body into parts. [Note: No significant shift.]
الصورة (al-ṣūra): The form (substantial or accidental) that determines the body's nature and necessitates quantity. [Note: In logic, ṣūra can mean second intention; here it is metaphysical.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the form (ṣūra) eliminated as the receptive principle in the proof for hayūlā?
A) Because the form is a simple substance and cannot receive division.
B) Because the form's necessary concomitant (quantity) is absent when division occurs, implying the form's absence.
C) Because the form is eternal and does not change.
D) Because the form is the same as quantity.

Key Takeaway
Logical elimination of alternatives demonstrates that prime matter (hayūlā) must exist as the persistent substrate underlying corporeal change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
