Shape Accidentality and Divisibility of Corporeal Form
Author proves shape is accidental to corporeal form, leading to divisibility and composition with hyle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism and disjunctive elimination used to prove that corporeal form is divisible and composed of hyle.

Core Idea
Shape is accidental to corporeality because if it were essential, all bodies would have one shape.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous proof that corporeal form (al-sura al-jismiyya) is not simple but composed of hyle (prime matter). He does this by first eliminating possibilities for how shape relates to corporeality, then showing that shape's accidentality implies divisibility, and finally using a categorical syllogism to link divisibility to composition. The objection about wax is answered with a micro-level analysis. We will walk through the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
The author aims to prove that corporeality is capable of division (al-infisal) and composed of hyle (al-hayula). This is the final conclusion of the argument.
2
Follow the disjunctive elimination
Shape can be essential to corporeality, a necessary concomitant, or accidental. The author rejects essentiality because all bodies would then have the same shape, which is false. He rejects necessity for the same reason. Thus shape must be accidental.
This is a disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens).
3
Extract the categorical syllogism
Major premise: Everything capable of division is composed of hyle. Minor premise: Corporeality is capable of division. Conclusion: Therefore, corporeality is composed of hyle.
This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara).
4
Analyse the objection and answer
Objection: Shape can change without division, as in wax. Answer: Even in wax, the change involves micro-level connection and separation of parts, so division does occur. The rebuttal defends the original claim.
The objection tests the link between shape change and divisibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان أنه لا سبيل إلى القسم الأول فلأنها لو كانت متناهية لأحاط بها حد واحد أو حدود، فتكون متشكلة؛ لأن الشكل هو الحاصلة من إحاطة الحد الواحد أو الحدود بالمقدار، فذلك الشكل إما أن يكون للجسمية لذاتها وهو محال، وإلا لكانت الأجسام كلها متشكلة بشكل واحد، أو بسبب لازم للجسمية، وهو أيضا محال لما مر، أو بسبب عارض لها، وهو أيضا محال. وإلا لأمكن زواله، فأمكن أن تتشكل الصورة بشكل آخر، فتكون قابلة للانفصال، وكل ما يقبل الانفصال فهو مركب من الهيولى. فثبت ما قصدناه من كون الجسمية قابلة للانفصال. فإن قيل: قد يتبدل الشكل من غير ورود الانفصال كما في الشمعة فلا يصح قوله: "قابلة للانفصال" قلت: أجيب عنه: أن تبدل أشكال الشمعة أيضا لا يخلو عن اتصال أجزاء كانت متفرقة، وافتراق أجزاء كانت متصلة، فيلزم الفصل.
As for the demonstration that there is no way to the first division, it is because if they were finite, they would be encompassed by one boundary or boundaries, and thus would be shaped; for shape is the result of the enclosure of a magnitude by one boundary or boundaries. That shape is either for corporeality essentially, which is impossible, otherwise all bodies would be shaped with one shape; or due to a necessary concomitant of corporeality, which is also impossible for what has passed; or due to an accident of it, which is also impossible. Otherwise, its removal would be possible, so the form could become shaped with another shape, and thus would be capable of division; and everything capable of division is composed of hyle. So what we intended is established: that corporeality is capable of division. If it is said: Shape may change without division occurring, as in wax, so his statement 'capable of division' is not valid. I say: The answer to it is that the change of shapes in wax also does not lack connection of parts that were separate and separation of parts that were connected, so division is necessitated.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the nature of corporeal form (al-sura al-jismiyya). The author begins by considering possible ways that shape (al-shakl) relates to corporeality. He argues that if bodies were finite, they would have boundaries and thus shape. Then he examines whether shape belongs to corporeality essentially, as a necessary concomitant, or as an accident. He dismisses the first two possibilities: if shape were essential, all bodies would have the same shape (which is false); if it were a necessary concomitant, the same problem arises. Therefore shape must be accidental. The consequence of shape being accidental is that it can cease, allowing the form to take another shape. This change in shape implies that the corporeal form is capable of division (al-infisal), because a change in shape requires a change in magnitude, which occurs through division. Then, using a categorical syllogism, he states that everything capable of division is composed of hyle (prime matter). Thus corporeal form is composite, not simple. The author then raises an objection: shape can change without apparent division, as in the case of wax (e.g., when a wax figure is reshaped without breaking). The answer is that even in wax, the change involves micro-level connections and separations of parts, so division still occurs. This refutes the objection and reinforces the conclusion.

Key Terms
الصورة الجسمية
الشكل
الانفصال
الهيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All bodies would have the same shape.
B) Bodies would be indivisible.
C) Shape could never change.
D) Corporeal form would be composed of hyle.

Key Takeaway
The shape's accidentality implies divisibility, which through a categorical syllogism proves corporeality is composite with hyle.

Proving Hyle Cannot Be Stripped of Form: The Dichotomy Argument
Author proves impossibility of hyle existing without form using position and divisibility arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the dichotomy argument against hyle's independence from form and identify the corrected formulation that avoids infinite regress.

Core Idea
Hyle without form is impossible: both options of position lead to contradictions.

The author sets out to prove that prime matter (hyle) can never exist without substantial form. The argument uses a forced dichotomy: either hyle with position or without. Both are dismantled, leading to an infinite regress if we say form is 'composed of' hyle. The commentator intervenes to refine the wording, showing a key logical subtlety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial dichotomy
The author splits the case: if hyle is stripped of form, it either has position (wad') or not. Both alternatives must be shown impossible.
This is a classic proof by cases (shakk).
2
Explore the first branch (has position)
If it has position, it must be divisible. Everything divisible is composed of hyle and form. So hyle would already have form, contradicting the assumption. Moreover, applying this to form itself leads to infinite regress.
The regress (tasalsul) is an absurdity the author wants to avoid.
3
Explore the second branch (no position)
The author dismisses this quickly, referencing a previous proof that everything having position is divisible. So having no position would contradict the nature of bodies.
This branch is rejected based on a prior established principle.
4
Recognize the corrected formulation
The commentator points out that saying 'everything admitting separation is composed of hyle and form' leads to regress. The correct expression is 'associated with hyle' (muqarin), which avoids the regress while preserving the relationship.
This subtlety is crucial for a coherent ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى لا تتجرد عن الصورة
لأنها لو تجردت عن الصورة، فإما أن تكون ذات وضع أو لا تكون، لا سبيل إلى كل واحد من القسمين، فلا سبيل إلى تجردها عن الصورة، أما أنه لا سبيل إلى الأول فلأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم أو لا، لا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن كل ما له وضع فهو منقسم على ما مر في نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزى. فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة لزم التسلسل؛ لأن الصورة قابلة للانفصال، وكل ما يقبل الانفصال فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة، فالصورة مركبة من الهيولى والصورة، وتلك الصورة أيضا قابلة للانفصال، فتكون تلك أيضا مركبة منهما، وهكذا إلى غير النهاية، وأيضا يلزم تركب الشيء من نفسه ومن غيره، وبطلانه لا يخفى على أحد، فالصواب أن يقول: 'كل ما يقبل الانفصال فهو مقارن للهيولى'.
Section on that hyle is not stripped of form. Because if it were stripped of form, then either it would have position or not. Neither of the two divisions is possible, so there is no way for it to be stripped of form. As for that the first is impossible: because then it would either be divisible or not. The second is impossible because everything that has position is divisible, as has been established in the refutation of the indivisible part. So it would be composed of hyle and form, leading to infinite regress because form is receptive of separation, and everything that admits separation is composed of hyle and form. Thus form would be composed of hyle and form, and that form also admits separation, so it would also be composed of them, and so on ad infinitum. Also it would necessitate the composition of a thing from itself and another, which is clearly false. So the correct statement is: 'Everything that admits separation is associated with hyle'.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that hyle (prime matter) cannot exist without form. The argument proceeds by dichotomy: if hyle were stripped of form, it must either have position (wad') or not. Both alternatives are shown impossible. If it has position, it must be divisible; everything divisible is composed of hyle and form, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because form itself would then be composed of hyle and form. This is absurd. Therefore, the correct formulation is not that everything admitting separation is 'composed of' hyle, but 'associated with' (muqarin) hyle, avoiding the regress. The commentator here expands on the matn, clarifying the logical subtlety.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة
ذات وضع
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) مركب من الهيولى والصورة
B) مقارن للهيولى
C) قائم بالصورة
D) مستقل عن الهيولى

Key Takeaway
Hyle cannot exist without form; the correct relation is 'associated with' not 'composed of' to avoid infinite regress.

Proof that Matter Cannot Be a Line
Author refutes matter being a line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional proof that if matter were a line, it would lead to an impossible overlapping or division.

Core Idea
If matter were a line, it would either prevent surfaces from meeting or not, both impossible.

The author begins his proof that matter is indivisible by examining the possibility that it could become a line if divided in one direction. He constructs a conditional argument: if matter were a line between two surfaces, then either it prevents their meeting or it does not. Each case leads to a contradiction, so matter cannot be a line.

Guided Path
1
Assume matter is a line
Start with the supposition that matter, if divisible, could become a line by division in one direction.
2
Consider two surfaces meeting at this line
If the line exists independently, the endpoints of two surfaces would meet at the line.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
The line either prevents the surfaces from meeting or it does not. Both are examined.
4
Derive contradictions
If it does not prevent, lines overlap (sum equal to one). If it does prevent, the line must divide in width.
A line has no width, so division is impossible.
5
Conclude matter cannot be a line
Since both branches lead to impossibility, the original assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى لا يتجزى، ولا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم في جهة واحدة فتكون خطا، أو في جهتين فتكون سطحا جوهريا، أو في ثلاث جهات فتكون جسما، وكل واحد منها باطل. أما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون خطا فلأن وجود الخط على سبيل الاستقلال محال؛ لأنه إذا انتهى إليه طرفا السطحين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، لا جائز أن لا يحجب، وإلا لزم تداخل الخطوط، وهو محال؛ لأن كل خطين مجموعهما أعظم من الواحد، والتداخل يوجب خلافه، هذا خلف. ولا جائز أن يحجب وإلا لانقسم الخط في جهتين؛ لأن ما يلاقي منه أحدهما غير ما يلاقي الآخر، وهو محال. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون سطحا فلأنها لو كانت سطحا، فإذا انتهى إليه طرفا الجسمين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، وكل واحد منهما باطل، على ما مر في الخط. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون جسما فلأنها لو كانت جسما لكانت مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لما مر. وأما أنه لا سبيل إلى الثاني فلأنها إذا كانت غير ذات وضع فإذا اقترنت بها الصورة الجسمية فصارت حينئذ ذات وضع، فإما أن لا تكون في حيز أصلا أو تكون في جميع الأحياز أو في بعض دون بعض، الأول محال؛ ضرورة حصول كل متحيز في حيز ما، والثاني أيضا محال؛ لامتناع حصول جسم واحد معين في حيزين في زمان واحد فضلا عن جميع الأحياز، والثالث أيضا محال؛ لأن نسبة الهيولى إلى جميع الأحياز سواء، والجسمية إنما تقتضي حيزا ما لا حيزا مخصوصا فلها أيضا النسبة إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية، فحصولها في حيز معين مع استواء النسبة ترجيح بلا مرجح، وهو محال.
Section on that matter (hayula) is indivisible. There is no way to the first [division]; because then it would either divide in one direction and be a line, or in two directions and be a substantial surface, or in three directions and be a body, and each of these is false. As for it not being possible for it to be a line: because the existence of a line independently is impossible; for if the two endpoints of the two surfaces meet at it, then either it prevents their meeting or it does not prevent. It is not possible that it does not prevent, otherwise the overlapping of lines would follow, which is impossible; because any two lines, their sum is greater than one, and overlapping would require the opposite, this is a contradiction. And it is not possible that it prevents, otherwise the line would divide in two directions; because that which contacts one of them is not that which contacts the other, and this is impossible. As for it not being possible for it to be a surface: because if it were a surface, then when the two endpoints of the two bodies meet at it, either it prevents their meeting or does not prevent, and each is false, as was passed in the case of the line. As for it not being possible for it to be a body: because if it were a body, it would be composed of matter and form, as previously. As for there being no way to the second [alternative]: because if it has no position, then when the corporeal form attaches to it, it becomes something with position. Then either it is not in any location, or it is in all locations, or in some rather than others. The first is impossible by necessity, for every located thing must be in some location. The second is also impossible, because it is impossible for one particular body to be in two places at one time, let alone all places. The third is also impossible; because the relation of matter to all locations is equal, and corporeality only requires some location, not a specific location, so it too has an equal relation to all locations. So its occurrence in a specific location despite the equal relation would be a preference without a preferrer, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative proof that matter (الهيولى) is indivisible. The author considers three possibilities if matter were divisible: it would become a line, a surface, or a body. He refutes each. First, if it were a line existing independently between two surfaces, it would either prevent the surfaces from meeting or not. If it does not prevent, the lines would overlap, which is impossible because overlapping would make the sum equal to one, contradicting that the sum of any two lines is greater than one. If it does prevent, the line would have to divide in width (since different parts contact each surface), but a line has no width, so division is impossible. Second, the same argument applies to a surface between two bodies: either it prevents their meeting (dividing in depth) or not (overlap), both impossible. Third, if it were a body, it would be composite of matter and form, which is circular since matter is the subject of form. Then the author addresses an alternative: if matter has no position (لا ذات وضع) but acquires it when form attaches, the question arises where it goes. It cannot be nowhere (impossible for a located thing), nor everywhere (impossible for one body to be in multiple places), nor in some place rather than others (because matter has equal relation to all places, and corporeality requires only any place, not a specific one, so a specific location would require a preferrer). Thus matter is indivisible.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الخط
التداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can matter not be a line according to the proof?
A) Because if matter were a line between two surfaces, it would either prevent them from meeting (which would require the line to have width, impossible) or not prevent (causing overlapping, which makes the sum of two lines equal to one, impossible).
B) Because a line cannot exist without a surface.
C) Because a line is too small.
D) Because matter is not geometric.

Key Takeaway
Matter cannot be a line because any line between two surfaces leads to an impossible consequence: either overlapping or division into width.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a conditional argument to prove that a point cannot be composed of smaller parts. Use the same structure: assume it is, then consider two lines meeting at it, and show both possibilities lead to impossibility.

Proof that Matter Cannot Be a Surface or a Body
Author refutes matter being a surface and a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extend the conditional proof to show that matter cannot be a surface or a body.

Core Idea
The same logical structure refutes surface and body: each leads to impossible consequences.

After refuting the possibility of matter being a line, the author applies similar reasoning to a surface and a body. The argument against a surface mirrors the line argument, while the argument against a body reveals a circular composition. This lesson examines both.

Guided Path
1
Extend the line argument to a surface
If matter were a surface between two bodies, the same two possibilities arise: either it prevents their meeting or it does not. Both are impossible by analogy with the line.
The author says 'as was passed in the case of the line'.
2
Consider matter as a body
If matter were a body, it would be composite of matter and form, but matter is the substrate that receives form, so it cannot itself be composed of matter.
This is a circularity: matter would be presupposing itself.
3
Conclude matter cannot be a surface or a body
Since both alternatives are impossible, matter cannot be divisible into a surface or a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى لا يتجزى، ولا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم في جهة واحدة فتكون خطا، أو في جهتين فتكون سطحا جوهريا، أو في ثلاث جهات فتكون جسما، وكل واحد منها باطل. أما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون خطا فلأن وجود الخط على سبيل الاستقلال محال؛ لأنه إذا انتهى إليه طرفا السطحين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، لا جائز أن لا يحجب، وإلا لزم تداخل الخطوط، وهو محال؛ لأن كل خطين مجموعهما أعظم من الواحد، والتداخل يوجب خلافه، هذا خلف. ولا جائز أن يحجب وإلا لانقسم الخط في جهتين؛ لأن ما يلاقي منه أحدهما غير ما يلاقي الآخر، وهو محال. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون سطحا فلأنها لو كانت سطحا، فإذا انتهى إليه طرفا الجسمين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، وكل واحد منهما باطل، على ما مر في الخط. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون جسما فلأنها لو كانت جسما لكانت مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لما مر. وأما أنه لا سبيل إلى الثاني فلأنها إذا كانت غير ذات وضع فإذا اقترنت بها الصورة الجسمية فصارت حينئذ ذات وضع، فإما أن لا تكون في حيز أصلا أو تكون في جميع الأحياز أو في بعض دون بعض، الأول محال؛ ضرورة حصول كل متحيز في حيز ما، والثاني أيضا محال؛ لامتناع حصول جسم واحد معين في حيزين في زمان واحد فضلا عن جميع الأحياز، والثالث أيضا محال؛ لأن نسبة الهيولى إلى جميع الأحياز سواء، والجسمية إنما تقتضي حيزا ما لا حيزا مخصوصا فلها أيضا النسبة إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية، فحصولها في حيز معين مع استواء النسبة ترجيح بلا مرجح، وهو محال.
Section on that matter (hayula) is indivisible. There is no way to the first [division]; because then it would either divide in one direction and be a line, or in two directions and be a substantial surface, or in three directions and be a body, and each of these is false. As for it not being possible for it to be a line: because the existence of a line independently is impossible; for if the two endpoints of the two surfaces meet at it, then either it prevents their meeting or it does not prevent. It is not possible that it does not prevent, otherwise the overlapping of lines would follow, which is impossible; because any two lines, their sum is greater than one, and overlapping would require the opposite, this is a contradiction. And it is not possible that it prevents, otherwise the line would divide in two directions; because that which contacts one of them is not that which contacts the other, and this is impossible. As for it not being possible for it to be a surface: because if it were a surface, then when the two endpoints of the two bodies meet at it, either it prevents their meeting or does not prevent, and each is false, as was passed in the case of the line. As for it not being possible for it to be a body: because if it were a body, it would be composed of matter and form, as previously. As for there being no way to the second [alternative]: because if it has no position, then when the corporeal form attaches to it, it becomes something with position. Then either it is not in any location, or it is in all locations, or in some rather than others. The first is impossible by necessity, for every located thing must be in some location. The second is also impossible, because it is impossible for one particular body to be in two places at one time, let alone all places. The third is also impossible; because the relation of matter to all locations is equal, and corporeality only requires some location, not a specific location, so it too has an equal relation to all locations. So its occurrence in a specific location despite the equal relation would be a preference without a preferrer, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative proof that matter (الهيولى) is indivisible. The author considers three possibilities if matter were divisible: it would become a line, a surface, or a body. He refutes each. First, if it were a line existing independently between two surfaces, it would either prevent the surfaces from meeting or not. If it does not prevent, the lines would overlap, which is impossible because overlapping would make the sum equal to one, contradicting that the sum of any two lines is greater than one. If it does prevent, the line would have to divide in width (since different parts contact each surface), but a line has no width, so division is impossible. Second, the same argument applies to a surface between two bodies: either it prevents their meeting (dividing in depth) or not (overlap), both impossible. Third, if it were a body, it would be composite of matter and form, which is circular since matter is the subject of form. Then the author addresses an alternative: if matter has no position (لا ذات وضع) but acquires it when form attaches, the question arises where it goes. It cannot be nowhere (impossible for a located thing), nor everywhere (impossible for one body to be in multiple places), nor in some place rather than others (because matter has equal relation to all places, and corporeality requires only any place, not a specific one, so a specific location would require a preferrer). Thus matter is indivisible.

Proof from Position: Matter Cannot Be Divisible
Author refutes alternative that matter becomes located.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the trilemma regarding matter's position after acquiring form, showing it leads to impossibility.

Core Idea
If matter acquires position, it must be somewhere, but it cannot be nowhere, everywhere, or somewhere particular without a preferrer.

The author considers a second alternative: that matter has no position but becomes located when form attaches. He refutes this by examining where it could be. The three possibilities (no location, all locations, or some location) are each shown to be impossible, proving that matter cannot become located and thus cannot be divisible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the alternative hypothesis
The alternative is that matter has no position (la dhat wad') initially, but when corporeal form attaches, it becomes located (dhat wad').
2
Enumerate the three possibilities
Once located, matter must be either in no location, in all locations, or in some specific location. These are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
3
Show each is impossible
First: being in no location is impossible because every located thing must be in some location. Second: being in all locations is impossible because one body cannot be in two places at once, let alone all. Third: being in a specific location is impossible because matter has equal relation to all locations, and corporeality requires only any location, not a specific one, so a specific location would require a preferrer (murajjih), which is absent.
4
Conclude the alternative is false
Since all three possibilities are impossible, the assumption that matter becomes located (and thus divisible in this second sense) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى لا يتجزى، ولا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأنها حينئذ إما أن تنقسم في جهة واحدة فتكون خطا، أو في جهتين فتكون سطحا جوهريا، أو في ثلاث جهات فتكون جسما، وكل واحد منها باطل. أما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون خطا فلأن وجود الخط على سبيل الاستقلال محال؛ لأنه إذا انتهى إليه طرفا السطحين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، لا جائز أن لا يحجب، وإلا لزم تداخل الخطوط، وهو محال؛ لأن كل خطين مجموعهما أعظم من الواحد، والتداخل يوجب خلافه، هذا خلف. ولا جائز أن يحجب وإلا لانقسم الخط في جهتين؛ لأن ما يلاقي منه أحدهما غير ما يلاقي الآخر، وهو محال. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون سطحا فلأنها لو كانت سطحا، فإذا انتهى إليه طرفا الجسمين، فإما أن يحجب تلاقيهما أو لا يحجب، وكل واحد منهما باطل، على ما مر في الخط. وأما أنه لا يجوز أن تكون جسما فلأنها لو كانت جسما لكانت مركبة من الهيولى والصورة؛ لما مر. وأما أنه لا سبيل إلى الثاني فلأنها إذا كانت غير ذات وضع فإذا اقترنت بها الصورة الجسمية فصارت حينئذ ذات وضع، فإما أن لا تكون في حيز أصلا أو تكون في جميع الأحياز أو في بعض دون بعض، الأول محال؛ ضرورة حصول كل متحيز في حيز ما، والثاني أيضا محال؛ لامتناع حصول جسم واحد معين في حيزين في زمان واحد فضلا عن جميع الأحياز، والثالث أيضا محال؛ لأن نسبة الهيولى إلى جميع الأحياز سواء، والجسمية إنما تقتضي حيزا ما لا حيزا مخصوصا فلها أيضا النسبة إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية، فحصولها في حيز معين مع استواء النسبة ترجيح بلا مرجح، وهو محال.
Section on that matter (hayula) is indivisible. There is no way to the first [division]; because then it would either divide in one direction and be a line, or in two directions and be a substantial surface, or in three directions and be a body, and each of these is false. As for it not being possible for it to be a line: because the existence of a line independently is impossible; for if the two endpoints of the two surfaces meet at it, then either it prevents their meeting or it does not prevent. It is not possible that it does not prevent, otherwise the overlapping of lines would follow, which is impossible; because any two lines, their sum is greater than one, and overlapping would require the opposite, this is a contradiction. And it is not possible that it prevents, otherwise the line would divide in two directions; because that which contacts one of them is not that which contacts the other, and this is impossible. As for it not being possible for it to be a surface: because if it were a surface, then when the two endpoints of the two bodies meet at it, either it prevents their meeting or does not prevent, and each is false, as was passed in the case of the line. As for it not being possible for it to be a body: because if it were a body, it would be composed of matter and form, as previously. As for there being no way to the second [alternative]: because if it has no position, then when the corporeal form attaches to it, it becomes something with position. Then either it is not in any location, or it is in all locations, or in some rather than others. The first is impossible by necessity, for every located thing must be in some location. The second is also impossible, because it is impossible for one particular body to be in two places at one time, let alone all places. The third is also impossible; because the relation of matter to all locations is equal, and corporeality only requires some location, not a specific location, so it too has an equal relation to all locations. So its occurrence in a specific location despite the equal relation would be a preference without a preferrer, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a demonstrative proof that matter (الهيولى) is indivisible. The author considers three possibilities if matter were divisible: it would become a line, a surface, or a body. He refutes each. First, if it were a line existing independently between two surfaces, it would either prevent the surfaces from meeting or not. If it does not prevent, the lines would overlap, which is impossible because overlapping would make the sum equal to one, contradicting that the sum of any two lines is greater than one. If it does prevent, the line would have to divide in width (since different parts contact each surface), but a line has no width, so division is impossible. Second, the same argument applies to a surface between two bodies: either it prevents their meeting (dividing in depth) or not (overlap), both impossible. Third, if it were a body, it would be composite of matter and form, which is circular since matter is the subject of form. Then the author addresses an alternative: if matter has no position (لا ذات وضع) but acquires it when form attaches, the question arises where it goes. It cannot be nowhere (impossible for a located thing), nor everywhere (impossible for one body to be in multiple places), nor in some place rather than others (because matter has equal relation to all places, and corporeality requires only any place, not a specific one, so a specific location would require a preferrer). Thus matter is indivisible.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الوضع
الحيز
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can matter not be in a specific location after acquiring form?
A) Because matter's relation to all locations is equal, and a specific location would require a preferrer, which is absent.
B) Because matter is not extended.
C) Because matter is invisible.
D) Because form prevents it.

Key Takeaway
Matter cannot become located because the trilemma of location shows every option is impossible, so the alternative that matter is divisible via position is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar trilemma to prove that a simple point cannot be extended. Assume it has position, then it must be either dimensionless (impossible), in all directions (impossible), or in a specific direction (requires a preferrer).

The Impossibility of Hyle Existing in a Place without a Preferentiator
Full dialectical argument on the impossibility of matter having a specific place without a determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dialectical argument about prime matter's place, identify the principle of tarjih bi-la murajjih, and evaluate the author's responses to objections.

Core Idea
Matter's indifference to place makes a specific location impossible without a determinant.

In this passage from Hidayat al-Hikma, the author tackles a fundamental question: how can prime matter, which is equally capable of being anywhere, actually end up in a specific place? He presents a trilemma, invokes the logical principle of tarjih bi-la murajjih, and then responds to objections. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of the argument and its defenses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trilemma
The author lists three possibilities for the corporeal form (matter with extension): no place, all places, or some places. Recognize these three options.
The first two are dismissed as impossible by intuition.
2
Rule out the first two options
Understand why the author says they are impossible: everything with a position must be in some place (contra no place), and one body cannot be in two places simultaneously (contra all places).
These are intuitive, self-evident truths.
3
Analyze the third option via tarjih bi-la murajjih
The third option (some places) leads to tarjih bi-la murajjih because matter's relation to all places is equal; choosing one without a cause is absurd. This forces the need for a determinant.
The principle of tarjih bi-la murajjih is central to Islamic theology.
4
Examine the first objection and response
The objector says a specific form or state could be the preferrer. The author responds: specific forms only determine a generic place (e.g., air's domain), not a specific part; states have no role in place determination.
The response shows that the objection fails to provide a sufficient determinant.
5
Address the transformation objection
The objector uses water turning into air as a counterexample. The author replies that the prior position of the water (proximity or being in the air's place by compulsion) acts as a preferrer, so no tarjih bi-la murajjih occurs.
This distinguishes bare matter, which has no prior position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الهيولى. الصورة الجسمية، فإما أن لا تحصل في حيز أصلا، أو تحصل في جميع الأحياز، أو تحصل في بعض الأحياز دون بعض، والأول والثاني محالان بالبداهة، والثالث أيضا محال؛ لأن حصولها في كل واحد من الأحياز ممكن. فلو حصلت في بعض الأحياز دون البعض يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح، وهو محال. فإن قيل: لا نسلم الترجيح بلا مرجح؛ لجواز أن يلحقها صورة نوعية أو حالة من أحوال يتعين بها حيز، فلا يكون الترجيح بلا مرجح بل بمرجح، وهو الصورة النوعية أو حالة من الأحوال، قلنا: الصورة النوعية إنما تقتضي مكانا كليا لا معينا كالصورة الهوائية تقتضي مكانا كليا لعنصر الهواء لا مكانا معينا، فحصولها في بعض أجزاء المكان الكلي دون بعض ترجيح بلا مرجح. وأما الحالة فليس لها دخل في اقتضاء الحيز؛ لأن اقتضاء الحيز منسوب إلى الصورة. بقي ههنا شيء، وهو أن تكون الهيولى لعنصر كلي فلا حاجة حينئذ إلى مخصص؛ لأنها تكون حينئذ في مكان كلي دون جزء واحد من أجزاء ذلك المكان الكلي، وهو نظر عويص، اللهم إلا أن يقال: الأجسام منحصرة في العنصريات والفلكيات وهيولى العنصريات مغايرة للفلكيات كما تقرر عندهم فهيولى العناصر إن كانت مجردة عن الصورة، ثم تلحقها الصورة لكان تحت فلك القمر خلاء، والخلاء محال، ولا يمكن أن يكون الفلكيات مالئة لتلك الأحياز قبل حدوث صورة العناصر؛ لامتناع انتقال الأفلاك عن أحيازها على رأي الحكماء، وأما امتناع تجرد هيولى الفلكيات عن الصورة فلأنها لو كانت خالية عن الصورة، ثم تلحقها الصورة، لزم كونها قابلة للكون والفساد وهما لا يجوزان على الأفلاك عندهم، وهذا كله على أصول الفلاسفة، وأما عندنا فلا هيولى ولا صورة، بل جسم متألف من أجزاء، وكله قابل للخرق والالتيام. والأول: أي عدم حصول هيولى ذات وضع في حيز ما محال؛ ضرورة أن كل ذات وضع تكون في حيز ما. والثاني: أي حصولها في جميع الأحياز محال؛ ضرورة أن حصول جسم واحد في حيزين في زمان واحد لا يتصور فضلا عن جميع الأحياز. وواحد من بلا مرجح محال؛ ممكن أي لعدم اختصاصها بحيز معين؛ لأن نسبتها إلى جميع الأحياز سواء لا تقتضي الحيز نظرا إلى ذاتها. وأما الصورة الجسمية وإن كانت تقتضي الحيز لكن مطلقا لا معينا، فلاح إذا أن الهيولى المقترنة بالصورة الجسمية حصولها على التبادل في كل الأحياز ممكن فلو حصلت في بعض الأحياز دون بعض يلزم الترجيح قطعا، وهو محال؛ ضرورة أن ترجيح أحد المتساويين على الآخر إنما يكون بمرجح، وههنا قد تساوت نسبتها إلى الأحياز وحصلت في بعض الأحياز، فصار الترجيح بلا مرجح. ولا يلزم دفع نقض يرد ههنا وتقريره أنا لا نسلم أن الترجيح بلا مرجح لأن الماء إذ انقلب هواء أو بالعكس حصل ذلك المنقلب ببعض أحياز المنقلب إليه مع تساوي نسبته إلى جميع أحياز المنقلب إليه، فأجاب عنه بأن المنقلب وضعا سابقا يقتضي الوضع اللاحق، يعني أن الماء الذي انقلب هواء فله اختصاص ببعض أحياز الهواء بأن يكون قريبا منه إن كان الماء في حيزه، أو حاصلا فيه إن كان موجودا في حيز الهواء بالقسر، ولما كان الاختصاص ببعض الأحياز حاصلا، ولم تكن نسبته إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية، لم يكن الترجيح بلا مرجح. مثلا ماء البحر إذا انقلب هواء، يسكن في حيز الهواء الذي يحاذيه فوقه، ولا ينتقل إلى حيز الهواء الذي يحاذي البر؛ لأن الأول قريب منه والثاني بعيد منه، فصار القرب مرجحا، وكذا الحصول فيه مرجح. وبالجملة ههنا وضع سابق مرجح بخلاف الهيولى المجردة؛ إذ ليست ذات الوضع حتى يتصور لها وضع سابق.
Chapter on that hyle. The corporeal form either does not occupy a place at all, or occupies all places, or occupies some places to the exclusion of others. The first and second are impossible by intuition. The third is also impossible because its occupying any one of the places is possible. So if it occupies some places to the exclusion of others, there would be a preferentiator without a preferrer, which is impossible. If it is said: We do not accept preferentiator without a preferrer; for it is possible that a specific form or a state attaches to it and determines a place for it, so the preferentiator is not without a preferrer but with a preferrer, namely the specific form or a state. We say: The specific form only necessitates a generic place, not a specific one, like the aerial form necessitates a generic place for the element of air, not a specific place; so its occurrence in some parts of the generic place to the exclusion of others is a preferentiator without a preferrer. As for the state, it has no role in determining place; because the determining of place is attributed to the form. There remains a matter: that the hyle of an elemental genus needs no particularizer; because then it would be in a generic place, not in a particular part of that generic place. This is a difficult point. Unless it is said: Bodies are divided into elemental and celestial; the hyle of elementals is different from that of celestials as established among them. So if the hyle of elements were stripped of form and then form attached to it, there would be a void under the sphere of the moon, and void is impossible. And the celestial bodies cannot have filled those places before the occurrence of the elemental forms, because of the impossibility of the spheres moving from their places according to the philosophers. As for the impossibility of the hyle of celestials being stripped of form, because if they were free of form and then form attached to them, they would be subject to generation and corruption, which are not allowed for the celestial bodies according to them. All this is on the principles of the philosophers. But according to us, there is no hyle nor form; rather a body composed of parts, all susceptible to tearing and joining. The first (impossibility): that a hyle having a position does not occupy a place is impossible; necessarily, everything with a position occupies a place. The second: that it occupies all places is impossible; necessarily, one body occupying two places at one time is inconceivable, let alone all places. And one (of the three) without a preferrer is impossible; possible meaning that it has no specification to a particular place; because its relation to all places is equal and does not necessitate a place in itself. As for the corporeal form, although it necessitates a place, it does so absolutely, not specifically. So it is clear that hyle combined with corporeal form’s occurrence in all places by alternation is possible. If it occurs in some places to the exclusion of others, there would be a preferentiator without a preferrer, which is impossible; necessarily, the preferentiator of one of two equals over the other is only with a preferrer. Here the relation of hyle to places is equal, yet it occurs in some places, so there is a preferentiator without a preferrer. And no refutation applies to the objection that arises here: its formulation is that we do not accept preferentiator without a preferrer because water when it turns into air or vice versa, the transformed thing occurs in some places of the thing transformed into, while its relation to all places of the thing transformed into is equal. He answered it by saying: the transformed thing has a prior position that necessitates the subsequent position. This means that water that turns into air has a specification to some places of air by being close to it if the water was in its place, or by being in it if it was in the air's place by compulsion. Since the specification to some places exists, and its relation to all places is not equal, there is no preferentiator without a preferrer. For example, seawater when it turns into air settles in the air space directly above it, and does not move to the air space above the land, because the first is near it and the second is far, so proximity becomes the preferrer. Similarly, being in it is a preferrer. In short, here there is a prior position that acts as a preferrer, unlike bare hyle, for it does not have a position such that a prior position could be conceived for it.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about the nature of prime matter (hayula) and its relation to place (hayyiz). The author begins by stating a trilemma: the corporeal form (i.e., matter considered as extended) must either occupy no place, occupy all places, or occupy some places exclusively. The first two are dismissed as impossible by intuition: everything with a position occupies some place, and one body cannot occupy two places simultaneously, let alone all. The third option is also impossible because if matter could occupy any place equally, its occurrence in some places rather than others would require a preferentiator (murajjih) with no preferrer (murajjah), which is absurd. The author then anticipates an objection: perhaps a specific form (sura naw'iyya) or a state (hala) attaches to matter and determines a particular place, providing a preferrer. The response is that specific forms only require a generic place, not a specific one—for example, the form of air necessitates the element of air's domain in general, not a particular spot within that domain. So the choice among parts of that generic place still lacks a preferrer. As for a state, it plays no role in place determination; that function belongs solely to form. Next, the author raises a subtle issue: if the hyle belongs to a generic element (like water as a kind), it might need no particularizer because its place is already generic. This, he says, is a difficult point, but he suggests that upon the philosophers' principles, the celestial and elemental realms are distinct; the hyle of elements, if formless, would leave a void under the moon, which is impossible; celestial bodies cannot fill that void because they cannot move; and celestial hyle cannot exist without form because that would imply generation and corruption, which are not allowed for the heavens. This remark is specific to the Peripatetic framework. The author then distinguishes his own theological view: according to Islam, there is no hyle or form, but a body composed of atoms, subject to division and reunion. The passage then recapitulates the argument, clarifying that 'possible' here means matter's relation to all places is equal in itself. The corporeal form necessitates place only absolutely, not specifically, so even when combined with that form, matter's relation to places remains indifferent, and any specific location requires a preferrer. Finally, the author addresses another objection: the transformation of water into air seems to show a preferentiator without a preferrer, because the transformed element moves to a specific part of the air's domain. The answer is that the previous position of the water acts as a preferrer—e.g., seawater turning into air occupies the air directly above because of proximity. Thus, a prior position provides the necessary determinant, unlike bare matter, which has no prior position. The commentary should follow this order, explaining each step using examples from natural philosophy (e.g., water, air, spheres) and emphasizing the logical structure of the objections and responses.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة الجسمية
الحيز
ترجيح بلا مرجح
الصورة النوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the specific form not provide a sufficient preferrer for the location of matter?
A) Because specific forms are not real; only corporeal form matters.
B) Because specific forms only require a generic place, not a specific part of that place.
C) Because specific forms attach to matter after location is already determined.
D) Because the trjih bi-la murajjih principle only applies to matter, not to forms.

Key Takeaway
The principle of tarjih bi-la murajjih is central to the argument: matter's indifference to place forces the need for a determinant; without it, a specific location is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the trilemma in the passage: 'Either matter is in no place, all places, or some places.' Explain why the first two are dismissed. Then, using the principle of tarjih bi-la murajjih, explain why the third is also impossible unless a determinant exists.

Proving the Substantial Form via Disjunctive Syllogism
Proof of substantial form via disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and validate the disjunctive syllogism used to prove the existence of substantial form from specific effects in natural bodies.

Core Idea
Specific effects require a specific cause: bodily form cannot explain them, so substantial form must exist.

In this passage, the author argues that natural bodies have a substantial form beyond their common bodily form. The proof is a disjunctive syllogism: either the bodily form or another form accounts for specific effects like natural place. We will walk through each step and see why the bodily form fails and the substantial form is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being proved
Find the conclusion: every natural body has a substantial form (al-sura al-naw'iyya) distinct from the bodily form. This is the target of the proof.
The conclusion is stated at the beginning: 'Know that each of the natural bodies has another form besides the bodily form.'
2
Extract the key observation
Notice that bodies have specific effects (athar mukhtassa), such as requiring a particular natural place (e.g., water seeks downward). These effects are particular to each species.
The passage mentions 'specification of some bodies to some places' as the key specific effect.
3
Set up the disjunction
The cause of these specific effects must be either the bodily form (common to all bodies) or another form specific to that species. This is an exclusive disjunction.
The author explicitly says: 'then either it is due to the common bodily form or to another form.'
4
Eliminate the first possibility
The bodily form cannot be the cause because if it were, all bodies would share those specific effects (since the bodily form is common). But we observe that different bodies have different specific effects, so this is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the bodily form causes specific effects leads to contradiction.
5
Conclude the second possibility
Since the first option is eliminated by the rule of disjunctive syllogism, the second option must be true: another form specific to the species (the substantial form) is the cause.
The author states: 'so the second is determined, which is what is sought.'

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الصورة النوعية. اعلم أن لكل واحد من الأجسام الطبعية صورة أخرى غير الصورة الجسمية؛ لأن اختصاص بعض الأجسام ببعض الأحياز دون البعض ليس لأمر خارج ولا للهيولى، فحينئذ إما أن يكون للجسمية العامة أو لصورة أخرى، لا سبيل إلى الأول وإلا لاشتركت الأجسام كلها في ذلك، فتعين الثاني، وهو المطلوب. هداية: واعلم أن الهيولى ليست الصورة النوعية: هي صورة مختصة بالنوع - لا توجد في نوع آخر، وبها يمتاز نوع الجسم من نوع آخر - داخلة في ماهية محصلة له مبدأ للفصل، وجميع الآثار المختصة بالنوع مستندة إليها. وزبدة ما ذكره المصنف في إثباتها أنه لا شك أن لكل نوع من الأجسام آثارا مختصة كاقتضاء الحيز المعين مثلا، فإما أن تكون تلك الآثار المختصة بالصورة الجسمية أو بصورة أخرى غير الجسمية، لا جائز أن تكون الجسمية، وإلا لزم اشتراك الأجسام كلها في تلك الآثار المختصة؛ لأنها مشتركة بين الأجسام كلها فتكون آثارها أيضا مشتركة، وقد كان الكلام في الآثار المختصة، هذا خلف، فتعين أن تكون تلك الآثار المختصة بصورة أخرى مختصة بذلك النوع، وإلا يلزم الاشتراك وهي المرادة بالصورة النوعية. وإنما لم يلتفت إلى احتمال كون تلك الآثار لهيولى؛ لأنها قابلة محضة فلا تكون فاعلة وأيضا أنها مشتركة، فلا تكون مبدأ للآثار المختصة، وهذا البيان الأخير مخصوص بالعناصر؛ لاشتراك هيولاها، وأما في الأفلاك فلا يجري فيها؛ لعدم اشتراك هيولاها، فالأولى الاكتفاء بالأول. لأن اختصاص: ومعنى الاختصاص أن يقتضي السكون فيه إذا كان فيه، والحركة إليه إذا كان خارجا عنه إن لم يمنع عنها مانع.
Section on the substantial form. Know that each of the natural bodies has another form besides the bodily form; because the specification of some bodies to some places without others is not due to an external thing nor to hyle; then either it is due to the common bodily form or to another form. The first is not possible, otherwise all bodies would share in that, so the second is determined, which is what is sought. Hidaya: Know that hyle is not the substantial form. The substantial form is a form specific to the species - not found in another species, and by it the species of a body is distinguished from another species - entering into its essence, constituting it, a principle of distinction, and all the specific effects of the species are attributed to it. The summary of what the author mentioned in proving it is: There is no doubt that every species of bodies has specific effects, such as requiring a determined place, for example. Then either those specific effects are due to the bodily form or to another form besides the bodily. It is not possible that they are due to the bodily form; otherwise all bodies would share in those specific effects because it (the bodily form) is common to all bodies, so its effects would also be common, but we were speaking about specific effects, which is a contradiction. So it is determined that those specific effects are due to another form specific to that species; otherwise commonality would arise, and that is what is meant by the substantial form. And he did not attend to the possibility that those effects are due to hyle because it is purely passive so it cannot be active, and also it is common, so it cannot be a principle for specific effects. And this latter statement is specific to the elements because their hyle is common; but in the celestial spheres it does not apply because their hyle is not common, so it is better to suffice with the first [argument]. Because specification: and the meaning of specification is that it necessitates rest in it when it is in it, and motion towards it when it is outside it if no obstacle prevents.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and syllogistic proof of the existence of substantial form (al-sura al-naw'iyya). The author first states the claim: each natural body has a form beyond the bodily form. The proof is a disjunctive syllogism: the specification of a body to a particular place must be due either to the bodily form (common to all bodies) or to another form. It cannot be due to the bodily form, because if it were, all bodies would share that specific place (since bodily form is common), but they do not. Therefore, it must be due to another form specific to that body type, which is called the substantial form. The commentary (Hidaya) then clarifies what the substantial form is: a form specific to a species, entering its essence, the principle of differentiation and source of all specific effects. It also addresses why hyle (prime matter) cannot be the cause: hyle is purely passive and common to all bodies (in the sublunary realm), so it cannot produce specific effects. The commentator notes that this last point applies only to elements where hyle is common; for celestial bodies, hyle is not common, so the first argument (based on effects) suffices. The passage defines 'specification' as implying rest when in the place and motion towards it when outside. This is a classic example from Islamic natural philosophy, using the behavior of elements (like water seeking its natural place) as evidence for substantial forms.

الصورة الجسمية
الآثار المختصة

A) Because the bodily form is not real.
B) Because the bodily form is common to all bodies, so its effects would also be common, contradicting the specificity of the effects.
C) Because the bodily form is only found in celestial bodies.
D) Because the bodily form is not a cause but an effect.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive syllogism proves substantial form by eliminating the common bodily form and hyle as causes of specific effects, leaving the species-specific form as the only viable explanation.

Distinguishing Efficient and Receptive Causes
Distinguishing efficient from receptive causality in matter-form relation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between efficient cause (illa fa'iliyya) and receptive cause (illa qabiliyya) in the context of hylomorphic composition and apply the distinction to Islamic philosophical arguments.

Core Idea
A cause can be efficient or receptive; confusing them leads to flawed reasoning about form and matter.

In this passage, the author clarifies why matter (hayula) cannot be an efficient cause for form (sura). He distinguishes between efficient and receptive causality, addresses an objection, and refines the argument. You will learn to identify these two types of causes and apply the distinction in Islamic philosophical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of causes
Locate in the Arabic text the terms علة فاعلية (efficient cause) and علة قابلية (receptive cause). Note that the author explicitly contrasts them.
2
Extract the definition of efficient cause
Find the phrase: 'والعلة الفاعلية للشيء يجب أن تكون موجودة قبله' (the efficient cause of a thing must exist before it). This is the key defining property.
Efficient cause has existential priority over its effect.
3
Understand why matter fails to be efficient cause
The author states that matter does not exist in actuality before form (لأنها لا تكون موجودة بالفعل قبل وجود الصورة). Therefore, it fails the condition for efficient causality.
4
Recognize the objection and response
The objection claims that priority can be essential, not temporal. The response distinguishes: matter is a receptive cause (علة قابلية), and 'the receptive is not an agent' (القابلة لا تكون فاعلة). However, note the nuance that something can be both receptive and agent from different aspects.
The example of the Creator being both receptive and agent is mentioned.
5
Apply the distinction
In hylomorphic analysis, form is the efficient cause of the composite, while matter is the receptive cause. Use this to analyze similar arguments in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الصورة النوعية. علة للصورة؛ لأنها لا تكون موجودة بالفعل قبل وجود الصورة، لما مر، والعلة الفاعلية للشيء يجب أن تكون موجودة قبله، والصورة - إحداهما علة للأخرى، أو كلتاهما معلولتي ثالث ولم يعلم من قبل أن التلازم بينهما على أي وجه هو، تحير ذهن المتعلم واشتبه الأمر عليه، ولإزالة ذلك الوهم والاشتباه أورد هداية وسماها "هداية"؛ لأن الاشتباه نوع من الضلالة، والتفصي عنه دلالة على المطلوب. علة والمراد بالعلة الفاعلية كما يدل عليه قوله : " والعلة الفاعلية للشيء يجب" إلخ. قيل عليه: إن ما مر إنما يدل على امتناع تقدم الهيولى على الصورة بالزمان، وذا لا يدل على عدم كونها علة للصورة؛ لأن تقدم العلة على المعلول ليس بالزمان بل بحسب الذات. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أن الهيولى علة قابلية والقابلة لا تكون فاعلة، وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون الشيء قابلا وفاعلا من جهتين كما قال المصنف في علم البارئ: "إنه ارتسامي والبارئ قابل له وفاعل". والعلة الفاعلية هذا استدلال من الشكل الثاني تحريره الهيولى ليست موجودة قبل الصورة، والعلة الفاعلية للشيء تكون موجودة قبله فالهيولى ليست علة فاعلية للصورة، وفيه أنه لا يلزم من نفي العلة الفاعلية نفي العلة مطلقا؛ لجواز أن تكون علة قابلية أو غيرها، فالأولى حذف قيد الفاعلية والاكتفاء على العلة بأن يقال: " والعلة للشيء يجب أن تكون موجودة قبله فينتج أن الهيولى ليست علة. فإن قيل: المراد نفي العلة الفاعلية لا نفي العلية مطلقا؛ فإن الهيولى علة قابلية للصورة، فكيف يتصور نفي كونها علة قابلية؟ قلت: إن علية الهيولى للأشياء إنما تتصور بعد وجودها، وسيحيء أن الهيولى تحتاج إلى الصورة في وجودها، فأني يتصور كونها علة قابلية للصورة؟ نعم عليتها للصورة الشخصية مسلمة، ولكن الكلام ههنا في الصورة مع قطع النظر عن التشخص؛ لأن الصورة الشخصية ليست متلازمة للهيولى إلا أن يكون المراد بالصورة فردا منتشرا منها، فيمكن التلازم بينه وبين الهيولى.
Section on specific form. It (matter) is a cause for form because it does not exist in actuality before the existence of form, as previously mentioned, and the efficient cause of a thing must exist before it. And form - one of them is a cause for the other, or both are effects of a third, but it was not known beforehand on which basis the mutual dependency between them is, so the mind of the learner becomes perplexed and the matter ambiguous. To remove that illusion and ambiguity, he presented a guidance and called it 'Guidance' because ambiguity is a type of error and extrication from it is a guide to the sought. The intended meaning is the efficient cause, as indicated by his statement: 'The efficient cause of a thing must...' etc. It was objected: That which was mentioned only indicates the impossibility of the priority of matter over form in time, and that does not indicate that matter is not a cause for form because the priority of cause over effect is not temporal but essential. And it can be answered that matter is a receptive cause and the receptive is not an agent, but there is consideration in that because it is possible for a thing to be receptive and agent from two aspects, as the author said in the science of the Creator: 'It is an impression, and the Creator is receptive of it and agent of it.' As for the efficient cause, this is a deduction from the second figure: its formulation: matter does not exist before form, and the efficient cause of a thing exists before it, so matter is not an efficient cause for form. But it may be said that from the negation of efficient causality, the negation of causality absolutely does not follow because it is possible that it be a receptive cause or other. So it is better to omit the qualification of efficiency and suffice with causality by saying: 'The cause of a thing must exist before it,' so it follows that matter is not a cause. If it is said: The intended is the negation of efficient causality, not the negation of causality absolutely, since matter is a receptive cause for form, so how can it be conceived to negate its being a receptive cause? I say: The causality of matter for things is only conceived after its existence, and it will come that matter needs form for its existence, so how can it be conceived that it is a receptive cause for form? Yes, its causality for the individual form is conceded, but the discussion here is about form abstracted from individuation because the individual form is not necessarily conjoined with matter unless the intended by form is a dispersed individual of it, then mutual dependency between it and matter is possible.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative commentary on the relationship between matter (hayula) and form (sura). The author begins by stating that matter is not an efficient cause for form because matter does not exist in actuality before form's existence, and the efficient cause must precede its effect. He notes that this confusion about priority leads to perplexity, so the author of the original text provided a 'guidance' (hidaya). An objection is raised: the priority of cause over effect is not temporal but essential, so the argument does not disprove matter being a cause for form. The answer distinguishes efficient causality from receptive causality: matter is a receptive cause (illa qabiliyya), not an efficient one. However, the author notes that a thing can be both receptive and agent from different aspects, citing an example from the author's other work on the Creator. Then, the author analyzes a categorical syllogism in the second figure: matter does not exist before form; the efficient cause exists before its effect; therefore matter is not an efficient cause of form. He criticizes this reasoning because negating efficient causality does not negate causality altogether; matter could still be a receptive cause. He suggests omitting the qualification 'efficient' to get a valid syllogism: matter is not a cause at all. A further objection contends that the intended negation was only of efficient causality, since matter is a receptive cause. The author replies that matter's causality is conceived only after its own existence, and since matter needs form for its own existence, it cannot be a receptive cause for form. However, he concedes that matter is a cause for the individual form (sura shakhsiyya), but the discussion here concerns form abstracted from individuation. The passage thus clarifies the different types of causality and avoids misunderstanding about the temporal vs. essential priority of causes.

صورة
علة فاعلية
علة قابلية

Checkpoint

A) Because matter does not exist before form in time and the efficient cause must be temporally prior.
B) Because matter is a receptive cause and the receptive is not an agent.
C) Because matter and form are both effects of a third cause.
D) Because matter is an efficient cause only for individual forms.

Key Takeaway
Matter is a receptive, not efficient, cause for form; confusing the two leads to erroneous conclusions.

In the standard Kalam proof, the world is contingent and requires a creator. Explain why matter (hayula) cannot be that creator by applying the distinction between efficient and receptive cause.

Analyzing a Second-Figure Syllogism on Causality
Analyzing a second-figure syllogism on causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a categorical syllogism in the second figure and evaluate whether its conclusion is logically sound, identifying when the scope of the conclusion is narrower than intended.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism may not prove the intended claim if the terms are not precisely aligned with the intended scope.

The author presents a second-figure syllogism to argue that matter is not an efficient cause for form. However, he critiques this reasoning by showing that the conclusion only negates efficient causality, not causality altogether. You will learn to identify the structure of the syllogism, determine its validity, and spot the logical nuance about the scope of the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the syllogism in the Arabic: 'الهيولى ليست موجودة قبل الصورة, والعلة الفاعلية للشيء تكون موجودة قبله فالهيولى ليست علة فاعلية للصورة.' The minor premise is 'matter does not exist before form'; the major premise is 'the efficient cause exists before its effect'; the conclusion is 'matter is not an efficient cause for form.'
2
Determine the figure and mood
The middle term is 'existing before its effect' (موجود قبله). The major term is 'efficient cause', the minor term is 'matter'. The minor premise is negative, the major premise is affirmative. This is the second figure. The mood is AEE (Camestres): all efficient causes are things that exist before effect; no matter is something that exists before form; therefore no matter is an efficient cause.
The syllogism is logically valid.
3
Recognize the critique
The author notes: 'لا يلزم من نفي العلة الفاعلية نفي العلة مطلقا' (from the negation of efficient causality, the negation of causality absolutely does not follow). The syllogism only proves that matter is not an efficient cause, but it does not prove that matter is not a cause in any sense (e.g., receptive cause).
4
Consider the improved syllogism
The author suggests removing the qualification 'efficient' and using 'cause' instead: 'والعلة للشيء يجب أن تكون موجودة قبله فينتج أن الهيولى ليست علة.' This would make the middle term 'cause' and then the conclusion would be that matter is not a cause at all. However, this improved syllogism faces an objection about the nature of receptive causality.
5
Evaluate the overall argument
The author concedes that matter is a cause for individual form but not for universal form. The discussion shows that careful definition of terms is crucial in logical reasoning, especially in theological and philosophical contexts.

Checkpoint
What is the main critique the author makes about the second-figure syllogism?

Key Takeaway
A valid syllogism may only prove a specific claim, not the broader one; always check the alignment between the middle term and the intended conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument: 'Every created thing is temporal. Every accident is created. Therefore every accident is temporal.' Identify the figure and mood, and evaluate whether the conclusion is valid. Then, if someone argued that this proves accidents are not eternal, is that conclusion fully supported? Explain using the lesson's insight about scope.
?
In a Kalam argument, a mutakallim says: 'The world is contingent. Everything contingent has a creator. Therefore the world has a creator.' Is this a valid syllogism? Which figure? What is the middle term? Could this syllogism be used to prove that the creator is eternal? Why or why not?

The Reductio Argument: Form is Not an Efficient Cause for Matter
Reductio argument that form is not an efficient cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional reductio argument showing that form cannot be an efficient cause for matter.

Core Idea
Shape's priority test proves form is not a cause.

The author uses a conditional argument (modus tollens) to show that if form were a cause for matter, then shape would have to be prior to matter—but shape is not prior; therefore form is not a cause. We will walk through each step of this logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the assumption being tested
The assumption is: 'Form (al-sura) is an efficient cause for matter (al-hayula).' This is the claim the author aims to disprove.
2
Trace the logical consequence involving shape
If form is a cause for matter, then form must be prior to matter. But form's existence requires shape (al-shakl) for its individuation—either shape causes the individuation or is concomitant with it. Therefore shape would also have to be prior to matter.
3
Check the factual premise about shape
The author states that shape does not exist before matter because matter is a receptive cause (i.e., locus) for shape. Thus shape is not prior to matter.
4
Recognize the contradiction
We derived that shape must be prior to matter (if form is a cause), but factually shape is not prior. This is a contradiction (khalaf).
5
Conclude the negation of the assumption
Since the assumption leads to a false consequence, the assumption itself is false. Therefore form is not an efficient cause for matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضا ليست علة للهيولى؛ لأن الصورة إنما يجب وجودها مع الشكل أو ليست علة إلخ حاصله أن الصورة الشخصية إنما توجد بالشكل إن كان الشكل علة لتشخصها، أو مع الشكل إن كان مساوقا لتشخصها والشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى؛ لأنها علة قابلية له، فلو كانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى لكانت متقدمة عليها، فيلزم تقدم الشكل عليها؛ لأن الشكل علة لتشخص الصورة أو مساوق له، فيلزم من تقدم الصورة تقدم علة لتشخصها أو ما يساوقه وقد كان الشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى، هذا خلف والمراد بالعلة الفاعلية؛ لأن نفي القابلية ظاهرة مستغنية عن البيان؛ لأن العلة القابلية محل للمقبول فلو كانت علة قابلية للهيولى لزم كون الصورة محلا للهيولى ،وقد بان بطلانه بل قد ثبت بالبرهان محلية الهيولى للصورة. ولا تكون أيضا علة صورية لأنها تكون جزءا للشيء، وليست الصورة جزءا للهيولى بل خارجة عنها ولاحقة لها، كما تقرر. وقد يقال: إن الشكل متأخر عن الصورة بمراتب؛ لأنه متأخر عن الأطراف وهي متأخرة عن المقدار وهو عن الصورة، فكيف يتصور تقدمه عليها أو أن يكون معها؟ وأجيب عنه: أن المراد أن الصورة الشخصية محتاجة في تشخصها إلى الشكل، ولا بأس يحتاج الشيء في تشخصه إلى ما يحتاج إلى ماهيته والكلام الذي ذكره المعترض إنما يفيد تأخر الشكل عن ماهية الصورة ولا كلام فيه وإنما الكلام في الصورة الشخصية، وقد يقال: احتياج الصورة في تشخصها إلى الشكل باطل؛ لأنه إن كان المراد الشكل الجزئي فهو ليس بصحيح، كما ترى في الشمعة المتبدلة الأشكال بقائها بشخصها وفي الهواء المتكاثف والمتخلخل، وإن كان المراد الكلي، فلا يتصور أن يفيد تشخيص الصورة؛ لأن الكلي أمر مبهم فكيف يتعين به غيره؟ وأجيب عنه أن المراد شكل له والمراد بالاحتياج أن الصورة لا تخلو عن فرد ما، وهو من أمارات التشخص لا أنه جزء، ويجوز أن يكون الفرد المنتشر من الشكل أمارة له وليست واحدة منهما غنية عن الأخرى، أما الهيولى فلها احتياج إليها في تحصلها ووجودها؛ لأنها أمر مبهم لا تتقوم بالفعل ما لم ينضم إليها معنى محصل.
Also it (form) is not a cause for matter because form must exist with shape, or it is not a cause etc. The summary is that the individual form exists only through shape if shape is a cause for its individuation, or with shape if it is concomitant with its individuation, and shape does not exist before matter because matter is a receptive cause for it. So if form were a cause for the existence of matter, it would be prior to matter; then the priority of shape over matter would follow because shape is a cause for the individuation of form or concomitant with it, so from the priority of form follows the priority of the cause of its individuation or what is concomitant with it, but shape does not exist before matter—this is a contradiction. The intended is the efficient cause, because the negation of receptivity is apparent and needs no explanation, because the receptive cause is a locus for the received, so if form were a receptive cause for matter, it would follow that form is a locus for matter, but its falsity has been shown; rather, the substantiality of matter for form has been established by demonstration. It is also not a formal cause because it would be a part of the thing, but form is not a part of matter; rather, it is external to it and subsequent to it, as established. And it might be said: Shape is posterior to form by degrees because it is posterior to extremities, which are posterior to magnitude, which is posterior to form, so how can it be conceived to be prior to it or with it? The answer: The intended is that the individual form needs shape for its individuation, and there is no harm that a thing needs for its individuation what is posterior to its essence, and the objection only indicates the posteriority of shape to the essence of form, and there is no dispute about that; rather, the discussion concerns the individual form. And it might be said: The need of form for shape in its individuation is false, because if the intended is the particular shape, it is not correct—as you see in the candle with changing shapes while its individual remains, and in rarefied and condensed air—and if the intended is the universal shape, it cannot be conceived to individuate form because the universal is ambiguous, so how can it determine something else? The answer: The intended is a shape for it, and the intended by 'need' is that form is not devoid of some individual shape, and it is a mark of individuation, not a part, and it is possible that the dispersed individual of shape is a mark for it, and neither of them is independent of the other. As for matter, it has a need for form in its attainment and existence because it is an ambiguous thing that is not actualized unless a actualizing meaning is joined to it.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative commentary proving that form (sura) is not a cause for matter (hayula) in any sense—efficient, receptive, or formal. The author begins by stating that form must exist with shape (shakl), either because shape is a cause of its individuation or concomitant with it. Since shape does not exist before matter (matter being a receptive cause for shape), if form were a cause of matter, form would be prior to matter, and then shape would also have to be prior to matter, which is false—hence a contradiction. The intended causality here is efficient, as the receptive causality is obviously false: if form were a receptive cause, it would be a locus for matter, but it is established that matter is the locus for form. Form is also not a formal cause because it would then be a part of matter, but form is external to matter. An objection is raised: shape is posterior to form by several degrees, so how could it be prior? The answer clarifies that we are discussing the individual form, not the essence of form; the individual form needs shape for its individuation, and it is acceptable that what is posterior to the essence is needed for individuation. Another objection: the need for shape is false because particular shape changes while the individual remains (e.g., a candle), and universal shape cannot individuate. The reply: the intended is that form is never without some individual shape, which serves as a mark (amara) of individuation, not a constituent part. The passage concludes that matter itself needs form for its actual existence, as matter is ambiguous and needs an actualizing meaning.

Key Terms
شكل
تشخص
علة فاعلية
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author derive in the reductio?
A) Shape is prior to matter, but shape is not prior to matter.
B) Form is prior to matter, but form is not prior to matter.
C) Matter is prior to shape, but matter is not prior to shape.
D) Shape is a cause for form, but form is a cause for matter.

Key Takeaway
Form cannot be an efficient cause for matter because that would force shape to be prior to matter, which is false.

Construct a modus tollens argument to refute the claim 'The world is eternal' using the impossibility of an actual infinite. Use an example from al-Ash'ari's proof of the world's creation.

Why Form is Neither Receptive nor Formal Cause for Matter
Eliminating receptive and formal causality of form for matter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why form cannot be a receptive or formal cause for matter, using the definitions of these causality types.

Core Idea
Form is external to matter, not a part or locus.

After disposing of efficient causality, the author quickly rejects the other two causal modes. He appeals to the very definitions of receptive and formal causality to show that form cannot fulfill them in relation to matter. We will examine these brief but crucial arguments.

Guided Path
1
Review the definition of a receptive cause
A receptive cause (al-illa al-qabiliyya) is a locus (mahall) that receives something else. It is that which holds what comes to exist in it.
In hylomorphism, matter is the receptive cause for form.
2
Test whether form can be a receptive cause for matter
If form were a receptive cause for matter, then form would be the locus for matter, i.e., matter would be in form. But it is established by demonstration that matter is the locus for form, not vice versa.
The author says this is 'apparent and needs no explanation.'
3
Review the definition of a formal cause
A formal cause (al-illa al-suriyya) is that which is a part (juz’) of the thing it causes. It enters into the thing’s definition.
For example, the soul is the formal cause of a human being.
4
Test whether form can be a formal cause for matter
Form is not a part of matter; rather, form is external to matter and subsequent to it. Therefore form cannot be a formal cause for matter.
The author states: 'form is not a part of matter; it is external and subsequent.'
5
Conclude that form is not a cause of matter in any traditional sense
Since form fails as efficient, receptive, and formal cause, the only remaining causal possibility (final cause) is not even considered because it is irrelevant to hylomorphic composition. Thus form is not a cause of matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضا ليست علة للهيولى؛ لأن الصورة إنما يجب وجودها مع الشكل أو ليست علة إلخ حاصله أن الصورة الشخصية إنما توجد بالشكل إن كان الشكل علة لتشخصها، أو مع الشكل إن كان مساوقا لتشخصها والشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى؛ لأنها علة قابلية له، فلو كانت الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى لكانت متقدمة عليها، فيلزم تقدم الشكل عليها؛ لأن الشكل علة لتشخص الصورة أو مساوق له، فيلزم من تقدم الصورة تقدم علة لتشخصها أو ما يساوقه وقد كان الشكل لا يوجد قبل الهيولى، هذا خلف والمراد بالعلة الفاعلية؛ لأن نفي القابلية ظاهرة مستغنية عن البيان؛ لأن العلة القابلية محل للمقبول فلو كانت علة قابلية للهيولى لزم كون الصورة محلا للهيولى ،وقد بان بطلانه بل قد ثبت بالبرهان محلية الهيولى للصورة. ولا تكون أيضا علة صورية لأنها تكون جزءا للشيء، وليست الصورة جزءا للهيولى بل خارجة عنها ولاحقة لها، كما تقرر. وقد يقال: إن الشكل متأخر عن الصورة بمراتب؛ لأنه متأخر عن الأطراف وهي متأخرة عن المقدار وهو عن الصورة، فكيف يتصور تقدمه عليها أو أن يكون معها؟ وأجيب عنه: أن المراد أن الصورة الشخصية محتاجة في تشخصها إلى الشكل، ولا بأس يحتاج الشيء في تشخصه إلى ما يحتاج إلى ماهيته والكلام الذي ذكره المعترض إنما يفيد تأخر الشكل عن ماهية الصورة ولا كلام فيه وإنما الكلام في الصورة الشخصية، وقد يقال: احتياج الصورة في تشخصها إلى الشكل باطل؛ لأنه إن كان المراد الشكل الجزئي فهو ليس بصحيح، كما ترى في الشمعة المتبدلة الأشكال بقائها بشخصها وفي الهواء المتكاثف والمتخلخل، وإن كان المراد الكلي، فلا يتصور أن يفيد تشخيص الصورة؛ لأن الكلي أمر مبهم فكيف يتعين به غيره؟ وأجيب عنه أن المراد شكل له والمراد بالاحتياج أن الصورة لا تخلو عن فرد ما، وهو من أمارات التشخص لا أنه جزء، ويجوز أن يكون الفرد المنتشر من الشكل أمارة له وليست واحدة منهما غنية عن الأخرى، أما الهيولى فلها احتياج إليها في تحصلها ووجودها؛ لأنها أمر مبهم لا تتقوم بالفعل ما لم ينضم إليها معنى محصل.
