Defining al-Hayz: Spatial Position vs. Place
Defines al-hayz and distinguishes it from al-makan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-hayz and al-makan using the author's definition and apply this distinction to identify which bodies have a hayz but no place.

Core Idea
Hayz is broader than place: every body has a hayz, but only contained bodies have a place.

The passage opens by defining al-hayz as the sensible position that distinguishes a body. The author immediately distinguishes it from al-makan (place), which is the inner surface of a container. This lesson will help you parse this subtle but crucial distinction, which underlies later arguments in natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of al-hayz
Identify the phrase 'الحيز ما يمتاز به الجسم في الإشارة الحسية' and note that hayz is a sensible spatial marker.
The word 'الإشارة الحسية' (sensible indication) ties hayz to empirical perception.
2
Contrast al-hayz with al-makan
The author says hayz is not place (المكان). Place is the inner surface of a container. Hayz is a body's position regardless of containment.
Example: the earth seeks the center (a hayz), not the water's surface (a place).
3
Apply the distinction to the celestial sphere
The author mentions 'فلك الأفلاك' (sphere of spheres) as a body with a hayz but no place, because it has no container.
This shows hayz is more general: all bodies have a position, but only bodies within a container have a place.
4
Compare alternative views on hayz and place
The passage notes that for those who affirm abstract dimension (al-bu'd al-mujarrad), hayz and place are identical. For those who affirm imagined dimension (al-bu'd al-mawhum), hayz is the imagined void. Understand that the definition is not universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحيز الحيز ما يمتاز به الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، وهو الوضع، وهو الطبعي لكل جسم لا المكان، أعني السطح الباطن من الحاوي؛ فإن الأرض مثلا إنما تطلب المركز لا سطح الماء حيث كان وإنما سطح الماء مطلوب بالعرض حيث كان حيزاهما متجاورين، والحيز عندهم أعم من المكان؛ إذ لبعض الأجسام حيز ولا مكان كفلك الأفلاك، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد المجرد فالمكان والحيز واحد، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد الموهوم فيفهم من كلام التفتازاني في شرح عقائد النسفي أن المكان حيث قال: المتمكن أخص من المتحيز؛ لأن الحيز هو الفراغ المتوهم الذي يشغله شيء ممتد أو غير ممتد.
القواسر وهي الأمور الخارجة عن الجسم المؤثرة فيه تأثيرا غريبا كرمي الحجر إلى فوق.
لذاته أي الطبيعة، وهي الصورة النوعية التي تقتضي حيزا معينا كما سلف تقريره في بحث إثبات الصورة النوعية، ولا دخل لهيولى الجسم في اقتضاء الحيز، أما أولا فلأنها قابلة محضة، وأما ثانيا فلأنها في الحيز تابعة للصورة الجسمية، وأيضا لا دخل في اختصاص الحيز للصورة الجسمية؛ لأن نسبتها إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية.
حيزان طبعيان: إنما اكتفى على الحيزين؛ لأنه لما بطل كون الحيزين لجسم، بطل ما زاد بالطريق الأولى، والحاصل أنه إذا كان الجسم واحد حيزان، فلا يخلو إما أن يحصل فيهما معا أو لا، وعلى الثاني إما أن يحصل في أحدهما أو يكون خارجا عنهما، والأول محال بالبداهة؛ لامتناع حصول جسم واحد في حيزين معا، والثاني بين إبطاله بقوله: فإذا حصل في أحدهما إلخ. والثالث وهو ما يكون خارجا عنهما بين بطلانه بأنه إذا كان خارجا عنهما، فإما يطلب كليهما معا، فهذا محال؛ لامتناع توجه الشيء إلى جهتين أو لا يطلب شيئا منهما فلا يكون واحد منهما طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا أو يطلب أحدهما دون الآخر، فيلزم أن لا يكون الآخر طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا، وإنما لم يتعرض للأول؛ لظهور استحالته، ولا للثالث؛ لرجوعه بالآخرة إلى الثاني الذي ذكره المصنف حاله.
Al-hayz is that by which a body is distinguished in sensible indication, and it is position (wad'), and it is natural for every body, not place (makan), meaning the inner surface of the container. For the earth, for example, seeks the center, not the surface of water wherever it is; rather, the surface of water is sought accidentally when their positions are adjacent. According to them, al-hayz is more general than place, for some bodies have a hayz but no place, like the sphere of spheres. According to those who affirm abstract dimension, place and hayz are identical. According to those who affirm imagined dimension, it is understood from the words of al-Taftazani in his commentary on 'Aqa'id al-Nasafi that place is 'that which occupies is more specific than that which is extended, because hayz is the imagined void that something extended or non-extended occupies.' [Definition of al-qawasir:] They are external things affecting the body in an unnatural way, like throwing a stone upward. [Explanation of 'by itself' (li-dhatihi):] That is, by nature, which is the specific form that necessitates a particular hayz, as established in the discussion of proving the specific form. And the hyle of the body has no role in necessitating the hayz: first, because it is mere potentiality; second, because it is in the hayz as following the corporeal form. Also, the corporeal form itself has no role in the specification of the hayz because its relation to all hayzes is equal. [On two natural hayzes:] He confined it to two hayzes because when the possibility of two hayzes for a body is disproved, anything more is disproved by priority. The upshot: if a body had two hayzes, then either it occupies both simultaneously (impossible by intuition, as one body cannot occupy two hayzes at once) or not. If not, then either it occupies one of them or is outside both. The second is disproved by his saying: 'If it occupies one...' etc. The third case (outside both) is disproved because: if outside both, then either it seeks both together (impossible, as a thing cannot be directed to two directions) or it seeks neither (then neither is natural, but they were assumed natural) or it seeks one but not the other (then the other is not natural, contrary to assumption). The author did not address the first case (both simultaneously) due to its obvious impossibility, nor the third case because it ultimately reduces to the second case which the author discussed.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines al-hayz (position) and distinguishes it from al-makan (place). Al-hayz is the sensible spatial position that every body possesses naturally. Unlike place (the inner surface of a containing body), al-hayz is not necessarily defined by a container; for example, the outermost celestial sphere (falak al-aflak) has a hayz but no place. The author notes alternative views: for those who believe in abstract dimension, hayz and place are identical; for those who believe in imagined dimension, hayz is the imagined void occupied by a body. The passage then defines 'qawasir' (external coercive forces) as forces that compel a body to move unnaturally, like throwing a stone upward. The author then explains that a body seeks its natural hayz by its specific form (surah naw'iyyah), not by its matter (hayula). He argues that a body cannot have two natural hayzes. The proof enumerates possibilities: if a body had two natural hayzes, it would either occupy both simultaneously (impossible), occupy one, or be outside both. The impossibility of occupying one is argued by noting that if it occupies one, it would still seek the other, contradicting the assumption that both are natural. The case of being outside both leads to similar contradictions. Thus, a body has exactly one natural hayz.

Key Terms
الحيز
المكان
الوضع
فلك الأفلاك
البعد المجرد
البعد الموهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following bodies has a hayz but no place?
A) A stone inside a well.
B) A fish in the ocean.
C) The celestial sphere (falak al-aflak).
D) The earth itself.

Key Takeaway
Al-hayz is the sensible position of a body, more general than place, and every body has exactly one natural hayz.

The Specific Form as the Determinant of Natural Position
Argues that the specific form, not matter or corporeal form, determines natural position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the specific form (surah naw'iyyah) and not matter or corporeal form determines a body's natural hayz, using the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
The specific form, not matter or corporeal form, drives a body to its natural position.

The author explains that a body seeks its natural hayz 'by itself' (li-dhatihi), meaning by its specific form (surah naw'iyyah). He rules out matter (hayula) and even the corporeal form (surah jismiyyah) as determinants. Understanding this causal hierarchy is key to grasping Aristotelian physics and its use in Kalam proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify what 'li-dhatihi' refers to
The phrase 'لذاته أي الطبيعة' clarifies that 'by itself' means 'by nature', i.e., the specific form.
Nature here is the internal principle of motion and rest.
2
Exclude matter (hayula) as a cause
The author gives two reasons: matter is pure potentiality (قابلة محضة) and it follows the corporeal form in being in a hayz.
Matter cannot initiate motion; it only receives.
3
Exclude corporeal form as a cause
The corporeal form (surah jismiyyah) has an equal relation to all hayzes, so it cannot specify one.
Corporeal form gives extension, not direction.
4
Confirm specific form as the determinant
By elimination, the specific form (surah naw'iyyah) must be what necessitates a particular hayz, as previously established in the discussion of proving the specific form.
This links back to earlier arguments about the existence of specific forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحيز الحيز ما يمتاز به الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، وهو الوضع، وهو الطبعي لكل جسم لا المكان، أعني السطح الباطن من الحاوي؛ فإن الأرض مثلا إنما تطلب المركز لا سطح الماء حيث كان وإنما سطح الماء مطلوب بالعرض حيث كان حيزاهما متجاورين، والحيز عندهم أعم من المكان؛ إذ لبعض الأجسام حيز ولا مكان كفلك الأفلاك، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد المجرد فالمكان والحيز واحد، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد الموهوم فيفهم من كلام التفتازاني في شرح عقائد النسفي أن المكان حيث قال: المتمكن أخص من المتحيز؛ لأن الحيز هو الفراغ المتوهم الذي يشغله شيء ممتد أو غير ممتد.
القواسر وهي الأمور الخارجة عن الجسم المؤثرة فيه تأثيرا غريبا كرمي الحجر إلى فوق.
لذاته أي الطبيعة، وهي الصورة النوعية التي تقتضي حيزا معينا كما سلف تقريره في بحث إثبات الصورة النوعية، ولا دخل لهيولى الجسم في اقتضاء الحيز، أما أولا فلأنها قابلة محضة، وأما ثانيا فلأنها في الحيز تابعة للصورة الجسمية، وأيضا لا دخل في اختصاص الحيز للصورة الجسمية؛ لأن نسبتها إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية.
حيزان طبعيان: إنما اكتفى على الحيزين؛ لأنه لما بطل كون الحيزين لجسم، بطل ما زاد بالطريق الأولى، والحاصل أنه إذا كان الجسم واحد حيزان، فلا يخلو إما أن يحصل فيهما معا أو لا، وعلى الثاني إما أن يحصل في أحدهما أو يكون خارجا عنهما، والأول محال بالبداهة؛ لامتناع حصول جسم واحد في حيزين معا، والثاني بين إبطاله بقوله: فإذا حصل في أحدهما إلخ. والثالث وهو ما يكون خارجا عنهما بين بطلانه بأنه إذا كان خارجا عنهما، فإما يطلب كليهما معا، فهذا محال؛ لامتناع توجه الشيء إلى جهتين أو لا يطلب شيئا منهما فلا يكون واحد منهما طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا أو يطلب أحدهما دون الآخر، فيلزم أن لا يكون الآخر طبعيا، وقد فرضناه طبعيا، وإنما لم يتعرض للأول؛ لظهور استحالته، ولا للثالث؛ لرجوعه بالآخرة إلى الثاني الذي ذكره المصنف حاله.
Al-hayz is that by which a body is distinguished in sensible indication, and it is position (wad'), and it is natural for every body, not place (makan), meaning the inner surface of the container. For the earth, for example, seeks the center, not the surface of water wherever it is; rather, the surface of water is sought accidentally when their positions are adjacent. According to them, al-hayz is more general than place, for some bodies have a hayz but no place, like the sphere of spheres. According to those who affirm abstract dimension, place and hayz are identical. According to those who affirm imagined dimension, it is understood from the words of al-Taftazani in his commentary on 'Aqa'id al-Nasafi that place is 'that which occupies is more specific than that which is extended, because hayz is the imagined void that something extended or non-extended occupies.' [Definition of al-qawasir:] They are external things affecting the body in an unnatural way, like throwing a stone upward. [Explanation of 'by itself' (li-dhatihi):] That is, by nature, which is the specific form that necessitates a particular hayz, as established in the discussion of proving the specific form. Also, the corporeal form itself has no role in the specification of the hayz because its relation to all hayzes is equal. [On two natural hayzes:] He confined it to two hayzes because when the possibility of two hayzes for a body is disproved, anything more is disproved by priority. The upshot: if a body had two hayzes, then either it occupies both simultaneously (impossible by intuition, as one body cannot occupy two hayzes at once) or not. If not, then either it occupies one of them or is outside both. The author did not address the first case (both simultaneously) due to its obvious impossibility, nor the third case because it ultimately reduces to the second case which the author discussed.

Proof by Cases: A Body Cannot Have Two Natural Positions
Proves by cases that a body cannot have two natural positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's proof by cases that a body cannot have two natural hayzes and evaluate its logical validity.

Core Idea
A body cannot have two natural positions; any scenario leads to contradiction.

The author concludes the passage with a rigorous proof that a body can have only one natural hayz. He systematically enumerates all possibilities if a body were assumed to have two hayzes, then eliminates each. This lesson will walk you through the case analysis, a method used extensively in Islamic logic and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption to be disproved
The assumption: a body (jism) has two natural hayzes (حيزان طبعيان).
The author uses 'حيزان طبعيان' to mean two hayzes that are each natural for the same body.
2
Enumerate the three possibilities
If a body has two hayzes, then either: (1) it occupies both simultaneously, (2) it occupies one of them, or (3) it is outside both.
These are exhaustive and mutually exclusive cases.
3
Eliminate case 1 (both simultaneously)
This is impossible by intuition (بالبداهة) because one body cannot be in two places at once.
The author does not elaborate because it is self-evident.
4
Eliminate case 2 (occupies one)
If it occupies one, it must still have a natural inclination to the other (since both are assumed natural). But if it occupies one, it is not in the other, so it would have to seek it. Seeking two different directions is impossible.
The author says: 'فإذا حصل في أحدهما إلخ' – the implication is that the body would be drawn to the other, contradicting its being at rest.
5
Eliminate case 3 (outside both)
If outside both, then either it seeks both (impossible for a single body), or it seeks neither (then neither hayz is natural, contradicting assumption), or it seeks one but not the other (then the other is not natural, again a contradiction).
Thus all possibilities lead to absurdity, so the initial assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحيز الحيز ما يمتاز به الجسم في الإشارة الحسية، وهو الوضع، وهو الطبعي لكل جسم لا المكان، أعني السطح الباطن من الحاوي؛ فإن الأرض مثلا إنما تطلب المركز لا سطح الماء حيث كان وإنما سطح الماء مطلوب بالعرض حيث كان حيزاهما متجاورين، والحيز عندهم أعم من المكان؛ إذ لبعض الأجسام حيز ولا مكان كفلك الأفلاك، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد المجرد فالمكان والحيز واحد، وأما عند القائلين بالبعد الموهوم فيفهم من كلام التفتازاني في شرح عقائد النسفي أن المكان حيث قال: المتمكن أخص من المتحيز؛ لأن الحيز هو الفراغ المتوهم الذي يشغله شيء ممتد أو غير ممتد.
القواسر وهي الأمور الخارجة عن الجسم المؤثرة فيه تأثيرا غريبا كرمي الحجر إلى فوق.

Natural Shapes and the Definition of Shape
Author determines the natural shape of simple and composite bodies and defines shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of shape (hay'a) and explain why simple bodies must be spherical using the uniformity argument.

Core Idea
Uniformity of cause and matter demands a spherical shape for simple bodies; shape is defined by boundaries enclosing extent.

Why are the elements spherical? Why do animals and plants have diverse forms? This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma answers these questions by analyzing the cause of shape. We will learn how the philosopher uses the principle of uniformity to deduce the shape of simple bodies, and then dissect the definition of shape itself, which applies to all bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of bodies
The passage divides bodies into simple (basit) and composite (murakkab). Simple bodies are elements like fire, air, water, earth; composite bodies are mixtures like animals and plants.
Simple bodies have a single nature; composites have mixed natures.
2
Understand the uniformity argument for simple bodies
A single force in a single homogeneous matter can only produce a homogeneous action. The sphere is the only shape where all directions are identical. Any other shape would imply different actions in different directions, which is impossible given the uniformity of agent and recipient.
This is a proof by contradiction: assume non-spherical → contradictory consequences → must be spherical.
3
Apply the same logic to composite bodies
Composite bodies have multiple forces and matters, so their shape is not uniform but determined by the specific mixture (mizaj) and composition. Thus they naturally take diverse shapes as seen in species.
The author says 'its natural shape is what its type requires' – a teleological explanation.
4
Extract and analyze the definition of shape
The definition is: 'The configuration (hay'a) resulting from the enclosure of one or more boundaries by the extent (miqdar) of the body.' Identify the genus (hay'a – configuration) and differentia (resulting from enclosure of boundaries by extent). Check if it covers all shapes (simple and composite).
This is a complete hadd (real definition) – includes cause (enclosure) and effect (configuration).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي الشكل كان ذلك الجسم بسيطا فشكله الطبعي هو الكرة؛ لأن القوة الواحدة في المادة الواحدة المتشابهة لا تفعل إلا فعلا متشابها، وما سوى الكرة ففيه اختلاف الأفعال، فلو كان شكل الجسم البسيط غير كري للزم اختلاف الأفعال، والتالي باطل؛ لأن اختلاف الأفعال إما بالفاعل أو بالقابل، وههنا كلاهما مفقودان، وإن كان ذلك الجسم مركبا فشكله الطبعي ما يقتضي نوعه بحسب المزاج والتركيب، كما ترى في الحيوانات والنباتات أشكالا مختلفة بحسب نوع نوع. الهيئة الحاصلة عن إحاطة الحد الواحد أو الحدود بمقدار الجسم
The shape: if that body is simple, its natural shape is the sphere, because a single force in a single homogeneous matter does nothing but a homogeneous act, and anything other than the sphere involves difference of actions. So if the shape of a simple body were not spherical, it would require difference of actions, and the consequent is false because difference of actions is either from the agent or recipient, and here both are absent. If that body is composite, its natural shape is what its type requires according to the mixture and composition, as you see in animals and plants different shapes according to their species. The configuration resulting from the enclosure of one or more boundaries by the extent of the body.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage discusses the natural shapes of bodies. For simple bodies (elements like fire, air, water, earth), the natural shape is spherical because a single uniform force in uniform matter produces a uniform effect. Any deviation from spherical would imply different actions in different directions, which is impossible given the uniformity of agent and recipient. For composite bodies (like animals and plants), the shape is determined by their specific mixture (مزاج) and composition, leading to diverse forms. Finally, the author gives a definition of shape: it is the configuration (هيئة) that results from the enclosure of one or more boundaries by the body's extent (مقدار). This definition is general and applies to both simple and composite bodies. The word 'هي' at the beginning may be a remnant of the previous section or a typographical artifact.

Key Terms
كرة
هيئة
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the natural shape of a simple body be spherical?
A) Because all bodies are spherical
B) Because a uniform force in uniform matter can only produce a uniform effect, and only the sphere has uniform action in all directions
C) Because composite bodies are spherical
D) Because the boundaries of a simple body are always spherical

Key Takeaway
Simple bodies are spherical due to uniformity of cause and matter; shape is the configuration resulting from boundaries enclosing the body's extent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition of shape provided in the passage, analyze the shape of a composite body like a horse: identify the boundaries and the extent that enclose it. Is the definition satisfied?

Apply the uniformity argument to a hypothetical simple body that is cubic. Why would a cubic simple body be impossible? Write a short proof using the author's reasoning.

Definitions of Motion and Rest
Author defines motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define motion and rest using the Aristotelian framework of potentiality and actuality, and distinguish rest from simple absence of motion.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual actualization; rest is the absence of motion in what can move.

This passage opens the chapter on motion and rest in al-Hidayat al-Hikma. The author gives definitions that tie motion directly to the potential-actual distinction used in Islamic philosophy. Understanding these definitions is crucial for the proof that follows and for later arguments about change and causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined terms
Find the two terms being defined: الحركة (motion) and السكون (rest). The author defines them in turn.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for motion
Motion's genus is 'exit' (خروج). The differentia includes 'from potentiality to actuality' (من القوة إلى الفعل) and 'gradually' (على سبيل التدريج). This distinguishes motion from instantaneous change.
The gradualness is key: it excludes non-gradual changes like creation ex nihilo.
3
Extract the genus and differentia for rest
Rest's genus is 'absence' (عدم). The differentia is 'in that which is apt to move' (عما من شأنه أن يتحرك). This separates rest from simple non-motion: a stone does not move but is not said to be 'at rest' in this sense because it lacks the capacity for motion.
Privative definition: rest is not a positive state but a lack in a suitable subject.
4
Classify the definition types
Motion's definition is a real definition (hadd haqiqi) because it captures the essence. Rest's definition is a description (rasm) because it is privative and uses negation.
In logic, privations cannot have a hadd; only a rasm is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحركة والسكون. أما الحركة فهي الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، وأما السكون فهو عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك، وكل جسم متحرك فله محرك غير الجسمية؛ إذ لو تحرك الجسم بما هو جسم لكان كل جسم متحركا، والتالي كاذب، فالمقدم مثله.
Chapter on motion and rest. As for motion, it is the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually. As for rest, it is the absence of motion in that which is apt to move. And every moving body has a mover other than its corporeality; for if the body moved insofar as it is a body, then every body would be moving. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines motion and rest, and then provides a proof that every moving body requires a mover distinct from its own corporeality. The definition of motion as gradual exit from potentiality to actuality is standard in Aristotelian physics. Rest is defined negatively as the absence of motion in something that naturally can move. The proof is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens): if a body moved by virtue of being a body, then every body would be moving (since the cause would be universal). But we observe that not every body is moving (some are at rest). Therefore, the body does not move merely by being a body; there must be an external mover. This argument resembles the kalam cosmological argument for a mover. The author does not here name the mover, but it is implied to be an immaterial principle.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
القوة
الفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what differentiates motion from any general change?
A) Motion is always in place.
B) Motion is gradual.
C) Motion requires an external mover.
D) Motion is the opposite of rest.

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined as gradual actualization; rest is the privation of motion in a subject capable of it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof of a Mover Beyond Corporeality
Author proves every moving body has a mover beyond its corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the modus tollens argument that every moving body requires a mover other than its own corporeality.

Core Idea
If motion came from being a body, all bodies would move; some don't, so motion's cause is beyond corporeality.

After defining motion and rest, the author presents a concise proof that a moving body cannot move simply because it is a body. The argument is a textbook example of a conditional syllogism (modus tollens). This proof is a microcosm of the philosophical arguments for a transcendent mover used in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
The claim to prove: 'Every moving body has a mover other than its corporeality' (وكل جسم متحرك فله محرك غير الجسمية).
This is the thesis of the entire section.
2
Locate the conditional premise
Find the 'if...then' statement: 'If the body moved insofar as it is a body, then every body would be moving' (إذ لو تحرك الجسم بما هو جسم لكان كل جسم متحركا).
The antecedent is 'body moves by virtue of being a body'.
3
Identify the denial of the consequent
The author asserts the consequent is false: 'The consequent is false' (والتالي كاذب). This is because we observe bodies at rest, i.e., not moving.
The observation is empirical: some bodies are in a state of rest.
4
Apply modus tollens
From 'if P then Q' and 'not Q', we infer 'not P'. Here: body does not move by virtue of being a body. Therefore, the moving body must have a mover distinct from its corporeality.
The logical form is valid: denying the consequent forces denial of the antecedent.
5
Conclude the existence of an external mover
Since the body does not move from its own nature, something else (a mover) causes its motion. The author does not name it, but it points to an immaterial principle.
This argument is a stepping stone to the proof of a transcendent mover.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحركة والسكون. أما الحركة فهي الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج، وأما السكون فهو عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك، وكل جسم متحرك فله محرك غير الجسمية؛ إذ لو تحرك الجسم بما هو جسم لكان كل جسم متحركا، والتالي كاذب، فالمقدم مثله.

And every moving body has a mover other than its corporeality; for if the body moved insofar as it is a body, then every body would be moving. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines motion and rest, and then provides a proof that every moving body requires a mover distinct from its own corporeality. The definition of motion as gradual exit from potentiality to actuality is standard in Aristotelian physics. Rest is defined negatively as the absence of motion in something that naturally can move. The proof is a conditional syllogism (modus tollens): if a body moved by virtue of being a body, then every body would be moving (since the cause would be universal). But we observe that not every body is moving (some are at rest). Therefore, the body does not move merely by being a body; there must be an external mover. This argument resembles the kalam cosmological argument for a mover. The author does not here name the mover, but it is implied to be an immaterial principle.

Key Takeaway

Defining Motion and Its Two Senses
Two senses of motion defined and contrasted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between intermediate motion and traversal motion and identify their definitions as presented in the text.

Core Idea
Motion has two senses: a non-stationary state and a continuous extension.

The author begins by distinguishing fully actual beings from partially actual ones, then defines motion as the gradual actualization of potential. He then explains that 'motion' is used for two distinct concepts: the intermediate state of being between start and end, and the extended continuum that coincides with the distance traversed. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of change and time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the generic definition of motion
Find where the author says 'the gradual exit of what is potential from potentiality to actuality is called motion'. That is the definition covering both senses.
2
Extract the two specific definitions
The author gives two descriptions. For intermediate motion: 'the body being between the starting point and the endpoint such that it is not at any limit after reaching nor before'. For traversal motion: 'the extended thing beginning from the start... coinciding with the distance'.
3
Compare the two senses
Intermediate motion is a non-static state (a 'how' of the body), while traversal motion is an extended continuum that is divisible with the distance. One is a momentary condition, the other is a gradual extension.
4
Note the ontological status
The author explicitly states that intermediate motion exists externally without doubt, while traversal motion is debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحركة الموجود بالفعل إما أن يكون بالفعل من كل وجه ليس له كمال منتظر، بل كل كمال له حاصل بالفعل كالبارئ والعقول عند الحكماء، أو يكون بالفعل من بعض الوجوه، وبالقوة من بعض آخر كالأجسام، وما بالقوة يمكن أن يخرج بالفعل، وإلا لم يكن بالقوة بل يصير ممتنعا، وقد فرض بالقوة، فإذا خرج من القوة إلى الفعل، فهو إما دفعة أو تدريجا، فخروج ما بالقوة بالتدريج يسمى حركة، ثم الحركة تطلق على معنيين أحدهما: كون الجسم بين المبدأ والمنتهى بحيث لا يكون في حد من حدود المسافة بعد الوصول ولا قبله يعني حالة للجسم تقتضي مروره على المسافة، ولا يستقر ما دام موصوفا بها على حد من حدودها، وتسمى حركة توسطية، وهذه موجودة في الخارج بلا ريب، والثاني: الأمر الممتد، المبتدئ من مبدأ المسافة المستمر إلى المنتهى المنطبق على المسافة المنقسم بانقسامها، يعني امتدادا غير قار متدرج الأجزاء يحصل بمرور الجسم على المسافة تدرجا، ويسمى حركة قطعية؛ لحصولها بقطع المسافة، وهل هي موجودة في الخارج أم لا؟ فقيل: ليست موجودة في الخارج بل في الخيال، وقيل موجودة في الخارج؛ لأنها محل الزمان، والزمان قائم بها، والزمان موجود في الخارج، فمحله لا بد أن يكون موجودا في الخارج، والموجود بالفعل من كل وجه لا يتصور له الحركة؛ لأنها لتحصيل كمال لم يكن، ولما كان كل كمال حاصلا له بالفعل لم يتصور له الحركة، وكذلك السكون؛ لأنه عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك.
Chapter on motion. The actual existent is either actual from every aspect, having no awaited perfection, but every perfection is already actual, like the Creator and the intellects according to the philosophers; or it is actual from some aspects and potential from others, like bodies. What is potential can become actual, otherwise it would not be potential but would become impossible, yet it was assumed potential. When it goes from potentiality to actuality, it is either instantaneously or gradually. The gradual exit of what is potential is called motion. Then motion is used in two senses: one is the body being between the starting point and the endpoint such that it is not at any limit of the distance after reaching nor before, meaning a state of the body requiring its passage over the distance, and it does not settle as long as it is described by it at any limit of its limits; this is called intermediate motion, and this exists externally without doubt. The second is the extended thing beginning from the start of the distance continuing to the endpoint coinciding with the distance divided by its division, meaning a non-static extension with gradual parts obtained by the body's passage over the distance gradually; it is called traversal motion, because it is obtained by traversing the distance. Does it exist externally or not? It is said: it does not exist externally but only in the imagination. And it is said: it exists externally because it is the locus of time, and time subsists in it, and time exists externally, so its locus must exist externally. That which is actual from every aspect cannot have motion, because motion is for acquiring a perfection not yet possessed; since every perfection is already actual for it, motion cannot be conceived for it. Similarly for rest, because it is the absence of motion in what is capable of motion.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage begins by distinguishing two types of existents: those that are fully actual (like the Creator and the intellects) and those that are partially actual and partially potential (like bodies). The latter can undergo change. The transition from potentiality to actuality, if gradual, is motion. Then the author presents the well-known distinction between two meanings of motion: intermediate motion (حركة توسطية) which is the state of being between the starting point and the endpoint, a non-static condition that exists in the external world; and traversal motion (حركة قطعية) which is the continuous extension that coincides with the distance traversed. The latter's existence is debated: some say it is only mental, others say it is real because it is the substratum of time, which itself is real. The passage also reiterates that fully actual beings cannot have motion or rest because they have no unrealized perfections. This distinction is crucial for understanding Aristotle's physics and its reception in Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
الحركة التوسطية
الحركة القطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which sense of motion is said to exist externally without doubt?
A) Traversal motion (الحركة القطعية)
B) Intermediate motion (الحركة التوسطية)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined as the gradual actualization of potential, and it is used in two senses: a non-stationary state (intermediate) and a continuous extension (traversal).

The Debate on Traversal Motion's Existence
The debate on traversal motion's external existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument for the existence of traversal motion based on its relation to time and evaluate the opposing view.

Core Idea
Traversal motion's reality is defended because it is the locus of time, which is real.

After defining the two senses of motion, the author presents a debate: does traversal motion exist externally or only in the imagination? He reports an argument for its external existence: it is the locus of time, and time exists externally. This argument is a classic example of how metaphysical premises are used to defend the reality of a concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim in dispute
The debate is over whether traversal motion (الحركة القطعية) exists externally or only in the mind.
2
Find the argument for external existence
The passage says: 'it is said it exists externally because it is the locus of time, and time subsists in it, and time exists externally, so its locus must exist externally.' Identify the premises.
3
Reconstruct the syllogism
Formalize: Major: Time exists externally. Minor: Traversal motion is the locus of time. Conclusion: Therefore, traversal motion exists externally. This is a conditional syllogism (if time exists, and motion is its locus, then motion exists).
This is a modus ponens argument.
4
Identify the opposing view
Others say it does not exist externally but only in the imagination. The author does not endorse either side here.
5
Consider the implication for fully actual beings
The passage concludes that fully actual beings cannot have motion, reinforcing that motion belongs to potential beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition of Motion in Position
Author elaborates motion in position and its subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify motion in position (الحركة في الوضع) and distinguish it from motion in place (الحركة في الأين) based on whether the location changes.

Core Idea
Motion in position changes relations, not necessarily location.

The passage defines motion in position as a change in the relation of a body's parts to external things. This is a distinct type of motion from motion in place, and it can occur with or without it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analysing physical change in the Islamic worldview.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic: الحركة في الوضع.
2
Extract the formal definition
The text says: هو التغير في نسبة أجزاء الجسم إلى الأمور الخارجية. This is the core definition: change in the relation of parts to external things.
3
Compare with motion in place
Motion in place involves a change of location (الأين). In motion in position, the body may stay in the same place while relations change.
4
Apply the definition to the example of standing
A seated person stands: both relation of parts and location change, so both motions occur together.
5
Test with rotational motion
A rotating sphere: location of the whole remains the same, but each part changes its position relative to external things – so motion in position occurs without motion in place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بوقوع الحركة في الوضع: هو التغير في نسبة أجزاء الجسم إلى الأمور الخارجية، والحركة في الوضع قد توجد مع حركة في الأين كالقاعد إذا قام؛ فإنه قد تبدل نسبة أجزاء الجسم بالنسبة إلى الأمور الخارجية مع تبدل المكان، فإن ما يحويه حين قعوده غير ما يحويه حين القيام، وقد توجد بدونها كالحركة على الاستدارة وهي ما ذكره المصنف بقوله: ' وهي أن تكون للجسم حركة على الاستدارة، وحاصله : أن يبقى الجسم في حيزه، وتتبدل نسبة أجزائه إلى الأمور الخارجية كالكرة إذا تحركت على مركزها؛ فإن كل جزء منها يفارق وضعه ومكانه والكل يلازم وضعه ومكانه، فإن قيل: لما فارق كل جزء منه مكانه ووضعه فارق كله مكانه أيضا؟ فإن كل الجسم عبارة عن أجزائه، قلنا: قد يغاير حكم المجموع حكم كل واحد كالرغيف الواحد يشبع كل واحد من الناس، ولا يشبع جميع الناس.
And what is meant by the occurrence of motion in position is: the change in the relation of the parts of the body to external things. Motion in position may exist together with motion in place, as when a seated person stands; for the relation of the body's parts to external things changes with the change of place, as what occupies when sitting is different from what occupies when standing. And it may exist without it, like rotational motion, which is what the author mentioned by his statement: 'That the body has rotational motion, and its gist is: that the body remains in its spatial region, and the relation of its parts to external things changes, like a sphere when it rotates on its axis; for each part of it leaves its position and its place, but the whole remains in its position and place.' If it is said: 'Since every part of it left its place and position, did the whole also leave its place? For the whole body is nothing but its parts.' We say: 'The ruling of the whole may differ from the ruling of each part, like a single loaf that satiates each individual person but does not satiate all people.'
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on motion in position (الوضع). It begins by defining the intended meaning: change in the arrangement of a body's parts relative to external things. It then distinguishes two scenarios: motion in position can accompany motion in place (e.g., a person standing up, where both the arrangement and the location change), or it can occur without place motion, as in rotational motion around an axis. The author quotes the original text: the sphere rotates, each part changes its place and position, but the whole sphere remains in the same place. An objection is raised: if every part leaves its place, then the whole must also leave, because the whole is just the sum of its parts. The answer is that the attribute of the whole can differ from that of its parts, illustrated by a single loaf that satiates each person individually but not all together. This analogy shows that the whole can retain its place while parts change, because place for the whole is defined differently.

The Objection and the Principle of Whole vs Parts
Author addresses an objection about rotational motion using the principle of whole vs parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that if each part moves position, the whole must as well, and explain the response using the loaf analogy.

Core Idea
The whole can have a different attribute than its parts, as shown by the loaf that satiates each but not all.

The passage raises an objection: if every part of a rotating sphere leaves its place, the whole should also leave. But the response introduces a logical principle: the ruling of the whole may differ from that of its parts. This principle is illustrated with a vivid analogy: one loaf satiates each individual but not the entire population. Understanding this principle is essential for avoiding fallacies about parts and wholes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the statement: 'فإن قيل: لما فارق كل جزء منه مكانه ووضعه فارق كله مكانه أيضا؟' The objector argues that since each part leaves its place, the whole must leave because the whole is just the sum of parts.
2
Identify the response
The author responds: 'قلنا: قد يغاير حكم المجموع حكم كل واحد' – the ruling of the whole can differ from the ruling of each part.
3
Understand the loaf analogy
The analogy: a single loaf satiates each individual (كل واحد من الناس) but does not satiate all people together (جميع الناس). This shows that the property of satiety changes when applied to the collective.
4
Apply to rotational motion
Similarly, the place of the whole sphere is defined relative to its environment, not as the aggregate of individual parts' places. The whole retains its place while parts change position.
5
Assess the validity of the response
Does the analogy hold? The objector might argue that place is different from satiety, but the principle is logical: a collective can have a property that individuals do not. The response is sound within the framework.

Two types of motion: real and accidental
Author classifies motion into real motion of the subject itself and accidental motion of an attached object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (real) motion and accidental motion, and identify the true subject of motion in physical scenarios.

Core Idea
Motion can belong truly to a body or be attributed accidentally through connection.

In the study of natural philosophy (tabīʿiyyāt), distinguishing the true subject of motion is crucial. The author classifies motion into two types: one where the body itself is truly moving, and another where motion is ascribed only because of connection to a moving body. This lesson will help you apply this classification to clarify physical causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find the sentence that states motion is of two types. Note the key terms: 'real and essential' (ḥaqīqa wa bi-dh-dhāt) vs. 'secondary and accidental' (thāniyan wa bi-l-ʿaraḍ).
These are the fundamental divisions.
2
Examine the first example: the ship
The author gives the ship (al-fulk) as an example of a body that moves essentially. Why is the ship described as truly moving? Because motion originates in it directly.
The ship is the subject of motion in itself.
3
Examine the second example: the passenger
The passenger on the ship is said to be 'at rest truly and essentially' (sākin ḥaqīqa wa bi-dh-dhāt). Yet we attribute motion to him 'by connection' (bi-l-ittiṣāl). Identify the contrast: the passenger is not intrinsically moving; his motion is accidental.
Accidental motion depends on the real motion of another body.
4
Apply the distinction to new cases
Consider a person sitting in a moving car. Is the person moving essentially or accidentally? Apply the same logic: the car moves essentially, the passenger accidentally via connection.
This classification helps determine the real cause of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحركة: الحركة على نوعين أحدهما: أن يكون قيام وصف الحركة بالمتحرك حقيقة وبالذات كالفلك التي تجري في البحر، والثاني: أن لا يكون المتحرك موصوفا بالحركة حقيقة وبالذات بل يكون قيام الحركة بجسم آخر بالذات وله اتصال به ومجاورة، وبذلك الاتصال يستند حركته إليه ثانيا وبالعرض كجالس الفلك؛ فإنه ساكن حقيقة وبالذات وله اتصال بالفلك وبه يستند حركة الفلك إليه.
Motion: motion is of two types. One: that the description of motion belongs to the moving object truly and essentially, like a ship that sails in the sea. The second: that the moving object is not described by motion truly and essentially, but rather motion belongs to another body essentially, and it has connection and adjacency to it, and by that connection its motion is attributed to it secondarily and accidentally, like a passenger on a ship; for he is at rest truly and essentially, but he has connection to the ship, and by it the motion of the ship is attributed to him.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents a distinction in how motion is ascribed to an object. It states that motion is of two kinds: first, the object itself truly and essentially moves, like a ship sailing on the sea. Second, an object that is not truly moving but is attributed with motion because it is attached to something that moves, like a passenger on a ship. The passenger is essentially stationary, but because of his connection to the moving ship, we say he moves accidentally. This distinction is important in physics to identify the real subject of motion and to avoid confusion when analyzing causes.

Three Types of Essential Motion: Natural, Forced, Voluntary
Author divides essential motion into three types based on the motivating force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between natural, forced, and voluntary motion based on their cause, and identify forced motion in examples.

Core Idea
Motion's type reveals its cause: nature, compulsion, or will.

This lesson examines a foundational classification in Islamic physics: essential motion (al-ḥaraka adh-dhātiyya) is divided into three types based on the motive force. The author explains that motion requires an efficient cause, and that cause either involves an external element (forced), is by will (voluntary), or by nature (natural). Understanding this division sharpens your ability to analyse causal relationships in natural philosophy and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of essential motion
Read the first sentence: 'الحركة الذاتية إما طبعية أو قسرية أو إرادية'. Note the three categories: natural (ṭabʿiyya), forced (qasriyya), and voluntary (irādiyya).
2
Define the motive force (al-quwwa al-muḥarrika)
The author defines force as 'ما هو مبدأ التغير في آخر' – the principle of change in another. This is the cause of motion. Recognise that motion requires an efficient cause.
3
Analyse the example of forced motion – a stone thrown upward
The stone's motion is caused by its nature (which would normally make it fall) but under compulsion (مقسورة) from the thrower. The thrower is not the direct cause; evidence: motion continues after thrower's death.
Compare with water heated by fire – heat persists after fire is extinguished.
4
Distinguish voluntary and natural motion
Voluntary: motion by will (e.g., Zayd moving by choice). Natural: motion by nature without external compulsion (e.g., stone falling downward). Notice that voluntary motion involves intention.
5
Apply the evidence to confirm indirect causation in forced motion
If the external agent were the direct cause, the effect would cease immediately when the agent ceases. The continuity of motion after the agent's removal proves the cause is the body's nature under compulsion.
This reasoning is used in kalām to discuss God's ongoing creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول أيضا الحركة الذاتية إما طبعية أو قسرية أو إرادية؛ لأن القوة المحركة إما أن تكون. القوة: القوة في عرفهم تطلق على ما هو مبدأ التغير في آخر أي ما يكون مؤثرا في الغير، والحركة لا بد لها من فاعل مؤثر فيها، وهو إما أن يؤثر في حركة الجسم بشركة أمر خارج بأن يكون ذلك الخارج معدا له يعني أن فاعل الحركة يكون صورته النوعية لكن لا بالذات، بل بقسر أمر خارج عن الجسم المتحرك كالحجر المرمي إلى فوق؛ فإن فاعل حركته هو طبيعة لكن لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنها مقسورة للرامي وليس الرامي فاعل حركته وإلا لزم من انعدامه انعدام الحركة؛ ضرورة أن عدم العلة يستلزم عدم المعلول، وربما يهلك الرامي بعد الرمي ويبقى حركة الحجر على حالها ويدل عليه أن الماء المسخن بالنار يبقى حرارته بعد إطفاء النار مدة ولو كانت النار فاعلة الحرارة الماء لزالت حرارة الماء بعد إطفائها، وتسمى هذه الحركة قسرية، أو يؤثر بلا شركة أمر خارج، وهو على نوعين، أحدهما: أن يؤثر بإرادة، وهي الحركة الإرادية كحركة زيد بالاختيار والثاني: أن يؤثر لطبعه، وهي الطبعية كهبوط الحجر إلى أسفل.
And we also say: essential motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. Because the motive force is either... Force: force in their terminology is applied to that which is the principle of change in another, i.e., that which affects another. Motion necessarily has an efficient cause that influences it, and that cause either affects the motion of the body with the participation of an external thing, meaning that external thing is a preparer for it; that is, the efficient cause of motion is the specific form but not essentially, rather by compulsion of an external thing external to the moving body, like a stone thrown upward; for the efficient cause of its motion is nature, but not absolutely, rather insofar as it is compelled by the thrower. And the thrower is not the efficient cause of its motion, otherwise if he ceased the motion would cease, necessarily because the absence of the cause entails the absence of the effect. Often the thrower may die after throwing and the stone's motion continues. Evidence is that water heated by fire retains its heat for a time after the fire is extinguished; if fire were the efficient cause of the water's heat, the water's heat would cease after its extinguishing. This motion is called forced. Or it affects without the participation of an external thing, and that is of two types: one: that it affects by will, and that is voluntary motion like the movement of Zayd by choice; the second: that it affects by its nature, and that is natural motion like the falling of a stone downward.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of essential motion based on its cause. It begins by stating that essential motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. The writer then defines 'force' as the principle of change in another. Since motion requires an efficient cause, that cause can either involve an external thing (making the motion forced) or not. In forced motion, the body's own nature is the immediate cause but under compulsion (e.g., a stone thrown upward – its nature would normally cause downward motion, but it is forced upward by the thrower). The thrower is not the direct cause; evidence is that the motion continues after the thrower's death, similar to water retaining heat after the fire is extinguished. If the external were the direct cause, the effect would cease immediately. The second type is without external participation: either by will (voluntary, like a person's deliberate action) or by nature (natural, like a stone falling down). This division is fundamental in physics to understand the origin of motion.

Key Terms
الحركة الذاتية
الحركة الطبعية
الحركة القسرية
الحركة الإرادية
القوة المحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the stone's nature is to fall, and the thrower only provides initial impetus
B) Because the motion continues after the thrower dies, and if the thrower were the cause, the motion would cease
C) Because the thrower is not a physical agent
D) Because forced motion does not require a cause

Key Takeaway
Essential motion is divided into natural, forced, and voluntary based on whether the motive force is the body's own nature, an external compulsion acting through that nature, or will.

Types of Intrinsic Motion and the Definition of Force
Author defines intrinsic motion, classifies it, and explains forced motion with a projectile example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three types of intrinsic motion (natural, forced, voluntary) and the argument that forced motion does not have the external mover as its true agent.

Core Idea
Motion is classified by its cause: nature, will, or coercion, and forced motion proves the true agent is the body's coerced nature, not the external mover.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of intrinsic motion as presented in the Hidayat al-Hikma. The author defines motion that originates from the body itself and divides it based on the source of its motive force. We will also analyse the key argument used to show that in forced motion, such as a stone thrown upward, the agent is not the thrower but the stone's nature under coercion. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the foundations of Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of intrinsic motion
From the Arabic, find the categories: natural (طبعية), forced (قسرية), voluntary (إرادية). Note that the classification is based on the motive force.
2
Understand the definition of force (قوة)
The author defines force as the principle of change in another. This is the key concept for explaining motion.
3
Analyse the forced motion argument
Read the stone example. The author argues: if the thrower were the agent, the stone's motion would cease when the thrower ceases; but it continues; therefore the thrower is not the agent. Identify this as a conditional argument (modus tollens).
4
Apply the classification to examples
Classify given motions (e.g., stone falling: natural; Zayd walking: voluntary; water heated by fire: forced) and justify using the passage's criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن تكون للجسم حركة على الاستدارة، فإن أجزائه يباين أجزاء مكانه ويلازم كله مكانه، فقد اختلف نسبة أجزاء إلى أجزائه مكانه على التدريج. ونقول أيضا الحركة الذاتية إما طبعية أو قسرية أو إرادية؛ لأن القوة المحركة إما أن تكون..... قلنا: قد يغاير حكم المجموع حكم كل واحد كالرغيف الواحد يشبع كل واحد من الناس، ولا يشبع جميع الناس. القوة: القوة في عرفهم تطلق على ما هو مبدأ التغير في آخر أي ما يكون مؤثرا في الغير، والحركة لا بد لها من فاعل مؤثر فيها، وهو إما أن يؤثر في حركة الجسم بشركة أمر خارج بأن يكون ذلك الخارج معدأ له يعني أن فاعل الحركة يكون صورته النوعية لكن لا بالذات، بل بقسر أمر خارج عن الجسم المتحرك كالحجر المرمي إلى فوق؛ فإن فاعل حركته هو طبيعة لكن لا مطلقا، بل من حيث إنها مقسورة للرامي وليس الرامي فاعل حركته وإلا لزم من انعدامه انعدام الحركة؛ ضرورة أن عدم العلة يستلزم عدم المعلول، وربما يهلك الرامي بعد الرمي ويبقى حركة الحجر على حالها ويدل عليه أن الماء المسخن بالنار يبقى حرارته بعد إطفاء النار مدة ولو كانت النار فاعلة الحرارة الماء لزالت حرارة الماء بعد إطفائها، وتسمى هذه الحركة قسرية، أو يؤثر بلا شركة أمر خارج، وهو على نوعين، أحدهما: أن يؤثر بإرادة، وهي الحركة الإرادية كحركة زيد بالاختيار والثاني: أن يؤثر لطبعه، وهي الطبعية كهبوط الحجر إلى أسفل. مستفادة من خارج أو لا تكون فإن لم تكن مستفادة من خارج، فإما أن يكون لها شعور أو لا يكون، فإن كان لها شعور فهي الحركة الإرادية، وإن لم يكن لها شعور فهي الحركة الطبعية، وإن كانت مستفادة من خارج فهي الحركة القسرية.
That a body have circular motion: its parts differ from the parts of its place while the whole stays in its place, so the relation of parts to the parts of its place changes gradually. And we also say: intrinsic motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary; because the motive force is either... We said: the ruling of the whole may differ from the ruling of each individual, like a single loaf that satisfies each person but does not satisfy all people. Force: force in their terminology is applied to what is the principle of change in another, i.e., what is effective in something else. Motion must have an agent that affects it. That agent either affects the motion of the body with the participation of an external matter, meaning that that external is a preparer for it, meaning that the agent of motion is its specific form but not essentially, rather by coercion from something external to the moving body, like a stone thrown upward; for the agent of its motion is nature, but not absolutely, but insofar as it is coerced by the thrower. The thrower is not the agent of its motion, otherwise the cessation of the thrower would necessitate the cessation of the motion; necessarily, the absence of the cause entails the absence of the effect. Perhaps the thrower dies after throwing and the stone's motion remains as it is. Evidence for this is that water heated by fire retains its heat for a time after the fire is extinguished; if the fire were the agent of the water's heat, the water's heat would vanish after its extinguishment. This motion is called forced. Or he affects without the participation of an external matter, and this is of two types: one that affects by will, which is voluntary motion like Zayd's motion by choice; the second that affects by its nature, which is natural motion like the falling of a stone downward. [The force is either] derived from outside or not; if not derived from outside, then either it has consciousness or not; if it has consciousness, it is voluntary motion; if it has no consciousness, it is natural motion; if derived from outside, it is forced motion.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory section on motion and force. It begins with a description of circular motion: a body in circular motion has parts that change place but the whole remains in the same location, so the relation of parts to place changes gradually. Then the author introduces the classification of intrinsic motion (الحركة الذاتية) into three types: natural (طبعية), forced (قسرية), and voluntary (إرادية), because the motive force is either... (the text is cut). Then there is a comment (قلنا) that the ruling of a whole may differ from the ruling of its parts, illustrated by a loaf that satisfies each individual but not the whole group. This may be a response to an objection about the previous classification. Next, the author defines force (القوة) as the principle of change in another. Motion requires an agent. If the agent acts with an external participant, that external is a preparer, not the actual agent; e.g., a stone thrown upward: the agent of its motion is its nature but under coercion, not the thrower. The author proves this by a reductio: if the thrower were the agent, then the stone's motion would cease when the thrower ceases, but the stone continues moving after the thrower dies. Analogously, water heated by fire retains heat after the fire is out. This motion is called forced (قسرية). If the agent acts without external participation, it is either voluntary (by will and consciousness) or natural (by nature, without consciousness). Examples: voluntary motion of Zayd walking, natural motion of a stone falling. The passage concludes by summarizing: if the motive force is derived externally, it is forced; if not external, then either with consciousness (voluntary) or without (natural). All examples are from the Islamic sciences (e.g., Zayd, stone, water heated by fire).

Key Terms

قسرية
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic motion is classified into natural, forced, and voluntary based on the source of its motive force; forced motion shows that the true agent is the body's nature under coercion, not the external mover.

Time as the Measure of Motion: Definition and Proof
Author defines time and proves it via first-figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of time and reconstruct the first-figure categorical syllogism that proves time is the measure of motion.

Core Idea
Time is not a container but the non-permanent measure of motion itself.

In this passage, the author defines time as a graduated non-permanent capacity that measures motion. He then proves this definition using a categorical syllogism. Understanding this definition requires careful attention to the genus (quantity) and differentia (measure of a non-permanent state). The syllogism demonstrates why time cannot be a measure of a permanent state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Read the passage and locate the definiendum. The text begins with 'فصل في الزمان' (Chapter on Time) and later says 'فهذا الإمكان قابل للزيادة والنقصان وغير ثابت... وهو المعني من الزمان'. So 'زمان' (time) is the term being defined.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The author states 'لأنه كم' (because it is a quantity). So the immediate genus of time is 'كَمّ' (quantity). This places time in the category of continuous quantities.
In Aristotelian categories, quantity is one of the ten categories.
3
Identify the differentia (fasl)
The author argues that time must be a measure of either a permanent or non-permanent state. Since time itself is non-permanent, it measures a non-permanent state. He then states 'وكل هيئة غير قارة فهي الحركة' (every non-permanent state is motion). Thus the differentia is 'مقدار الحركة' (measure of motion), i.e., measuring a non-permanent state.
The differentia distinguishes time from other quantities.
4
Reconstruct the categorical syllogism
The proof is a first-figure syllogism: Major premise: 'Every non-permanent thing is a measure of a non-permanent state.' Minor premise: 'Time is non-permanent.' Conclusion: 'Therefore time is a measure of a non-permanent state.' Then a second premise: 'Every non-permanent state is motion.' Thus: 'Time is the measure of motion.'
This is a compound argument, but the core syllogism is first figure.
5
Verify the figure and mood
The first syllogism is in the first figure (middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor). Mood is Barbara (all A are B, all C are A, so all C are B). The second step is also Barbara. The entire proof is valid.
First-figure syllogisms are naturally valid and require no conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الزمان وتركها إمكان يسع قطع مسافة معينة بسرعة معينة، وأقل منها ببطء معين فهذا الإمكان قابل للزيادة والنقصان وغير ثابت؛ إذ لا يوجد أجزاؤه معا، فههنا إمكان متقدر غير ثابت وهو المعني من الزمان وهو مقدار الحركة؛ لأنه كم، ولا يخلو إما أن يكون مقدارا لهيئة قارة أو لهيئة غير قارة، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ لأن الزمان غير قار، وما لا يكون قارا لا يكون مقدارا لهيئة قارة، فهو مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وكل هيئة غير قارة فهي الحركة، فالزمان مقدار الحركة، وهو المطلوب.
Chapter on Time: And its leaving (of motion) is a capacity that can cover a specific distance with a specific speed, and less than that with a specific slowness. This capacity is subject to increase and decrease and is not permanent, since its parts do not exist together. So here is a graduated non-permanent capacity, and that is what is meant by time. And it is the measure of motion, because it is a quantity. And it must either be a measure of a permanent state or of a non-permanent state. The first is impossible because time is non-permanent, and whatever is non-permanent cannot be a measure of a permanent state. So it is a measure of a non-permanent state. And every non-permanent state is motion. Therefore time is the measure of motion, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines time as the non-permanent measure of motion. The author begins by describing a 'capacity' (imkan) that can accommodate different speeds over a distance—this capacity varies and is not fixed because its parts are not simultaneous. This non-permanent, graduated capacity is identified as time. Then an argument is given: time is a quantity (kamm) and must be a measure of either a permanent or non-permanent state. Since time is non-permanent, it cannot measure a permanent state; thus it measures a non-permanent state, which is motion. This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure: (Major premise) Every non-permanent thing is a measure of a non-permanent state. (Minor premise) Time is non-permanent. (Conclusion) Therefore time is a measure of a non-permanent state. Then the non-permanent state is motion, so time is the measure of motion. The example from fiqh: just as intoxication is the 'illa for prohibition, time is the measure for motion.

Defining Direction (Jiha) and Its Types
Commentator defines jiha and distinguishes six directions, real vs. relative fawq/taht.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jiha according to the commentator, distinguish its three applications, and differentiate absolute from relative directions.

Core Idea
Direction is the extremity of an imaginary line — some directions are relative, but above and below are absolute.

This lesson unpacks the commentator's definition of jiha (direction) from the matn. You will learn the formal definition, how it applies to indication, motion, and bodies, and why above and below are absolute while the other four directions are relative. This distinction is essential for later proofs about the finitude of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its formal definition
Find the definition of jiha in the Arabic: 'الجهة عبارة عن طرف الامتداد الموهوم الآخذ من المشير المنتهى إلى المشار إليه'. Break down: extremity (طرف), imaginary extension (امتداد موهوم), pointer (مشير), pointed-to (مشار إليه).
2
Distinguish the three contexts of jiha
The commentator lists three additions: to indication (جهة الإشارة), to motion (جهة الحركة), and to bodies/dimensions (جهة الأجسام وسائر الأبعاد). Note how the meaning shifts in each case.
3
Map the six directions from the three dimensions
A body has three dimensions (length, width, depth); each has two extremities, giving six directions. Identify the six names: above, below, right, left, front, back. Understand the human posture mapping.
4
Distinguish relative from absolute directions
The four directions (front, back, right, left) change when the observer turns. Above and below do not change when inverted. The real sense: above is the outermost sphere, below is the earth's center.
The author confirms that the intended above and below are the absolute, non-changeable ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهتين: الجهة عبارة عن طرف الامتداد الموهوم الآخذ من المشير المنتهى إلى المشار إليه، وهي قد تضاف إلى الإشارة، فيقال: جهة الإشارة، ويراد بها منتهى الإشارة، وقد تضاف إلى الحركة فيقال: جهة الحركة ويراد بها ما منه الحركة أو ما إليه الحركة، وقد تضاف إلى الأجسام وسائر الأبعاد من الخط والسطح، فيراد بها نهاية الجسم أو البعد والجسم؛ إذ هو ذو أبعاد ثلاثة، ولكل بعد طرفان فله جهات ست، فطرفا الامتداد الطولي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار طول قامته حين هو قائم فوقا وتحتا، فالفوق ما يلي رأسه بالطبع، والتحت ما يلي قدمه بالطبع، وطرفا الامتداد العرضي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار عرض قامته باليمين والشمال وطرفا الامتداد العمقي يسميهما الإنسان باعتبار عرض قامته بالقدام والخلف وقد تطلق الجهة على ما يلي النهاية، فالذي يلي وجهه قداما، وما يلي ظهره يسمى خلفا وما يلي يمينه يمينا، وما يلي شماله شمالا، وهذه الجهات الأربع إضافية تسمى بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، ولذا إذا تحول من وجهه إلى ظهره صار ما كان من قبل خلفا قداما، وما كان قداما صار خلفا، وهكذا في اليمين واليسار، وأما جهتا الفوق والتحت فلا تتبدلان إذا تنكس الإنسان بل يقال للقدم إنها صارت فوقا، وللرأس إنه صار تحتا، والفوق والتحت قد يستعملان بالإضافة إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض فيقال: زيد فوق السرير وتحت السقف، ثم إذا صعد السقف صار السقف تحته وصار هو فوق السقف، وبهذا الاستعمال ما هو فوق بالقياس إلى الجسم تحت بالقياس إلى جسم، وبالعكس، وقد يستعملان بمعناهما الحقيقيين والفوق بهذا المعنى فوق ليس فوقه فوق والتحت هذا المعنى تحت ليس تحته تحت، والمقصود ههنا الحقيقي ولذا قال المصنف لا تتبدلان. ولما كان امتداد العالم متناهيا؛ لما مر من امتناع وجود بعد غير متناه كان لامتداد العالم طرفان فالطرف الأعلى وهو سطح فلك الأفلاك يسمى فوقا، والأسفل وهو مركز الأرض يسمى تحتا.
Direction (jiha): direction is a term for the extremity of the imaginary extension extending from the pointer to the pointed-to. It is sometimes added to indication, and it is said: direction of indication, meaning the endpoint of the indication. And it is sometimes added to motion, and it is said: direction of motion, meaning that from which motion [originates] or that towards which motion [goes]. And it is sometimes added to bodies and the rest of dimensions such as line and surface, meaning the extremity of the body or dimension. Since a body has three dimensions, each dimension has two extremities, so it has six directions. The two extremities of the longitudinal extension are called, by a person with respect to his height when he is standing, 'above' and 'below'. So 'above' is that which is naturally towards his head, and 'below' is that which is naturally towards his foot. The two extremities of the latitudinal extension are called, by a person with respect to his breadth, 'right' and 'left'. And the two extremities of the depth extension are called, by a person with respect to his depth, 'front' and 'back'. And sometimes 'direction' is applied to that which is next to an extremity. So what faces his face is called 'front', what faces his back is called 'back', what faces his right is 'right', what faces his left is 'left'. These four directions are relative, named with respect to humans. Therefore if he turns from his face to his back, what was previously back becomes front, and what was front becomes back, and likewise for right and left. As for the two directions of above and below, they do not change when a person is inverted; rather it is said of the foot that it has become above, and of the head that it has become below. 'Above' and 'below' are sometimes used relatively to some bodies with respect to others, e.g., 'Zayd is above the bed and below the ceiling'. Then if he climbs the ceiling, the ceiling becomes below him and he is above the ceiling. In this usage, what is above relative to one body is below relative to another, and vice versa. And they are sometimes used in their real senses. 'Above' in this sense is that above which there is no above, and 'below' in this sense is that below which there is no below. What is intended here is the real [sense], and therefore the author said: 'they do not change'. And since the extent of the world is finite, as previously established due to the impossibility of an infinite dimension, the extent of the world has two extremities: the highest extremity, which is the surface of the sphere of spheres, is called 'above'; and the lowest, which is the center of the earth, is called 'below'.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the term 'two directions' (jihatayn) from the matn. The commentator defines jiha as the extremity of an imaginary extension from a pointer to a pointed-to object. He explains that the term 'direction' can be used in three ways: (1) with respect to indication, meaning the endpoint of a pointing gesture; (2) with respect to motion, meaning the origin or goal of motion; and (3) with respect to bodies and dimensions, meaning the extremity of a body or dimension. Since a body has three dimensions—length, width, depth—each has two ends, giving six directions. The commentator then explains how humans assign names to these directions based on posture: longitudinal ends become above and below (head and foot), latitudinal ends become right and left, and depth ends become front and back. These six include four relative directions (front, back, right, left) that change when the human body turns, and two absolute directions (above, below) that do not change even when the body is inverted, because their reference is not the human body but the natural extremities of the world: the outermost sphere (fawq) and the center (taht). The commentator distinguishes between relative usage (e.g., 'Zayd is above the bed') and real, absolute usage, emphasizing that the proof in the matn concerns the real, non-changeable directions. Finally, he connects the absolute directions to the finite extent of the universe: since the world has finite dimensions, its two termini are the surface of the outermost sphere (above) and the center of the earth (below).

فوق
تحت

D) The directions of right and left are absolute because they are defined by the body.

Key Takeaway
The commentator defines jiha systematically, distinguishing its three applications and emphasizing that above and below are absolute directions tied to the finite cosmos.
