The Four Elements: Definitions and Primary Qualities
Defines four elements and their primary qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four elemental simple bodies and their associated primary qualities (hot, cold, moist, dry).

Core Idea
The four elements are defined by pairs of primary qualities that determine their nature and behavior.

In this passage, the author defines the four elemental simple bodies—water, earth, fire, and air—and assigns to each a pair of primary qualities. These qualities are not arbitrary; they explain why each element behaves the way it does. Understanding these definitions is foundational for Islamic natural philosophy, medicine, and cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four elements
Locate the names of the four elemental simple bodies in the Arabic text: water (الماء), earth (الأرض), fire (النار), and air (الهواء).
These are the fundamental simple bodies from which all composites are made.
2
Extract the primary qualities for each element
For each element, note the pair of primary qualities: water is cold and moist, earth is cold and dry, fire is hot and dry, air is hot and moist.
The qualities are standard: heat/cold and moisture/dryness.
3
Define the qualities of moisture and dryness
Read the definitions: moisture (الرطوبة) is the quality that makes a body readily accept different shapes; dryness (اليبوسة) is the opposite, making it difficult to shape.
These definitions are given explicitly in the passage.
4
Understand the definition of a simple body
The passage defines a simple body (البسيط) as one that is not composed of bodies of different realities. This is the genus for the elements.
This definition distinguishes elements from compound bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في العنصريات فصل في البسائط العنصرية الماء والأرض والنار والهواء كل واحد منها يخالف الآخر في صورته في العنصريات أي في بيان الأحوال المختلفة بالعناصر وهي جمع عنصر، وهو في اللغة الأصل ولما كانت البسائط العنصرية أصولا للمركبات سميت هذا الاسم، والبسيط هو الجسم الذي لا يكون مركبا من أجسام مختلفة الحقائق، والبسائط العنصرية أربعة بالاستقراء -۱ الماء هو بارد رطب -۲ والأرض وهي باردة يابسة، والنار هي حارة يابسة ٤ والهواء، وهو حار رطب ومعنى البرودة والحرارة ظاهر لا يحتاج إلى البيان أما الرطوبة فهي كيفية في الجسم بما يقبل الأشكال المختلفة بسهولة، وأما اليبوسة فهي كيفية في الجسم بما يعسر قبول أشكال مختلفة. كل واحد منها : أشار بهذا الكلام إلى أن الاختلاف بينهما نوعي لا صنفي ولا شخصي؛ لاختلاف صورها النوعية، وإن لم تكن صورها النوعية مختلفة لكان كل واحد منها شاغلا لمكان الآخر بالطبع، والتالي باطل؛ ضرورة أن النار والهواء طالبان بالطبع لجهة الفوق هاربان بالطبع عن جهة التحت والماء والأرض طالبان للسفل هاربان عن العلو، ولما كان حيز كل واحد منهما مختلفا بالطبع علم أن المقتضي للحيز أيضا مختلف المقتضي لحيز الصورة النوعية فالصورة النوعية مختلفة
The third art on elementals. A section on elemental simple bodies. Water, earth, fire, and air—each one of them differs from the other in its form. 'On elementals' means in explaining the various states of the elements, which is the plural of 'element', and in language it is the origin. Since elemental simple bodies are origins for compounds, they are called by this name. The simple body is the body that is not composed of bodies with different realities. The elemental simple bodies are four by induction: 1- water is cold and moist, 2- earth is cold and dry, fire is hot and dry, 4- air is hot and moist. The meaning of coldness and heat is apparent and needs no explanation. As for moisture, it is a quality in the body whereby it readily accepts different shapes. As for dryness, it is a quality in the body whereby it is difficult to accept different shapes. Each one of them: he indicated by this statement that the difference between them is specific, not generic or individual, because of the difference of their specific forms. If their specific forms were not different, each one would naturally occupy the place of the other, and the consequent is false necessarily: fire and air naturally seek the direction above and flee from below, while water and earth seek the lower and flee from above. Since the place (ḥayyiz) of each is naturally different, it is known that the determinant of the place is also different, and the determinant of the place is the specific form, so the specific forms are different.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is definitional, combined with a proof of specific difference. The author first defines the four elements (water, earth, fire, air) as the simple bodies that are the principles of compounds. He then enumerates their primary qualities: each element has two of the four primary qualities (hot, cold, moist, dry) as per the classical system. Water is cold and moist, earth cold and dry, fire hot and dry, air hot and moist. He defines moisture as the quality that makes a body easily shapeable, and dryness as the opposite. After these definitions, he argues that the elements differ specifically (نوعي) not merely generically or individually. The proof is by way of different natural places: fire and air naturally move upward, water and earth downward. If the elements had the same specific form, they would naturally occupy the same place, but they do not—thus their specific forms must be different. This is a classical demonstration in Aristotelian physics, using the natural motion of elements to establish their distinct essences.

الصورة النوعية
الرطوبة

Proving Specific Difference of the Elements
Proves specific difference of elements by natural place argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that the four elements differ specifically (نوعي) and not merely generically or individually.

Core Idea
The natural direction of motion reveals that each element has a distinct specific form.

After defining the elements and their qualities, the author proves that each element has a different specific form. He argues by considering their natural places: fire and air rise, while water and earth fall. If they shared the same specific form, they would naturally occupy the same place, but they do not. This proof is a classic example of using observable behavior to infer essential differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proven
The author states that the difference between the elements is specific (نوعي), not generic or individual. This is the conclusion to be established.
Specific difference means distinct species, not just different individuals of the same species.
2
Find the conditional premise
The author says: 'If their specific forms were not different, each one would naturally occupy the place of the other.' This is a conditional statement: if not different forms, then same natural place.
This is an implicit 'if-then' structure.
3
Observe the evidence against the consequent
The author points out that fire and air naturally move upward, while water and earth move downward. Thus each has a distinct natural place, not the same.
This is empirical evidence from natural motion.
4
Apply modus tollens
Since the consequent (same natural place) is false, the antecedent (forms not different) must also be false. Therefore the forms are different.
This is a valid logical inference: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.
5
State the conclusion
The author concludes that the specific forms are indeed different, which is what he set out to prove.
The proof is complete and follows from the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في العنصريات فصل في البسائط العنصرية الماء والأرض والنار والهواء كل واحد منها يخالف الآخر في صورته في العنصريات أي في بيان الأحوال المختلفة بالعناصر وهي جمع عنصر، وهو في اللغة الأصل ولما كانت البسائط العنصرية أصولا للمركبات سميت هذا الاسم، والبسيط هو الجسم الذي لا يكون مركبا من أجسام مختلفة الحقائق، والبسائط العنصرية أربعة بالاستقراء -۱ الماء هو بارد رطب -۲ والأرض وهي باردة يابسة، والنار هي حارة يابسة ٤ والهواء، وهو حار رطب ومعنى البرودة والحرارة ظاهر لا يحتاج إلى البيان أما الرطوبة فهي كيفية في الجسم بما يقبل الأشكال المختلفة بسهولة، وأما اليبوسة فهي كيفية في الجسم بما يعسر قبول أشكال مختلفة. كل واحد منها : أشار بهذا الكلام إلى أن الاختلاف بينهما نوعي لا صنفي ولا شخصي؛ لاختلاف صورها النوعية، وإن لم تكن صورها النوعية مختلفة لكان كل واحد منها شاغلا لمكان الآخر بالطبع، والتالي باطل؛ ضرورة أن النار والهواء طالبان بالطبع لجهة الفوق هاربان بالطبع عن جهة التحت والماء والأرض طالبان للسفل هاربان عن العلو، ولما كان حيز كل واحد منهما مختلفا بالطبع علم أن المقتضي للحيز أيضا مختلف المقتضي لحيز الصورة النوعية فالصورة النوعية مختلفة
The third art on elementals. A section on elemental simple bodies. Water, earth, fire, and air—each one of them differs from the other in its form. 'On elementals' means in explaining the various states of the elements, which is the plural of 'element', and in language it is the origin. Since elemental simple bodies are origins for compounds, they are called by this name. The simple body is the body that is not composed of bodies with different realities. The elemental simple bodies are four by induction: 1- water is cold and moist, 2- earth is cold and dry, fire is hot and dry, 4- air is hot and moist. The meaning of coldness and heat is apparent and needs no explanation. As for moisture, it is a quality in the body whereby it readily accepts different shapes. As for dryness, it is a quality in the body whereby it is difficult to accept different shapes. Each one of them: he indicated by this statement that the difference between them is specific, not generic or individual, because of the difference of their specific forms. If their specific forms were not different, each one would naturally occupy the place of the other, and the consequent is false necessarily: fire and air naturally seek the direction above and flee from below, while water and earth seek the lower and flee from above. Since the place (ḥayyiz) of each is naturally different, it is known that the determinant of the place is also different, and the determinant of the place is the specific form, so the specific forms are different.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is definitional, combined with a proof of specific difference. The author first defines the four elements (water, earth, fire, air) as the simple bodies that are the principles of compounds. He then enumerates their primary qualities: each element has two of the four primary qualities (hot, cold, moist, dry) as per the classical system. Water is cold and moist, earth cold and dry, fire hot and dry, air hot and moist. He defines moisture as the quality that makes a body easily shapeable, and dryness as the opposite. After these definitions, he argues that the elements differ specifically (نوعي) not merely generically or individually. The proof is by way of different natural places: fire and air naturally move upward, water and earth downward. If the elements had the same specific form, they would naturally occupy the same place, but they do not—thus their specific forms must be different. This is a classical demonstration in Aristotelian physics, using the natural motion of elements to establish their distinct essences.

The Four Elemental Simples and Their Primary Qualities
Author defines the four elements and their primary qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four elemental simples and their primary qualities, and define wetness and dryness in terms of shape acceptance.

Core Idea
The four elements are defined by pairs of primary qualities.

In this passage, the author introduces the four elemental simples that constitute all composite bodies. He defines each by a pair of primary qualities: hot, cold, wet, dry. We will explore these definitions and the meaning of wetness and dryness, which are crucial for understanding how substances interact in natural philosophy and medicine.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four elements
Find the names of the four elements in the Arabic text. They are enumerated by induction.
2

3
Understand the definitions of wetness and dryness
The author defines wetness as the quality that allows easy acceptance of shapes, and dryness as the quality that makes shape acceptance difficult.
These definitions are operational – they describe behaviour rather than substance.
4
Reflect on cold and hot
The author says their meaning is self-evident. Why might that be? They are directly perceptible qualities.

Shapes and Positions of the Elements
Author states that the elements are spherical and arranges them in order from fire to earth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the shape and order of the four elements according to the passage.

Core Idea
Elements are spherical and concentrically ordered from fire to earth.

This passage presents the foundational cosmological model adopted by Islamic philosophers. It asserts that each element is spherical due to its simplicity, and they are arranged in concentric layers with fire at the top touching the lunar sphere, and earth at the centre. Understanding this order is essential for studying natural philosophy and its impact on Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed shape
The passage says all elements are spherical (كريّة) because of their simplicity (بساطتها).
Simplicity here means they are not composed of other elements.
2
List the elements from top to bottom
The order given is: fire (النار), air (الهواء), water (الماء), earth (الأرض).
Fire is highest, earth is lowest.
3
Note special positions
Fire's upper surface touches the concave of the lunar sphere (فلك الدنيا). Earth's centre coincides with the centre of the world, and it is stationary.
This arrangement explains natural motions: fire rises, earth falls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: إن العناصر كرية الأشكال لبساطتها، والنار فوق العناصر الثلاثة بحيث يماس سطحها الأعلى بمقعر فلك الدنيا، ثم الهواء ثم الماء ثم الأرض تحت الكل بحيث ينطبق مركزها على مركز العالم وهي ساكنة.
They said: The elements have spherical shapes because of their simplicity. Fire is above the three elements such that its upper surface touches the concave of the nearest celestial sphere, then air, then water, then earth below all such that its centre coincides with the centre of the world, and it is stationary.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage describes the physical arrangement of the four elements. The author reports the view that the elements are spherical because of their simplicity (basāṭa). They are arranged in concentric spheres: fire at the top, touching the lunar sphere; then air; then water; and finally earth at the centre, being stationary. This cosmological model is foundational in Aristotelian natural philosophy, which was adopted by many Islamic philosophers.

Generation and Corruption of Elements: Empirical Proofs and Systematic Enumeration
Author demonstrates that elements are subject to generation and corruption by giving examples and listing all possible transformations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify empirical evidence for element transformation and enumerate all possible direct and indirect transformations among the four elements.

Core Idea
Elements transform into each other: empirical examples prove generation and corruption.

The author demonstrates that each of the four classical elements—earth, water, air, fire—is subject to generation and corruption. Through observable examples, he shows transformations like water turning to stone and air condensing into water. He then systematically lists all twelve possible inter-element transformations, dividing them into direct (adjacent) and indirect (requiring an intermediate). This lesson will guide you through the evidence and the enumeration so you can apply this logical structure in Islamic natural philosophy and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence that states each element is subject to generation and corruption. The phrase 'قابل للكون والفساد' is key.
2
Examine the empirical examples
List the four transformations given: water to stone, stone to water, air to water (mountain tops), water to air (evaporation), air to fire (bellows), fire to air (lamp). Understand how each demonstrates change.
These examples are drawn from everyday observation.
3
Analyse the author's reasoning
The examples are presented as evidence that generation and corruption occur. The author references a detailed work (al-Hadiyya al-Saʿīdiyya) for further aspects.
The reasoning is empirical: observed change implies the capacity for generation and corruption.
4
Understand the systematic enumeration
The author calculates 12 possible transformations (4×3). Six are direct between adjacent elements in the order earth-water-air-fire. Six are indirect, requiring an intermediary. Identify the pattern.
Direct: earth↔water, water↔air, air↔fire. Indirect: e.g., earth→air via water or fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل واحد منها قابل للكون والفساد؛ لأن الماء ينقلب حجرا والحجر ينحل ماء، وكذا الهواء ينقلب ماء كما يرى في قلل الجبال، فإنه يغلظ الهواء ويصير ماء ويتقاطر دفعة والماء أيضا ينقلب هواء بالتبخير، وكذا الهواء ينقلب نارا كما في كور الحدادين، والنار أيضا ينقلب هواء كما يشاهد في المصباح. بوجوه شتى أفضل المحققين في "الهدية السعيدية"، من شاء التفصيل فليرجع إليها، الأرض معمورة، وثلاثة الأرباع مغمورة، والكيفية في الهيئة مذكورة. وربع لأن الماء: هذا دليل لانقلاب العناصر بعضها إلى بعض والصور المحتملة للانقلاب اثنا عشرة بضرب كل من الأربعة بالثلاثة الباقية، ستة بلا واسطة، وهي انقلاب العنصرين المتجاورين كانقلاب الماء أرضا وبالعكس، والماء هواء وبالعكس، والهواء نارا وبالعكس، وستة بواسطة كانقلاب الأرض إلى الهواء والنار، وكانقلاب النار إلى الماء والأرض، وكانقلاب الماء إلى النار، وكانقلاب الهواء إلى الأرض.
And each one of them is susceptible to generation and corruption, because water turns into stone and stone dissolves into water, and likewise air turns into water as seen on mountain tops: the air thickens and becomes water and trickles down at once; and water also turns into air by evaporation, and likewise air turns into fire as in blacksmiths' bellows, and fire also turns into air as observed in a lamp. By various aspects—the best of investigators in 'al-Hadiyya al-Saʿīdiyya'; whoever wants detail should refer to it. The earth is inhabited, three-quarters are submerged, and the quality in the configuration is mentioned. And a quarter because water: this is a proof for the transformation of elements into each other. The possible forms of transformation are twelve, by multiplying each of the four by the remaining three: six without intermediary, which are the transformation of adjacent elements, e.g., water to earth and vice versa, water to air and vice versa, air to fire and vice versa; and six with intermediary, e.g., earth to air and fire, fire to water and earth, water to fire, and air to earth.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains that each element can undergo generation and corruption, i.e., change into another element. The author provides empirical examples: water turns into stone (hardening), stone dissolves into water; air condenses into water on mountain tops; water evaporates into air; air ignites into fire in blacksmiths' bellows; fire turns into air (as seen in a lamp). The passage then references a detailed work (al-Hadiyya al-Saʿīdiyya) for further aspects. Finally, the author enumerates all possible transformations: twelve possibilities, calculated as 4 × 3. Six are direct transformations between adjacent elements in the order earth-water-air-fire. Six require an intermediary, e.g., earth to air (via water or fire?). The enumeration is systematic.

Key Terms
الكون والفساد
الانقلاب

Proof of Elemental Interconvertibility
Author proves elemental interconvertibility via empirical examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the author's inductive argument for elemental interconvertibility using empirical examples.

Core Idea
Elements change into each other; therefore they are not absolutely simple.

The author of Hidayat al-Hikma provides observable evidence that the four classical elements (water, earth, air, fire) can transform into one another. This lesson unpacks that evidence and the logical structure of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author states that each element is susceptible to generation and corruption (kawn wa fasad). This is the conclusion to be proved.
2
List the empirical examples
Find the specific transformations mentioned: water to stone, stone to water, air to water, water to air, air to fire, fire to air. Note that each transformation is presented as common observation.
These are not hypothetically but from real-world experience: petrification, condensation, bellows, lamp.
3
Assess the inductive strength
The argument uses multiple instances to support a universal claim. Ask: Are all possible transformations covered? Are there counterexamples? The author's completeness is suggestive but not exhaustive.
This is an incomplete induction (istiqra' naqis), but the number and variety of examples lend probability.
4
Formalize the reasoning
The implicit logic is: If a substance can turn into another, it is subject to generation/corruption. Water turns to stone, stone to water, etc. Therefore, each element can be generated and corrupted.
This is an inductive generalization from observed changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل واحد منها قابل للكون والفساد؛ لأن الماء ينقلب حجرا والحجر ينحل ماء، وكذا الهواء ينقلب ماء كما يرى في قلل الجبال، فإنه يغلظ الهواء ويصير ماء ويتقاطر دفعة والماء أيضا ينقلب هواء بالتبخير، وكذا الهواء ينقلب نارا كما في كور الحدادين، والنار أيضا ينقلب هواء كما يشاهد في المصباح، ونقول أيضا: الكيفيات زائدة على الصورة الطبعية؛ لأنها تستحيل في الكيفيات مثل التسخن والتبرد مع بقاء الصور الطبعية بذواتها، ولو كانت نفس الصور الطبعية لاستحال ذلك.
And each one of them is susceptible to generation and corruption; because water turns into stone and stone dissolves into water, and likewise air turns into water as seen at mountain peaks, for the air thickens and becomes water and drips at once, and water also turns into air by evaporation, and likewise air turns into fire as in blacksmiths' bellows, and fire also turns into air as observed in a lamp. And we also say: the qualities are additional to the natural form; because they change in qualities such as heating and cooling while the natural forms remain in their essences, and if they were the natural forms themselves, that would be impossible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs from the author of Hidayat al-Hikma. First, it proves the interconvertibility of the four elements (water, earth, air, fire) through empirical observations: water becomes stone (petrification), stone becomes water (dissolution), air becomes water (condensation at mountain tops), water becomes air (evaporation), air becomes fire (in bellows), fire becomes air (in a lamp). This shows each element can change into another, implying they share a common substrate and are susceptible to generation and corruption. Second, the passage argues that sensible qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) are accidental additions to the natural form (al-sura al-tab'iyya) of each element. Evidence: the same element can become hot or cold while retaining its specific nature (e.g., water can be heated without losing its wateriness). If the qualities were identical to the form, such variation would be impossible, because the form would change. Thus, qualities are 'extra' (za'ida) and not constitutive of the elemental essence.

Key Terms
كون وفساد
صورة طبعية
كيفية

Key Takeaway
The author uses multiple empirical examples to inductively support the claim that elements are not permanent but can transform into one another.

Qualities are Accidental to the Natural Form
Author argues qualities are accidental to the natural form

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's conditional syllogism proving that sensible qualities are accidental additions to the natural form.

Core Idea
A substance can change in quality while remaining the same kind – so qualities are not the essence.

After establishing elemental change, the author argues that the sensible qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) are not identical to the natural form (the substance's essence). The proof is a simple but powerful conditional syllogism based on observable variation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The author implies: If qualities were the natural form itself, then they could not change while the form remains (because a thing cannot lose its essence). This is the 'if' clause.
2
Find the observed fact
The author notes that qualities do change (e.g., water becomes hot or cold) while the natural form (water-ness) persists. This is the 'not q' part: the consequent is false.
Example: water can be heated without ceasing to be water.
3
Apply modus tollens
If p then q; not q; therefore not p. Here p = 'qualities are the form', q = 'qualities cannot change while form stays'. Since q is false (change occurs), p must be false. So qualities are not the form.
This is a valid deductive argument.
4
State the conclusion
Therefore, the sensible qualities are additional (za'ida) to the natural form. They are accidents, not part of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل واحد منها قابل للكون والفساد؛ لأن الماء ينقلب حجرا والحجر ينحل ماء، وكذا الهواء ينقلب ماء كما يرى في قلل الجبال، فإنه يغلظ الهواء ويصير ماء ويتقاطر دفعة والماء أيضا ينقلب هواء بالتبخير، وكذا الهواء ينقلب نارا كما في كور الحدادين، والنار أيضا ينقلب هواء كما يشاهد في المصباح، ونقول أيضا: الكيفيات زائدة على الصورة الطبعية؛ لأنها تستحيل في الكيفيات مثل التسخن والتبرد مع بقاء الصور الطبعية بذواتها، ولو كانت نفس الصور الطبعية لاستحال ذلك.

And we also say: the qualities are additional to the natural form; because they change in qualities such as heating and cooling while the natural forms remain in their essences, and if they were the natural forms themselves, that would be impossible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs from the author of Hidayat al-Hikma. First, it proves the interconvertibility of the four elements (water, earth, air, fire) through empirical observations: water becomes stone (petrification), stone becomes water (dissolution), air becomes water (condensation at mountain tops), water becomes air (evaporation), air becomes fire (in bellows), fire becomes air (in a lamp). This shows each element can change into another, implying they share a common substrate and are susceptible to generation and corruption. Second, the passage argues that sensible qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry) are accidental additions to the natural form (al-sura al-tab'iyya) of each element. Evidence: the same element can become hot or cold while retaining its specific nature (e.g., water can be heated without losing its wateriness). If the qualities were identical to the form, such variation would be impossible, because the form would change. Thus, qualities are 'extra' (za'ida) and not constitutive of the elemental essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does this reasoning work? Critique it.

The Formation of Temper from Elemental Simples
Author explains how elemental simples combine to produce a uniform temper.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the interaction of elemental simples produces a uniform temper (mizaj) and identify the four simples involved.

Core Idea
Temper is an intermediate quality arising from mutual breaking of extremes.

In this passage, the author defines the process by which a compound acquires a uniform temper from its constituent elemental simples. Understanding this process is fundamental to Islamic natural philosophy and medicine, as it explains how different substances get their characteristic qualities. We will walk through the steps of this interaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
The passage mentions 'basa'it' (elemental simples): earth, water, air, fire. Each has primary qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry). Identify what these are.
Earth is cold and dry; water is cold and wet; air is hot and wet; fire is hot and dry.
2
Understand the interaction
When simples combine, each acts on the other with its power and 'breaks the sharpness' (sawrah) of the opposing quality. This means the intensity is reduced, not eliminated.
The verb 'kasara' suggests a tempering or moderation.
3
Recognize the result
The outcome is an intermediate quality (kayfiyya mutawassita) that lies between the opposing extremes. This quality is uniform (mutashābihah) throughout all parts of the composite.
Uniformity means every part of the compound shares the same temper.
4
Define the term
This intermediate quality is called 'mizaj' (temper). The word derives from the root m-z-j, implying mixing, and here it denotes the resultant balanced state.
Mizaj is not a new simple quality but a relational one from mutual modification.
5
Apply to a real substance
Consider water (cold/wet) and fire (hot/dry). Their mixture produces a warm and moist temper, explaining the nature of something like steam or a compound drug.
This application is used in Islamic pharmacology to predict drug effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبسائط إذا اجتمعت في المركب وفعل بعضها في بعض بقواها، وكسر كل واحد منها سورة كيفية الآخر، فتحصل كيفية متوسطة توسطا ما بين الكيفيات المتضادة متشابهة في أجزائه، وهو المزاج.
And the simples, when they come together in a composite and act upon one another with their powers, and each one of them breaks the sharpness of the quality of the other, then an intermediate quality results, with a certain intermediateness between the opposing qualities, uniform in its parts, and that is the temper (mizaj).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the process by which a compound (murakkab) is formed from the four elemental simples (basa'it). When the simples meet in one locus, each exerts its own power (e.g., heat, cold, wetness, dryness) and 'breaks' the intensity of the opposing quality. The result is a new quality that is intermediate between the extremes, and this quality is uniform throughout all parts of the compound. That intermediate quality is called 'mizaj' (temper). The term mizaj is derived from the verbal noun of mutual interaction (mufa'ala), implying reciprocity. The author emphasizes that the resulting quality is not a single personal quality inherent in all simples simultaneously—rather, each part retains its own quality, but these qualities become alike due to the breaking of extremes, so the compound has a homogeneous temper.

Key Terms
مزاج (mizaj): Temper; the intermediate quality resulting from the interaction of opposing elemental qualities, uniform throughout the composite.
بسائط (basa'it): Elementary simples; the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) that are the basic constituents of all bodies.
كيفية متوسطة (kayfiyya mutawassita): Intermediate quality; the balanced quality that lies between opposing extremes such as hot-cold and wet-dry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The simples separate.

Key Takeaway
Temper (mizaj) is an intermediate quality produced when elemental simples mutually break each other's intensity, resulting in a uniform property throughout the compound.

Classifying Fog, Dew, and Frost by Vapor and Cold
Author defines three atmospheric phenomena from condensed vapor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify fog, dew, and frost based on the quantity of vapor and the effect of cold as defined in the classical text.

Core Idea
Three phenomena from one source: vapor's abundance or sparsity plus cold determines fog, dew, or frost.

This lesson unpacks how classical Islamic natural philosophy defines three common atmospheric phenomena. The text uses a simple binary: vapor is either abundant or sparse, and cold either freezes or does not. Understanding these definitions helps you see how the tradition categorizes natural events by cause and condition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish abundant vs. sparse vapor
The passage first divides vapor by quantity: 'If it is abundant...' and 'If it is little...'. This primary criterion determines the category of phenomenon.
Abundant vapor does not lead to rain clouds as a condition for fog.
2
Determine outcome for abundant vapor
Abundant vapor either becomes rain clouds or fog. Fog is defined as abundant vapor that does not form rain clouds. The marginal note adds that fog is a cloud covering the earth like smoke.
3
Determine outcome for sparse vapor
For sparse vapor, cold is applied. The text states: 'if cold strikes it, if it does not freeze, it is dew; if it freezes, it is frost.' Freezing is the deciding factor.
4
Analyze the note on fog and smoke
The marginal note defines fog as a ground-covering cloud resembling smoke, and smoke as a fine mixture of fire and earth particles so blended that the senses cannot differentiate them. This shows the elemental theory underlying the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كثيرا فقد ينعقد سحابا ماطرا وقد لا ينعقد، ويسمى ضبابا، وإن كان قليلا فإذا ضربه البرد فإن لم ينجمد فهو الطل، وإن انجمد فهو الصقيع.
ضبابا الضبابة سحابة تغشى الأرض كالدخان والجمع ضباب الدخان: نارية تخالطها أجزاء أرضية صغار تلطفت بحيث لا تمايز بينهما في الحس.
If it is abundant, it may form rain-bringing clouds, and may not form, and is called fog. If it is little, then if cold strikes it, if it does not freeze, it is dew; if it freezes, it is frost.
[Note:] Fog: it is a cloud that covers the earth like smoke; its plural is 'dabab'. Smoke: it is a fiery substance mixed with small earthly particles that are so rarefied that no distinction between them is perceptible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines three terms derived from atmospheric water vapor. When vapor is abundant, it either becomes rain clouds or fog. When it is sparse, cooling produces either dew (if unfrozen) or frost (if frozen). The marginal note expands on 'fog' as a ground-covering cloud resembling smoke, and 'smoke' as a mixture of fire and fine earthy particles so blended that the senses cannot differentiate them. These definitions are rooted in the elemental theory of the time, where vapors and smoke arise from moisture and heat.

Key Terms
ضباب (dabāb): A cloud that covers the earth like smoke, formed from abundant vapor that does not form rain clouds.
طل (ṭall): Dew: sparse vapor that is cooled by cold but does not freeze.
صقيع (ṣaqīʿ): Frost: sparse vapor that is cooled by cold and freezes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Fog, dew, and frost are all forms of water vapor distinguished by its quantity and whether cold causes freezing.

Causes of Winds and Simoom
Author enumerates multiple physical causes of winds, including cloud descent, rarefaction, and simoom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the four causes of wind and explain the mechanism of rarefaction as described by the author.

Core Idea
Winds arise from multiple distinct physical processes, not a single cause.

In this passage, the author explores the physical causes of wind within the framework of Islamic natural philosophy. Understanding these causes is not just a matter of physics; it demonstrates how philosophers in the Islamic tradition analyzed natural phenomena through systematic causal reasoning. This lesson will guide you through the causes mentioned and the detailed explanation of rarefaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four causes of wind
Find and list the four causes mentioned: heavy cloud descent, thrust from cloud accumulation, expansion by rarefaction, and cooling of ascending smoke.
The author uses 'قد تكون' to introduce each cause.
2
Analyze the rarefaction mechanism
Read the second paragraph to understand how rarefaction causes wind through a chain of displacement of neighboring air.
Key phrase: 'when it rarefies and increases in volume, it must occupy some of the space of the neighboring air'.
3
Distinguish the causes of simoom
Locate the description of simoom and note its two causes: internal combustion due to rays or passing over extremely hot earth.
The author says 'due to its combusting in itself by rays, or due to its passing over very hot earth.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرياح فقد تكون بسبب أن السحاب إذا ثقل لكثرة البرد اندفع إلى السفل، فصار هواء متحركا، وقد تكون لاندفاع يعرض بسبب تراكم السحب فيصير السحاب من جانب إلى طرف آخر، وقد تكون لانبساط الهواء بالتخلخل في جهة، وقد تكون بسبب برد الدخان المتصعد ونزوله، ومن الرياح ما يكون سموما محرقا؛ لاحتراقه في نفسه بالأشعة، أو لمروره بالأرض الحارة جدا.
لانبساط الهواء بالتخلخل فإن الهواء الكائن في ناحية إذا تخلخل وإزداد مقداره لا بد أن يشغل شيئا من حيز الهواء المجاور، فينتحي هذا الهواء المجاور عن حيزه- ضرورة امتناع التداخل إلى حيز هواء آخر مجاور له وهكذا فيتموج الهواء وتضعف تلك المدافعة شيئا فشيئا إلى غاية ما، فيقف.
As for winds, they may occur because when the cloud becomes heavy due to much cold, it pushes downward, becoming moving air; and they may occur from a thrust that happens due to the accumulation of clouds, so that the cloud moves from one side to another; and they may occur from the expansion of air by rarefaction in one direction; and they may occur due to the cold of ascending smoke and its descent. Among the winds are those that are a burning simoom, due to its combusting in itself by rays, or due to its passing over very hot earth.
[Explanation of rarefaction:] For the air in one direction, when it rarefies and increases in volume, it must occupy some of the space of the neighboring air; this neighboring air then shifts from its space—due to the impossibility of interpenetration—into the space of another neighboring air, and so on; thus the air undulates, and that repulsion weakens gradually until a certain point, then it stops.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents multiple causes of wind. Firstly, heavy cold clouds descend, displacing air. Secondly, accumulated clouds push from one side to another. Thirdly, rarefaction of air in one region causes neighboring air to move in. Fourthly, the cooling and descent of ascending smoke generate wind. Additionally, simoom is a hot, burning wind caused either by internal combustion from rays or by passing over extremely hot terrain. The detailed note on rarefaction explains the mechanical process: when air in a region becomes rarefied, it expands and pushes adjacent air, setting up a chain of displacement that eventually dissipates.

تخلخل

The Rainbow and Halo as Illusions: The Peripatetic View
Author presents the Peripatetic view that the rainbow is an illusion caused by light reflection from moist particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Peripatetic argument that the rainbow is an illusion (khayal) and identify how it depends on the emission theory of vision.

Core Idea
The rainbow is not a physical arch but an optical illusion caused by reflection, akin to a mirror image.

This lesson explores the Peripatetic position on rainbows and halos, introducing the concept of khayal (illusion) in optics. The passage defines illusion and addresses an objection, showing how the emission theory of vision supports the claim. Understanding this helps distinguish between real objects and mere appearances in natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing views on the rainbow
Find where the author states that the Peripatetics (المشاؤون) consider the rainbow an illusion, while others (الآخرون) hold it to be real. Underline the terms خيال and موجودة في الخارج.
2
Define 'khayal' as used in this passage
The passage gives the meaning: seeing the image of a thing together with a polished surface (صيقلي), leading one to falsely suppose the image inheres in it. Compare this to the ordinary usage of 'illusion'.
This is a technical optical definition, not the broader epistemological one.
3
Trace the objection and response
An objector asks: if the image is not actually in the polished surface, how can we see it there? The response relies on the emission theory of vision: the visual ray (شعاع البصر) reflects off the surface to the real object, so we see the object as if it were in the surface.
4
Contrast the two theories of vision
The author notes that the response is only valid for the emission theory. According to the intromission theory (where the image enters the eye), this explanation of khayal fails. Identify which theory the Peripatetic view presupposes.
5
Apply the geometric conditions for rainbow formation
The passage describes when the phenomenon occurs: spray particles in a circular arrangement opposite the sun, a dark background (mountain or cloud), and the sun near the horizon. The small mirrors (droplets) show only light, not distinct shapes. Verify these conditions in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوس قزح فهي إنما تحدث من ارتسام ضوء النير الأكبر في أجزاء ...
قوس قزح: اعلم أن الحكماء اختلفوا في قوس قزح وأمثالها كالهالة: هل هي خيالات أم لا ؟ فذهب المشاؤون إلى أنها خيالات والآخرون إلى أنها موجودة في الخارج، ومعنى الخيال ههنا هو أن يرى صورة الشيء مع صورة شيء صيقلي مظهر له كالمرآة، فيظن أن الصورة حاصلة فيه في نفس الأمر. قيل: إذا لم تكن الصورة حاصلة فيه فكيف يتصور رؤيتها فيه؛ إذ الرؤية لا تتعلق إلا بالحاصل؟ وأجيب عنه بأن الصورة وإن لم تكن حاصلة في شيء صيقلي مظهر، لكن الأشعة الخارجة عن البصر تنعكس منه إلى المرئي فيرى المرئي بعد ذلك ولما كانت رؤيته بعد انعكاس شعاع البصر من شيء صيقلي إليه ظن أن صورته مرتسمة في ذلك الشيء الصيقلي. (علمي) مع أدنى (تغيير هذا على مذهب القائلين بخروج الشعاع من البصر إلى المرئي صحيح، وأما على مذهب القائلين بارتسام الصورة في البصر فلا. ضوء بيانه أنه إذا وجد في خلاف جهة النير الأكبر أجزاء رشية على هيئة الاستدارة بحيث ينعكس الشعاع البصري من تلك الأجزاء إلى ذلك النير، وكان وراءها جسم كثيف إما جبل أو سحاب كدر، وكان النير قريبا من الأفق، ونظرنا إلى تلك الأجزاء وانعكس الشعاع البصري منها إلى النير، رأينا ضوء النير في تلك الأجزاء دون شكله؛ لأن المرأة إذا كانت صغيرة جدا لم يظهر أشكال الجزئيات على التمام؛
As for the rainbow, it occurs from the impression of the light of the greater luminary (the sun) in particles ...
Rainbow: Know that the philosophers disagreed about the rainbow and its likes, such as the halo: whether they are illusions or not. The Peripatetics held that they are illusions, while others held that they exist externally. The meaning of 'illusion' here is that one sees the image of a thing together with the image of a polished thing that manifests it, like a mirror, so one supposes that the image actually inheres in it. It was said: if the image does not inhere in it, how is it possible to see it in it, since sight only pertains to what exists? The answer was given that even though the image does not inhere in a polished manifesting thing, the rays emanating from the eye are reflected from it to the object seen, so the object is seen after that. And since its sight occurs after the reflection of the visual ray from a polished thing to it, one supposes that its image is impressed in that polished thing. (Scientific: with minimal change, this is correct according to the doctrine of those who posit the emission of the ray from the eye to the object. But according to the doctrine of those who posit the impression of the image in the eye, it is not. Clarification: when there are, in the opposite direction of the greater luminary, spray-like particles arranged in a circular form such that the visual ray is reflected from those particles to that luminary, and behind them there is a dense body, either a mountain or a dark cloud, and the luminary is near the horizon, and we look at those particles and the visual ray is reflected from them to the luminary, we see the light of the luminary in those particles but not its shape; because when the mirror is very small, the shapes of the details do not appear completely.)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophical status of the rainbow and halo: are they real objects or illusions? The Peripatetics say they are illusions, meaning that what is seen is not an actual image on the droplets but a reflected image, similar to a mirror. An objection arises: if the image is not actually in the reflective medium, how can we see it there? The answer relies on the emission theory of vision: the ray from the eye reflects off the polished surface (the droplet) to the sun, so the sun is seen as if located in the droplet. The author notes that this explanation works only if one holds that the ray leaves the eye; if one holds that the image enters the eye (intromission theory), it fails. The explanation continues with a geometric condition: when the observer is opposite the sun, with spray particles arranged circularly, and there is a dark background, the reflected light is seen but the shape of the sun is not clearly discerned because the particles act like very small mirrors.

Key Terms
خيال
قوس قزح
هالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it disappears quickly
B) Because the image is not actually in the droplets but results from light reflection
C) Because it only appears after rain
D) Because it has no color

Key Takeaway
The Peripatetic view defines the rainbow as an illusion because the image seen is not actually in the droplets but results from reflection, akin to a mirror image.

Classification of Natural Bodies: Mineral, Plant, Animal
Author defines the perfect composite and divides it by growth and sensation into mineral, plant, animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any natural body as mineral, plant, or animal by applying the dichotomous division based on growth and sensation as taught in this passage.

Core Idea
Growth and sensation are the key differentia dividing the natural world into three categories.

This lesson examines the logical definition and classification of natural bodies. The author first defines the 'perfect composite' — a body with a specific form preserving its composition — then divides it using the presence or absence of growth and sensation. This hierarchical classification is foundational in Islamic natural philosophy, and mastering it will sharpen your ability to parse definitions and apply dichotomous division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'the perfect composite' (al-murakkab al-tamm). Locate it in the Arabic and note its definition: a body having a specific form (sura naw'iyya) that preserves its composition.
This definition excludes simple elements and imperfect mixtures.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'natural body' (implied), and the differentia is 'having a specific form that preserves its composition.' This distinguishes perfect composites from other bodies.
The specific form is the substantial form that gives the thing its identity.
3
Follow the first division
The passage then divides the perfect composite: either it has growth (nama') or it does not. Those without growth are minerals.
Growth is the capacity for increase and development inherent in living things.
4
Follow the second division
The remaining category (with growth) is subdivided: either it has sensation (hiss) and voluntary motion (haraka iradiyya) or not. Those with both are animals; those without are plants.
Sensation and voluntary motion distinguish animals from plants.
5
Map the three categories
The dichotomous division yields three final classes: mineral (no growth), plant (growth but no sensation/voluntary motion), animal (growth and sensation/voluntary motion). Confirm each fits the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المركب التام - وهو الذي له صورة نوعية تحفظ تركيبه – إما أن يكون له نماء أو لا، الثاني المعدن، والأول إما أن يكون له حس وحركة إرادية أو لا، الأول: الحيوان والثاني: النبات.
The perfect composite - which is that which has a specific form preserving its composition - either has growth or not; the second is mineral. The first either has sensation and voluntary motion or not; the first is animal and the second is plant.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage defines a classification of natural bodies. It begins by defining the 'perfect composite' (al-murakkab al-tamm), which is a body that possesses a specific form (sura naw'iyya) that preserves its composition—this distinguishes it from simple elements or compounds without enduring form. This perfect composite is then divided into two categories: those that have growth (nama') and those that do not. Those without growth are minerals (ma'dan). The growth-having category is further subdivided into those that have sensation (hiss) and voluntary motion (haraka iradiyya) and those that do not. The former are animals (hayawan), the latter are plants (nabat). This dichotomous division captures the traditional three kingdoms of nature: mineral, plant, and animal. The defining features are growth (common to plants and animals) and sensation/voluntary motion (unique to animals).

Key Terms
مركب تام (murakkab tamm): A perfect composite: a body that has a specific form (sura naw'iyya) that preserves its composition, as opposed to imperfect mixtures. [Note: In logic, 'murakkab' often means 'compound' in a propositional sense; here it is a physical composite.]
صورة نوعية (sura naw'iyya): Specific form: the substantial form that makes a thing what it is and maintains its structure.
نماء (nama'): Growth: the inherent capacity for increase in size and development, characteristic of living things.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Natural bodies are classified into minerals, plants, and animals based on the presence or absence of growth and sensation/voluntary motion, with growth as the first differentia and sensation as the second.

The Vegetative Soul: First Perfection and Its Three Faculties
Defines vegetative soul and its three faculties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three faculties of the vegetative soul and explain why it is called the first perfection.

Core Idea
The vegetative soul is the first perfection of a natural body—it makes a plant be a plant.

In this passage, the author defines the vegetative soul as the first perfection (kamaāl awwal) of a natural body that is instrumental for generation, growth, and nutrition. He then breaks it down into three faculties: nutritive, growth, and reproductive. Understanding this classification is foundational for later discussions on the animal and rational souls.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term 'nafs nabātiyya' in the Arabic. Notice how the author first describes it as a power without sensation causing plant movements, then formally defines it as first perfection.
The definition is given in two parts: a functional description then a formal definition.
2
Identify the genus and differentia
The definition places the vegetative soul in the genus of 'first perfection' and differentiates it by being instrumental for generation, growth, and nutrition only. So the differentia specifies what kind of first perfection it is.
This is a hadd (real definition) with genus and differentia.
3
Extract the three faculties
List the three faculties: nutritive (ghādhiya), growth (nāmiya), and reproductive (mūlida). Note that the nutritive has four subservient powers: attractive, retentive, digestive, expulsive.
These are not sub-faculties but servants (khawādim) of the nutritive power.
4
Understand 'first perfection'
The text distinguishes first perfection (essential form) from second perfection (accidents). Confirm that the vegetative soul is a first perfection because it completes the essence of a plant—without it, the plant would not be alive.
This distinction is crucial for later lessons on definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله قوة عديمة الشعور تصدر عنها حركات النبات في الأقطار وأفعال مختلفة بآلات مختلفة، وتسمى نفسا نباتية، وهي كمال أول لجسم طبعي آلي من جهة ما يتولد ويزيد ويغتذي فقط، فلها قوة غاذية، وهي القوة التي تحيل جسما آخر إلى مشاكلة الجسم الذي هي فيه، فتلصق به بدل ما تحلل عنه بالحرارة، ولها قوة نامية وهي التي تزيد في الجسم الذي هي فيه زيادة في أقطاره طولا وعرضا وعمقا إلى أن يبلغ كمال النشوء على تناسب طبعي، ولها قوة مولدة، وهي التي تأخذ من الجسم الذي هي فيه جزءا، وتجعله مادة ومبدأ لمثله، والغاذية تجذب الغذاء وتمسكه وتهضمه وتدفع ثفله، فلها خوادم أربع: قوة جاذبة وماسكة وهاضمة ودافعة. كمال الكمال ما يتم به الشيء، إما في حقيقته كالصورة النوعية تتم بها حقيقة النوع، ويسمى كمالا أول، وإما في وصفه كالأعراض يتم بها الشيء في وصفه، ويسمى كمالا ثانيا.
And it has a power devoid of sensation from which proceed the movements of the plant in its directions and various actions through various instruments, and it is called the vegetative soul. It is the first perfection for a natural body that is instrumental with respect to what is generated, grows, and is nourished only. So it has a nutritive power, which is the power that transforms another body into similarity to the body in which it is, and attaches to it in place of what is dissolved from it by heat. And it has a growth power, which is the power that increases the body in which it is, an increase in its dimensions—length, width, depth—until it reaches the perfection of growth in natural proportion. And it has a reproductive power, which takes a part from the body in which it is and makes it matter and principle for its like. The nutritive power attracts food, retains it, digests it, and expels its waste; thus it has four servants: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive powers. Perfection: perfection is that by which a thing is completed; either in its essence, like the specific form by which the reality of the species is completed, and it is called first perfection; or in its attribute, like the accidents by which the thing is completed in its attribute, and it is called second perfection.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the vegetative soul (al-nafs al-nabātiyya). It first defines it as a power without sensation causing plant movements and actions, then defines it as the first perfection (kamaāl awwal) of a natural body that is instrumental (ālī) specifically for generation, growth, and nutrition. This is a standard philosophical definition, where 'first perfection' is the form that makes a thing what it is. The text then enumerates three faculties: nutritive (ghādhiya), growth (nāmiya), and reproductive (mūlida). For the nutritive power, it lists four subservient powers: attractive, retentive, digestive, and expulsive. Finally, it explains the distinction between first perfection (substantial form) and second perfection (accidental properties), using the example of the specific form as first perfection and accidents as second perfection. This clarification ensures the definition of vegetative soul as first perfection is properly understood.

First Perfection vs Second Perfection
Defines first and second perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish first perfection (kamaāl awwal) from second perfection (kamaāl thānī) and apply the distinction to any substance.

Core Idea
First perfection is what makes a thing what it is; second perfection is what perfects its attributes.

After defining the vegetative soul, the author clarifies the concept of 'perfection' (kamaāl), dividing it into first and second. The first perfection is the substantial form that completes the essence (like the soul for a living being). The second perfection is the accidental properties that further complete the thing (like knowledge for a human). This distinction is crucial in Islamic philosophy for understanding how things are defined and classified.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of 'perfection'
Locate the sentence: 'الكمال ما يتم به الشيء' and translate it: 'Perfection is that by which a thing is completed.' Write it down.
This is the general meaning of kamaal.
2
Understand the two types
The text says: 'إما في حقيقته كالصورة النوعية تتم بها حقيقة النوع، ويسمى كمالا أول، وإما في وصفه كالأعراض يتم بها الشيء في وصفه، ويسمى كمالا ثانيا.' Identify the two cases: 1) in the essence (e.g., specific form) = first perfection; 2) in the attribute (e.g., accidents) = second perfection.
First perfection is the form; second perfection is the accidents.
3
Apply to the vegetative soul
Confirm that the vegetative soul is a first perfection because it gives the plant its essence of being alive (vegetatively). Without it, the plant would not be a living thing.
The vegetative soul is a substantial form, not an accident.
4
Give another example from Islamic science
For a human, the rational soul is first perfection; knowledge (acquired) is second perfection. Explain why: the rational soul makes a human a human; knowledge perfects the human in description.
This distinction is used in metaphysical discussions of the soul.

Defining the Animal Soul and Defending Its Definition
Defines animal soul and addresses an objection about its definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the definition of the animal soul and reconstruct the dialectical argument that defends it against an objection.

Core Idea
The animal soul's definition is defended by showing that its instrumental actions are specific to perception and motion, not to vegetative functions.

This passage defines the animal soul (al-nafs al-ḥayawāniyya) and then engages with a potential objection that arises from a clarification about the nutritive and growth faculties. We will trace how the author uses a distinction between different faculties to protect the definition.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Definition
Locate the definition: the animal soul is defined as a first perfection (كمال أول) of a natural instrumental body (جسم طبعي آلي). Note the differentia: insofar as it perceives particular corporeal things and moves voluntarily.
The definition is standard and hierarchical.
2
Analyze the Differentia
Identify the two key aspects: perception of particulars and voluntary motion. These distinguish the animal soul from the vegetative soul (which only has growth and nutrition) and from the rational soul (which deals with universals).
3
Examine the Clarification
The teacher (الشيخ) clarifies the difference between nutritive (الغاذية) and growth (النامية) faculties. Growth requires intake exceeding dissolution, unlike mere replenishment. This nuance sets up the objection.
This clarification is not central to the definition but provokes the dialectical exchange.
4
Reconstruct the Objection
The objection attacks the phrase 'instrumental from these two aspects' (الآلي من هاتين الجهتين). If it means only perception and motion, vegetative actions also come from the animal soul, so it fails to exclude. If it means absolutely, the rational soul also has perception and motion, so it fails to include uniquely.
This is a classic dilemma attack.
5
Examine the Answer
The answer follows what 'some claimed': the animal body contains different faculties for different actions. Mineral form preserves structure, vegetative soul handles growth and nutrition, animal soul perceives material particulars. Thus, the definition stands because the 'instrumental' qualification is specific to the animal soul's proper actions.
The resolution relies on a multi-faculty model of the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الحيوان وهو مختص بالنفس الحيوانية، وهي كمال أول لجسم طبعي الي من جهة ما تدرك الجزئيات الجسمانية، وتتحرك بالإرادة، فلها قوة مدركة ومحركة. أما المدركة فهي إما في الظاهر أو في الباطن، أما التي في الظاهر فهي خمس السمع النامية: الفرق بين الغاذية والنامية كما قال الشيخ: إن الغاذية تورد الغذاء تارة مساويا لما ينحلل كما في سن الوقوف، وتارة أنقص كما في سن الذبول، وتارة أزيد كما في سن النمو، والنمو لا يمكن إلا بأن يكون الوارد وأزيد من المتحلل، إلا أنه ليس كلما كان الوارد زائدا كان نموا، فإن السمن بعد الهزال من هذا القبيل، وليس بنمو . من جهة قيل عليه إن أراد أنها آلي من هاتين الجهتين فقط، فلا يصدق هذا التعريف على النفس الحيوانية؛ لأنها آلية من جهة الأفعال النباتية أيضا؛ لأنها صادرة عنها، وإن أراد أنها آلية من هاتين الجهتين مطلقا فينتقض بالنفس الناطقة؛ لأنها آلية من هاتين أيضا، أجيب عنه أنه سلك ما زعم بعضهم أن بدن الحيوان مشتمل على مختلفة لأفعال مختلفة، فحفظ التركيب للصورة المعدنية، والنمو والاغتذاء للنفس النباتية وإدراك الجزئيات المادية للنفس الحيوانية، حاصله: أنا نختار الشق الأول ولا يلزم عدم الصدق.
Section on the animal. It is specific to the animal soul, which is a first perfection for a natural instrumental body insofar as it perceives particular corporeal things and moves by will. So it has a perceptive power and a motive power. As for the perceptive, it is either external or internal. As for the external, they are five: hearing. The growth: the difference between the nutritive and growth faculties as the teacher said: The nutritive brings food sometimes equal to what is dissolved as in the age of cessation, sometimes less as in the age of decline, and sometimes more as in the age of growth. Growth cannot occur unless the intake is more than what is dissolved, but not every increase in intake is growth, for becoming fat after emaciation is of this kind and is not growth. From one perspective, it was objected: if he meant that it is instrumental from these two aspects only, then this definition does not apply to the animal soul because it is instrumental from the aspect of vegetative actions as well, since they originate from it. If he meant that it is instrumental from these two aspects absolutely, then it is refuted by the rational soul because it is instrumental from these two as well. It was answered that he followed what some claimed: that the animal body includes different parts for different actions, so preservation of composition is for the mineral form, growth and nutrition for the vegetative soul, and perception of material particulars for the animal soul. The summary: we choose the first disjunct and it does not entail lack of applicability.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the animal soul and then engages in a dialectical refinement prompted by an earlier distinction between the nutritive and growth faculties. The author first gives the standard definition of the animal soul: it is a first perfection (كمال أول) of a natural instrumental body, specifically regarding perception of particulars and voluntary motion. Hence it has two main powers: perceptive and motive. The perceptive is then divided into external and internal; the external are five, beginning with hearing. At this point, the commentator inserts a clarification about the difference between the nutritive (الغاذية) and growth (النامية) faculties, explaining that growth requires intake exceeding dissolution, not just any increase. This leads to an objection: the definition of the animal soul as being instrumental from the two aspects (perception and motion) is critiqued. If it means solely those two, then vegetative actions (like nutrition) are also instrumental but come from the animal soul; if it means absolutely, then the rational soul also has perception and motion. The answer resolves this by noting that the animal body contains different faculties for different actions: mineral form preserves structure, vegetative soul handles growth and nutrition, and animal soul perceives material particulars. Thus, the definition stands, and the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
النفس الحيوانية
كمال أول
آلي
الغاذية
النامية

Definitions of the Five External Senses
Defines each of the five external senses with their organs and modes of perception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the definitional structure (genus, differentia, medium) for each of the five external senses as presented in the Hidayat al-Hikma.

Core Idea
The five external senses are powers lodged in specific organs, each perceiving through a unique medium.

This passage from the Hidayat al-Hikma defines the five external senses. It first reminds us that the true perceiver is the rational soul; the senses are merely instruments. Each sense is then defined by its anatomical location and the medium through which it perceives. By analyzing these definitions, we learn how the Islamic philosophical tradition classified sensory perception—a necessary foundation for later discussions on epistemology and the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general framework
Locate the initial remark about the rational soul and the metaphorical use of 'perceiver'. Why does the author insist that the senses are instruments?
This sets the stage: the senses are not independent agents but tools of the soul.
2
Extract the definition pattern for each sense
For each of the five senses, find: (a) the power lodged (قوة مودعة), (b) the specific organ or nerve, (c) the medium of perception. Write them in a table.
Notice that every definition follows the same syntactic structure in Arabic.
3
Compare the three theories of sight
The text mentions three schools for sight. Note how this contrasts with the other senses, for which only one theory is given. What does this tell us about the complexity of vision?
The debate about sight is a classic example of philosophical disagreement within the tradition.
4
Apply the pattern to check definitions
Using the pattern you extracted, verify that all five senses follow the same structure. What would be missing if a definition were incomplete?
Completeness requires specifying both the power's location and the medium.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المدركة أي الآلات للإدراك ؛ لأن المدرك في الحقيقة النفس الناطقة، والقوى الجسمانية آلات لها في إدراك المحسوسات وتسميتها مدركة مجاز. صور السمع: هو قوة مودعة في العصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ الذي فيه هواء محتقن، فإذا وصل الهواء المتكيف بكيفية الصوت لتموجه الحاصل من قرع أو قلع، أدركته القوة المودعة فيه. البصر : هو قوة مودعة في ملتقى عصبتين نابتتين من مقدم الدماغ محوفتين، تتقاربان حتى تتلاقيا وتتقاطعا تقاطعا صليبيا، ثم تتباعدان إلى العينين، ويسمى ذلك الملتقى مجمع النور، وفي الإبصار ثلاثة مذاهب، فبعضهم يزعم أنه بخروج الشعاع من البصر، وبعضهم يقول بانطباع الصورة للمرئي، وبعضهم يحسب أن الهواء الذي بين الرائي والمرئي يتكيف بنور البصر، فيصير آلة للإبصار. الشم : قوة مودعة في زائدتين نابتتين من مقدم الدماغ شبيهتين بحلمي الثدي، تدرك بتوسط الهواء المتكيف بذي الرائحة الذوق : هو قوة مودعة في العصب المفروش على جرم اللسان، تدرك الطعوم بواسطة الرطوبة اللعابية المتكيفة بطعم ذي الطعم. اللمس هو قوة مودعة في العصب المخالطة لأكثر البدن.
The perceptive [powers] are instruments for perception, because the perceiver in reality is the rational soul, and the bodily powers are instruments for it in perceiving sensibles, and they are called perceivers metaphorically. Forms of hearing: it is a power lodged in the nerve spread in the concavity of the ear canal which contains confined air. When the air conditioned with the quality of sound due to its undulation resulting from a strike or a pull reaches it, the power lodged there perceives it. Sight: it is a power lodged at the meeting point of two nerves growing from the front of the brain, hollowed, they approach until they meet and intersect in a crosswise intersection, then they separate to the two eyes, and that meeting point is called the 'gathering of light'. Concerning vision, there are three schools: some claim it is by the emission of a ray from the eye, some say by the impression of the image of the seen object, and some think that the air between the seer and the seen becomes conditioned by the light of the eye, thus becoming an instrument for vision. Smell: a power lodged in two outgrowths growing from the front of the brain resembling the nipples of the breast, it perceives by means of the air conditioned with the odor. Taste: a power lodged in the nerve spread on the substance of the tongue, it perceives tastes by means of the salivary moisture conditioned with the taste of the tasted thing. Touch: a power lodged in the nerves mingled with most of the body.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the five external senses: hearing, sight, smell, taste, and touch. The author begins with a general remark that the perceptive powers are merely instruments; the true perceiver is the rational soul, and calling these powers 'perceivers' is metaphorical. Each sense is then defined by its specific organ and the medium through which it perceives. Hearing is lodged in the ear canal nerve, perceiving sound via air undulation. Sight is at the optic nerve intersection; the author notes three theories of vision: emission of rays, impression of form, or the air becoming an instrument. Smell is in two outgrowths in the nose, perceiving via odor-laden air. Taste is on the tongue nerve, perceiving via saliva. Touch is in nerves spread throughout the body. For each, the definition follows the pattern: a power (قوة) lodged in a specific bodily location, perceiving through a medium.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الشم
الذوق
اللمس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The five external senses are defined by a common pattern: a power lodged in a specific nerve, perceiving through a specific medium—this pattern is the foundation for understanding perception in Islamic philosophy.
